THE TED PAGE
THE NOVELS OF GALSWORTHY
FOR the novelist England has one
advantage over the newer countries ;
by long isolation and inbreeding her
civilisation has crystallised out into
stable types. The English novelist can
always depend upon his stock characters,
such as the working man, the commercial
man, and the country gentleman. These
types once evolved are preserved and fixed
by that caste which is the still feebly wagging
tail-end of England’s feudal system. Each
type is carefully isolated in its own parti-
cular pigeon-hole; in so well-regulated a
society as England a person who has in-
stalled himself in the wrong pigeon-hole
usually feels so uncomfortable that he is
glad to vacate it. With us there are as yet
no pigeon-holes. Society is a heap that is con-
tinually being resifted; in England society is
a series of superimposed fossil strata. Here
all is flux, the flux of life. There all is frozen
solid, fossilised in death. The Australian
national type is merely in process of evolu-
tion; but in England, not only is the
national type easily recognisable, but sub-
sidiary types like the man of property, the
club man, the manufacturer, the yokel, the
army officer, the Oxford man, the ’bus-
driver, the landed proprietor, are already
safe within their little pigeon-holes. So the
English novelist has to his hand a series of
character studies already labelled.
A striking instance of this definiteness of
type lies in the coincidence that two recent
novels by different authors depict what is
practically the same family. The Country
Gentleman by John Galsworthy (Heine-
mann, per Dymock’s Library) is the country
gentleman called by Snaith Broke of Coven-
den. The English county family has be-
come stereotyped : in both novels we are
introduced to the same set of characters.
J*
Galsworthy is a novelist whose future is
worth speculation. He is undeniably clever,
he has much constructive power, and though
lacking the originality and the breadth of
treatment observable in Snaith’s work, he
means business in the literary world. While
Snaith, however, in his latest novel shows a
masterful disdain for formulae, Galsworthy’s
second work is in style but a variant
of his first. The forthcoming books of
these two should enable us to “ place ” one
as the coming English novelist. That one
will probably be Snaith. Yet The Man of
Property (Heinemann, per Dymock’s Lib-,
rary), Galsworthy’s first considerable novel,
is a remarkable achievement. Galsworthy
has in both his books dispensed with the
traditional hero and heroine. He is con-
cerned not with any individual souls ;■ he is
concerned with the soul of a family. In
England, where the cold winters make the
hearth still the symbol of the national life,
the family still survives as the social unit.
In Australia, as in the Latin races, the pro-
duct of less rigorous climes, the influence of
the sun is swiftly breaking down the domi-
nance of the hearth ; there is no longer with
us the centripetal force of a home fire ; the
God of Light is dissipating the family : home
is not the sole place of warmth.
In The Man of Property Galsworthy de-
picts in all its ramifications through three
generations the development of the soul of
the Forsyte family—or rather its resistance
to development, for the type is static. His
characters number at least two dozen, each
palpably a Forsyte, yet each a distinct
characterisation. Nothing much happens
within this family except eating and drink-
ing, and nothing at all happens outside it,
except the nominal hero and heroine. Of
Philip Bosinney, architect, who called on
the family in a grey hat, and of Irene, the
wife of Somes Forsyte,’ who loves him, we
learn little except as their characters are re-
flected through the Forsyte mind. And
even the opinions of the Forsyte family
are almost inarticulate. Of one member
of the family the author remarks that
his mind was a place where very little
took place from morning to night. This
inarticulateness is a mark of the modern
Englishman. It is the first thing that strikes
a visitor to England ; the Englishman is not
morose nor stupid ; he is either unable to say
what he thinks, or he does not think
it worth while saying—or, more probably,
he does not think it worth while think-
ing. Of the expression of emotion in
any articulate form, the Englishman of
modern novels and modern dramas is horribly
afraid. When a mother wishes to express
her love for her son in his trouble, she usually
talks of the weather, and the son grows
restive. He watches her covertly, lest she
should shed tears or kiss him.
In the prosperous, smug Forsyte family,
buttressed by family pride and almost im-
pervious to assult, Galsworthy has, we
suspect, written a parable of England. The
spectacle of a family festival of the For-
sytes, Galsworthy writes, would bring before
the psychological analyst evidence of that
mysterious concrete tenacity which renders
a family so formidable a unit in society, so
clear a reproduction of society in miniature.
Such an observer
Has been admitted to a vision of the dim roads
of social progress, has understood something of
patriarchal life, of the swarmings of savage
hordes, of the rise and fall of nations. He is like
one who, having watched a tree grow from its
planting—a paragon of tenacity, insulation, and
success, amidst the deaths of a hundred other
plants less fibrous, sappy, and persistent—one
day .will see it flourishing with bland, full foliage,
in a,n almost repugnant prosperity, at the summit
of its efflorescence.
And the Forsytes cannot be underestimated
in our conception of England. In Gals-
worthy’s opinion they are half-England. As
one of them, the only one who had “ made
a mistake ” by deserting his wife for another
woman, genially puts it:
They are half-England, and the better half,
too, the safe half, the three per cent, half, the
half that counts. It’s their wealth and security
that makes everything possible ; makes your art
possible, makes literature, science, even religion,
possible. Without Forsytes, who believe in none
of these things, but turn them all to use, where
we • My dear air, the Forsytes are the
middlemen, the commercials, the pillars of society,
the corner-stones of convention ; everything that
is admirable. . . . Art, literature, religion, survive
by virtue of the few cranks who really believe in
such things, and the many Forsytes who make a
commercial use of them. At a low estimate,
three-fourths of our Royal Academicians are
Forsytes, seven-eighths of our novelists, a large
proportion of the press. Of science I can’t speak;
they are magnificently represented in religion :
in the House of Commons perhaps more numerous
than anywhere ; the aristocracy speaks for itself.
But I’m not laughing. It is dangerous to go
against the majority—and what a majority !. „ .
My people are not very extreme, . , . but they
possess in a remarkable degree those two qualities
which are the real tests of a Forsyte—the power
of never being able to give yourself up to any-
thing soul and body, and the “ sense of property.*
Jt
Into this compact family group, most of
whom hated each other with a hate that
was so alike that it kept them together, in-
truded the hero with a grey hat.
“So extraordinary, my dear—so odd !” Aunt
Hester, passing through the little, dark hall (she
was rather short-sighted) had tried to ‘ shoo’ it off
a chair, taking it for a strange, disreputable cat —
tommy had such disgraceful friends 1 She was
disturbed when it did not move.
The action of the story begins with the
rumor, whispered among the horror-stricken
Forsyte aunts, that the wife of Somes
Forsyte “ was asking for a separate room ” ;
and ends abruptly in a tragedy that seems
as if it must shatter the centripetal force
that holds the Forsyte clan together; but
nothing happens. Nothing canf happen—
to a Forsyte. The wound in /the family
honor is forcibly closed, the door slammed
on the world outside. Somes Forsyte can
manage his own affairs. We are left to im-
agine the horror of that prosperous house-
hold where the husband and wife know all—
and can do nothing.
je
By such a tour de force, The Country House,
excellent work as it is, is necessarily over-
shadowed. Galsworthy has a subtle humor,
and his observation is always exact. His
Pendyces are as characteristic a type as his
Forsytes ; and in both books he walls round
his family from the world. The Pendyces
exist in their country house as isolated from
the world as if on Kosciusco.
What, indeed, could be more delightful than
this country-house life of Mr. Pendyce; its per-
fect cleanliness, its busy leisure, its combination
of fresh air and scented warmth, its complete
intellectual repose, its essential and professional
aloofness from suffering of any kind, and its soup
—emphatically and above all, its soup—made
from the rich remains of pampered beasts ?
Mr. Pendyce thought this life the one right
life; those who lived it the only right people
He considered it a duty to live this life, with its
simple, healthy, yet luxurious curriculum, sur-
rounded by creatures bred for his own devouring
surrounded, as it were, by a sea of soup ! And
that people should go on existing by the million
in the towns, preying on each other, and getting
continually out of work, with all those other
depressing concomitants of an awkward state
distressed him. ’
Galsworthy puts into words the inarticu-
late creed of the country gentleman.
I believe in my father, and his father, and
his father s father, the makers and keepers of my
estate ; and I believe in myself, and my son, and
my son s son. And I believe that we have made
the country, and shall keep the country what, it
is. And I believe in the Public Schools, and
especially the Public School that I was at. And I
believe in my social equals, and the country house
and in things as they are, for ever and ever'
Amen.
&
But the eldest son, educated upon these
sterling principles, falls in love with Helen
Bellew, a woman separated from her hus-
band. Helen, as a lady delicately puts
it, is “one of those women you never
can look at without seeing that she has
a—a—body.” The discarded husband “turns
nasty,” and the Pendyces see their im-
maculate country house threatened with
divorce proceedings. And suddenly the
squire sees that, after all, this house of
his is but a house of cards. The romance is
the tragedy of a pair who think their social
world well lost for love; but one of the
pair—the beautiful Mrs. Bellew who has a
body—gets tired of George Pendyce even
before the divorce proceedings start. So
the solution is unconventional, brought
about by the delightful revolt of Mrs.
Pendyce, the patient, faded English aristo-
cratic mother, who “has never known the
luxury of breaking down.” Mrs. Broke, in
Snaith’s novel, also revolts, and also for the
sake of her son : it has evidently become a
habit among the English gentry. If Gals-
worthy had not written The Man of Pro-
perty, this novel would have been regarded
as a strikingly clever production, but both
novels may be placed in the class, F 1.
AUSTRALASIA, AND A BUTTONHOOK
A lot of loose and scrappy journalistic refuse
has recently been raked together and deposited
by tidy publishers in a couple of garbage-tins.
The tins are respectively labelled, The Real Aus-
tralian (by Alfred Buchanan, Unwin), and Ad-
vance Australasia (by Frank T. Bullen, Hodder
and Stoughton, both per Roycroft Library). Occa-
sionally quite valuable things are found in garbage-
tins, and in Bullen’s garbage-tin we discovered a
nice button-hook. We have no use for button-
hooks ; but we mean to frame it as a memento of
Frank T. Bullen. Buchanan claims to know the
real Australia because he has lived here—may be
living here now. He prattles of many things,
most of which we knew before. His value as a
national critic may be gauged by his chapter
headed “Pseudo-Literary,” wherein he divides
our alleged literature into three schools : —the
humorous-descriptive style, the flippant, and the
erotic.
“Td be a successful writer of the descriptively
humorous kind it is merely necessary to attend to
a few simple rules. It is necessary to get to-
gether as many adjectives as you can, and
always to apply them in a context unlike that
to which they have grown accustomed. Thus, if
if you are describing something tragic and awful
—say, a murder —it is a good plan to make use of
such adjectives as commonly do duty for an
artistic criticism or a musical performance. . . .
String together all the unlikely and dissimilar
phrases you can invent or remember ; make a
liberal and generous use of ‘and’s’ and ‘also’s’;
be prodigal of semicolons and sparing of full
stops; above all, cultivate an appearance of
abruptness and of brevity.”
Buchanan has much to say, most of it qualified
out of recognition, of the Australian girl, of the
Little Australian, of politics and journalism.
He devotes a chapter to Steele Rudd; and of
George Reid he makes these remarkable observa-
tions : —“ If he followed out his own inclinations,
he would either be a transcendental philosopher
or a poet of the mystic school. He would never
speak a word about politios, and he would never
make a joke. . . . But he has never got quite free
from the idea that success is a desirable thing to
obtain. It is the one infirmity that sticks to him.
. . . He knows that for a clever man the best and
surest way to success is to play the fool. That is
why he has talked on such a commonplace subject
as polities to tens of thousands of people; that is
why he has so successfully, so brilliantly, played
the fool. ... If he has one political leaning over
another, it is in favor of Protection. ... It is
possible to look forward to the time when circum-
stances, and his own desire to be impartial, will
bring him out as the champion of Protection in
Australia. This is necessary to the complete and
artistic balancing of his career. . . . We are bound
to admit that he touched nothing he does not
adorn.”
Buchanan notes that there is so much talent
running to waste in the daily press that the con-
tinent should be able to support two or three
magazines of its own. And, finally, “The re-
turned Australian—the man who has battled hard
for a living in London, and has more or less
failed—comes back with the conviction that
racial sentiment is a vain and foolish thing. For
him it is dead ; its embers lie strewn about the
pavement that runs past London newspaper
offices, and are trampled under foot by the in-
different millions on their passage to and fro.
The thoughtful and clever Australian... begins
to pin his faith to a future that is not the future
of the old world, but of the new.”
Buchanan has not succeeded in visualising the
real Australia, but among the sweepings are to be
found occasional shrewd pronouncements; and
the book will do no harm. Bullen’s book, the
work of the somewhat tired and overworked
author of The Cruise of the “ Cachelot," is frankly
a picture glimpsed by a hurrying and preoccupied
tourist. The articles in this garbage-tin were
written for the London Standard, and were pre-
sumably dashed off in the intervals between his
lectures. Bullen has dropped a lot of butter into
his book : Australasia is yellow with it. But
beneath the butter we came upon the button-hook.
Overstimulated by the fact that he bought a lob-
ster in Australia for Is. 6 d. that would have cost
him ss.—think of it, Englishmen !—in London,
our seaman-lecturer adventured rashly out and
purchased a button-hook. And the button-hook
purveyor charged him sixpence for it! In Eng-
land, where the male inhabitants presumably still
have a use for this archaic instrument, it appears
that button-hooks are six a penny—even cheaper
than Gilbert’s dukes. But Bullen bore up bravely
under the extortion. “ I gasped, and then
laughed heartily.” The situation was saved.
In addition to the button-hook, Bullen dis-
covered several other things in Australasia. He
enthuses over the excellence of the surface and
care of the streets in all the Australasian cities ;
except, of course, Sydney, the splendor of tk 1
public buildings, and the comfort and cheapness 5
of the hotels. This Englishman never seems t
be able to accept without comment the facts that
our food is good and cheap, and that baths are
free. Of Maoriland he remarks that with all his
admiration for her institutions, “as far as growtl j
is concerned, she appears to be about at a stand,
still, especially when compared with provincial I
towns at Home which might be named by the
score.” And everywhere he is struck with the
abject defencelessness of our cities. “I do not
care whether they acquire a purely Australasian
Navy or subsidise a sufficiently powerful squadron
of the Imperial Navy for Australasian defence
The thing is to prepare for the defence of this
wonderful group of cities set on the borders of
the Southern Seas, reared by men of our own
race, Anglo-Saxon to the core, without more than 1
a trace of that extraordinary mixture of breeds
which is seen in the United States.”
Bullen notes the clear line of demarcation
between English Socialism and the Australasian
brand. He defines our Socialism as “the inalien-
able, incontrovertible right of every man to live
and enjoy life, providing that he can justify his
claim to be fit to live” ; while his definition of
English Socialism is that “every human being
born has a right to live whether he will
work or n0t.... In consequence of this
difference, Labor Legislation, or even Socialism,
does not strike me out here as presenting any
dangerous features.” But it is about Food that
Bullen becomes most enthusiastic. The English-
man reverences Food, and it is gratifying to find
that our Food agreed with Bullen. In this
important matter “ the Antipodes can challenge
the world. As far as food is concerned, it is like
travelling from one home to another.” One feels
that our Food almost made up for the Button-
Hook.

Some cheering news from John P. The
wild wail of “T. D. Mutch ” (Red Page,
27/6/’O7) is really too D mutch. I have had
seven solid years’ experience in the literary
line, and I know that bis sneers are un-
called for. During these seven years I’ve been
metaphorically kicked out of every magazine
editorial room in the English printing world;
but I’ve sold a lot of stuff nevertheless. If any-
one can offer me advice about the selling of fiction j
I want to hear from him right away. I’ve sold I
work in America, England, and a little in Aus-
tralia. (By the way, I never sold sixpenn’orth of
fiction in my native country—Maoriland.) But
I’ve sold to the first-flight magazines of America.
I’ve sent dozens of manuscripts through the post,
and never lost one, nor have I ever had an
important MS. marked in any way. The Bulletin
man is the only editor I’ve ever known tco
offend in this manner. H@ has scrawWed
“D” on small things of mine (probably mefant
it for D -), and has scratched the At ago
of the cross on the back of nearly ajHW’
verse I’ve ever sent him. Have invariably® farad
editors, especially American ones, promp®*, con-
siderate and gentle to your offerings. If V'Jfeuwat
attention, courtesy, first-rate pay, and editor
hungry for good work by unknown writelm try
America. That’s been 'my experience every i|time,
and I’ve bumped against every big ew&or
worth his salt in the States, from S
McClure to Norman Hapgood. I’ve had personal
letters from several of them asking me to subnlrit
fiction. Of e»urae, T. D. Mutch may never haw
heard of me, for I’m still “unknown,”
God ! Only my intimate friends know me as an'
author. When I got 18s. 6d. per 1000 words for a!
story from Geo. Newnes, or Arthur Pearson, I
didn’t rush round to the local “ rag” and beg. for
a two-inch “personal” to the effect that “Mr.
Scrawlem Scribble, the rising young Maoriland
author, has just received advice of the sale of a
powerful story from his pen to ‘ Scragg’s Maga-
zine’ for the satisfactory sum of 50 guineas.”
I’ve always been after cheques, and not news-
paper gush. T. D. Mutch’s closing advice,
“get an axe and go to work,” has been given
many times before, but it only applies to
the incompetent. If writing stories isn’t
work, well tell me! You go about always
with your eye cocked to observe the elusive plot,
you draft out your ideas, you write your stories,
you re-write them, you boil them down, you
patch them up, and then finally you typewrite the
MS. And then you’re told to “go to work.
The insinuation that literary work doesn’t pay is
also incorrect and misleading. I know that there
is big money in it, and that there is no more need
to pack your bag and flee to England than there
is necessity to rush round madly, and then dash
off to Siam. A man with the ability to write
well can sit down in Australia and make a good
living. Stay at home and use the mails. They re
frequent and sure. Some day I contemplate
writing an article to demonstrate that a man can
stay in Australia and have the whole world as a
market for the products of his pen. I’ll also prove
from my own experience that a man can sit
here and make a comfortable living at the ganie.
Just now, however, I’m too busy. Got 9870
stories, 167 articles, two novels, and one play to
write.
[The joyous John P. will please note that The
Bulletin has reformed. It no longer—if it ever
did —disfigures copy returned. It may think
D and say D , but it doesn’t write it.]

A. and C. Black’s charming color-books reaches
another issue in The Savage South Seas, painted
by Norman H. Hardy, recently of Sydney,
and written by E. Way Elkington, author
of a recent book upon Maoriland. The p>°‘
tures are, as usual, well produced, but the
artist fails to realise the brilliant glow ot
color that floods our tropics. But for this un-
accountable sombreness, the work is admirable,
and for the leisure hour the book makes a delight-
ful companion.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mercy.
They say my boy lies dead and cold
And by the churchside buried deep,
And long I wept who now am bold
To seek him while they lie asleep.
Asleep they lie; and, waking, lie,
Who point me out that heavy stone ;
Cold earth to hide him from the sky,
Who danced with laughter in the sun !
For he is lost, who loved to seek
The red sun’s death place in the hills ;
There must I find him, by some peak,
And swiftly, for the night wind chills.
Long have I sought him, till the moon
Swung brilliant from her shadowed shroud,
And now the sun burns fierce, and soon
Comes sombre even, cloudy-browed.
I called him through the silent gums
That silver gleam and stand so drear,
So still that not a wind’s breath comes
To witness if he wandered here.
I sought him through the tangled scrub,
Where twisted branches mock and leer,
And shadows slide, and dry boughs rub
And groan, “ He has not wandered here.”
I toiled through all the forest broad,
And by the moss-rimmed streamlet clear,
Waist-deep in fern-tips, cried to God —
He might, he might have wandered here.
I sought him o’el the barren plain,
Sun-baked to stone, and down the steep
Cold gullies, where he might have lain,
Soothed with soft shadows, deep asleep.
I called him where the curlew cried
Out of the valley eerily,
I called him o’er the bare cliff-side,
And my thin voice returned to me.
O, here he played, and there he stayed,
And chuckled, when I sought before;
See there the little house he made,
Shall he not build it any more?
O Christ, that wast a little child,
O Mary mother, by your pain—
My arms are empty, that were filled.
O give him, give him back again !
Now here they come who tell me oft
His little limbs lie cold as stone ;
Where bushes cluster. Soft—step soft—
There must I hide till they be gone.
His little limbs so rosy red,
His little voice that crowed and cried —
O Christ, dear Christ, if I were dead
My heart had comfort by his side !
What’s this warm shadow dancing free
In the light air that laughs aloft?
My little one’s come back to me !
Come back ! I hear his whisper soft.
Come back, come back ! Now sleep thee well,
Safe rocked, safe cradled. ... See, he’s found !
No thanks to foolish folks that tell .
Of cold, still silence underground 1
HARRY SULLIVAN.
The World’s Delight.
A flight of swallows across the sky,
A burst of sudden song,
And my heart goes out to the starlings’ cry
And the fields where they belong.
For now is summer upon the way,
With sun-drenched skies above,
And I shall go out in the fields to-day
A-searching for my love.
Winter has gone, with the unleashed storms
And wild, revolting waves ;
Now the haggard cliff in sunlight warms
Its harsh and pallid caves.
A passing cloud and a frail, faint shower
Have starred the bush with gems,
And the wild, wet wings of the passion flower
Float up the red gum stems.
O, lush and green are the far-off hills
That, curve on soft curve, lie ;
And an echo sweet in the valley spills
From a bird in the nether sky.
’Tis there, where the uplands burst to flame
In a riotous, golden sea,
Shall I stand and whisper my love’s dear name,
And my love will come to me.
And w.e shall follow the roadway red
That winds across the downs,
Where soft-splashed pools of iris spread
And golden wort-weed drowns.
Our feet shall be fleet through the gleaming wheat
That hides the stubble scars,
And our hearts as high as the lark’s note sweet
That seeks the day-hid stars.
O, clear is the East from wind and rain,
And drenched are the vales in bloom ;
The world has come into its own again
Across the starved months’ tomb.
My feet are ready and swift for flight,
As hot the warm blood thrills ;
Now we shall go seeking the world’s delight
Across the crescent hills.
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 8, 1907.
Price 6».
NO ROOM FOR THE FUTURE CHILDREN OF AUSTRALIA.
Merivale, chairman of Sydney Chamber of Commerce, urges that because the infant nation of Australia hasn’t yet fully occupied the Northern
Territory it should be handed over to niggers. This picture represents Merivale’s own house. When he was newly married, and the children
hadn’t yet begun to arrive, he really only required about four rooms of his mansion. So, on his own principle, he doubtless invited Chinese and
Hindus to permanently occupy the rest of the premises. According to Merivale's theory it is foolish and unpatriotic to keen any space for
the children who are to come.

How to Address hotters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with, regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
alivays is.

lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.

acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.

dential.

address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY. AUGUST 8, 1907.
THE VOICE OF COMMERCE.
In Australia a very large proportion of the
artisan classes take a profound and intelligent
interest in politics, and in history, political
economy, statistics, and the other subjects which
are the concomitants of politics. Probably there
is no country in the world, save Switzerland, where
this is more conspicuously the case. But there
are always compensations, and in Australia the
amazing ignorance, narrowness, arrogance, and
lack of patriotism of top-hatted commercialism
go far to counterbalance the unusual mental
brightness of the masses. To get 50 commercial
men together is generally equivalent to being in
the company of 49 men who never read a work
on political economy in their lives, to whom his-
tory is either a haze or a blank, and whose politics
are a mass of antiquated class prejudice, tem-
pered by the wildly inaccurate leading article
in that morning’s Aegus or Telegeaph. Of
this kind of commercialism Sydney Chamber of
Commerce is a fair example, and Mr. G. M.
Meeivale, the Chairman thereof, seems to be the
pinnacle of its ignorance, and the top note
of its arrogance, just as the Chairman of
such a body should be. Last week the Chamber
gathered itself together in all its glory, and G. M.
Meeivale spoke a piece upon Australian politics,
which should get him disfranchised along with
the lower animals by special Act of Parliament,
on the ground that he is unique. Here is the
condensed report:—
Mr. G. M. Merivale . . . made a suggestion that the
Northern Territory might be handed over to Great
Britain, to be populated by blacks from India.
Without population, or even police outposts connected
by telegraph, he said, it offered an irresistible temptation
to any foreign nation who might be able to take advantage
of England’s absence or distraction. Never in the world’s
HISTORY HAD A VIGOROUS WHITE POPULATION BEEN REARED IN
the tropics. If the Northern Territory was to be kept for
white men only, it must be kept empty for ever. The
danger of a black population was only real if that popula-
tion'was not under proper control, and a solution of the
difficulty might be found if England would resume the
government of the Territory, as provided when the grant
was made to South Australia. This might easily be done
hv arrangement with the Commonwealth Government
under conditions which need not be onerous-to England,
OR TOO LIBERAL FOR US. .
“ I believe,” he continued, that the country is quite
able to support a large black population, and that the
Indian Government might find it most useful as an over-
flow for the teeming millions of Britishlndia, welldisci-
nlined and accustom, d to the rule of men such as those
who have built up the Indian Empire and now govern it
with conspicuous ability and success. Australia would
have the great advantage of having a large tract of coun-
try, now lying- dangerously idle, peopled and governed,
not only by friends, but by her own kin, and the certainty
that once England adopted such a scheme and undertook
such a duty, our northern coast would be adequately pro-
tected against any attack. No greater interference with,
trade need happen than we now experience from India.”
Now, in the first place, G. M. Merivale in-
trudes into the province of God, and prophesies
and prophecy, especially when it runs counter to
history and evidence, is a rash and impious thing
for any commercial person to undertake. Accord-
ing to Merivale “if the Territory is to be kept
for white men only it must be kept empty for
ever.” Why? And Merivale also says that
“Never in the world’s history has a vigorous
white population been reared in the tropics.” If
he believes that he is one of the most astoundingly
unlettered persons in Australia, for his own
country is proof to the contrary. A vigorous
white population has grown up before Merlvale’s
eyes at Rockhampton, Townsville, Charters
Towers, and over a huge proportion of the
Queensland interior, and in these regions
the death-rate among people of British descent
is far less than the death-rate in England
itself. A vigorous white population is growing up
before his eyes in tropical Westralia. A vigorous
and healthy white population has lived in the
Territory itself for 40 years; it is a very small
population, but, save for the awful inaccessibility
of the place —the drawback which the Common-
wealth proposes to remove—it might be already
as large as that of Queensland itself. And out-
side Australia, as Mr. Merivale probably doesn’t
know, the Boer, who is about as sturdy a fighting-
man as even the Chamber of Commerce need
desire to meet, is, as regards a good part of his
territory, a child of the tropics. Rhodesia is
wholly tropical, and the white man is building
towns and railways there, and hustling in a white
population of which some 13,000 are already
settled. All the fairly well-populated parts of
Mexico lie in the tropics; Mexico has some
3,000,000 of white inhabitants along with some
6,000,000 of half-breeds, and the white race has
been doing reasonably well there, so far as its health
is concerned, for nearly 400 years, while, politically
and commercially, it is doing as well as its
Spanish brethren in Europe. It may not be all it
might be in the way of education, but as regards
vigor the men who crumpled up the French line at
Puebla showed no lack of energy. The white race
does well enough, too —except that it has unfortu-
nately adulterated itself with colored peoples—in
Peru, Bolivia, Ecuador, Colombiaandßrazil. Even
Argentina, where the amazing growth of the
white population has been pointed to by Fat 1000
times as an example to Australia, lies to some
extent in the tropics. The partly-tropical republic
of Argentina added 1,700,000 to its population in
the ten years between 1895 and 1905, and the only
explanation of that progress is that its people have
more grit than Mr. G. M. Merivale.
* » * »
If it is to be accepted as a fact that the
Territory, because it lies partly in the tropics, must
remain empty for ever, so far as white population is
concerned, then Mr. Merivale, of the Chamber of
Commerce, must be prepared to prove that Rock-
hampton, Townsville, Bowen, Cairns, Cooktown,
Charters Towers, and dozens of other places don’t
exist. If he can’t do that, then he brands him-
self as about the last possibility in the way
of unreliability and incapacity. And if the same
amazing gentleman holds that the Territory can
never be settled, because it hasn’t been settled
yet, then he is a still more incredible sample of
absurdity and folly. European occupation began
in Australia about i2O years ago, and the
Territory—the spot that was left to the last—
contains now only about 4000 white settlers.
In the same way what is now the United States
had been formally annexed and in occupation by
white races for 260 years before its farthest out-
post, the State of Washington, contained
11,000 people. Then in 40 years that same State
accumulated another 507,000 people. All the
Pacific coast of the U.S. was still empty 240
years after the white man annexed it; if
Merivale had been there, and had had his
way, the whole area would probably have
been ceded to China, and there would never have
been any United States as we know it. Australia’s
Northern Territory, if it receives fair treatment
at the hands of the Australian Government, will
probably do as well as Washington and
California have done. The present Sydney
Chamber of Commerce may be dead b'y that time,
but the world will be alive and busy falsifying
its lugubrious prophecies.
Then having wiped out with a wave of his hand
a thousand prosperous tropical white-man towns,
and declared them, in the vastness of his ignorance,
quite impossible, Merivale comes forward with a
remedy. This is one of the very few countries on
earth where an unpatriotic Tory capitalist could
propose to give away one-sixth of the nation’s
territory out of mere cussedness, and not be
stoned by the populace in the streets. Appar-
ently the pride of race is not in Merivale. His
soul isn’t uplifted from the muck-rake of commerce
by the marvel, never before recorded in the world’s
history, of a whole continent peopled by one race,
ruled by one Government, and speaking one
language—a continent at peace internally, with
no race problem in all the length and breadth of
its vast extent. God gave to Australia a blessing
and an ideal such as no other continent possesses,
and Merivale cheerfully proposes to throw them
away as if they were a bone and a rag. His pro-
posal is that the Territory—more than 500,000
square miles of it—should be voluntarily severed
from the Commonwealth on condition that
Britain fills it with people of an Asiatic race. He
proposes to do this for purposes of defence
—yet the Asiatic in question couldn’t defend him-
self, otherwise he would never be the conquered
person he is. But Merivale thinks that for some
inscrutable reason Britain would defend Northern
Australia with more energy if it were full of
niggers than if it were left in the hands of people
of British descent. Heaven only knows how he
works out the idea, but he seems to believe that
Britain, which has to keep nearly half its regular
army in India to defend one nigger country, is
yearning to keep the other half in Australia to
defend another nigger country. Britain could
make no special profit out of a Northern Territory
which had its own niggers to support, and
Heaven only knows why it should feel thankful,
just when its voluntary military system is almost
breaking down for lack of recruits, to have the
defence and permanent garrisoning of another
500,000 square miles of country left on its
hands. The Territory would be just as much
a part of the Empire without niggers as
with niggers, and the supposition that Britain
would protect Australia if one-sixth of it
were full of niggers, and wouldn’t defend any
of it if it were merely occupied by people of
British race and British speech, reads like the
theory of a madman. Britain will certainly
lend its aid to defend any and every part of the
empire when necessary if its hands are not too
full, though it will be a disgrace to Australia if
British aid is necessary except against a very big
enemy. If a big enemy comes along, and
Britain’s hands are full, and Australia has
to look out for itself —well, how much better
would Australia be for having the chance of
another Indian Mutiny and another massacre of
Cawnpore within its own borders ? Also Meri-
Vale makes the usual parrot remark about the
“ teeming millions ” of the India empire, which,
after all, only teem per square mile, to the same
extent as the inhabitants of thinly-peopled
Switzerland. There is superabundance of room
for the Hindu person in his own country, and
there is no need whatever for him to come and
teem here.
» * * *
There is no record in the daily paper report
that the Chamber of Commerce cried shame on
Merivale, that it howled him down, that it threw
anything at him. The monstrous assertions with
which he bolstered up his case didn’t rouse its
anger. The monstrous cowardice of asking the
overloaded Mother Country to undertake our
defence didn’t arouse its contempt. The gigantic
treason embodied in the idea of bringing racial
wars, hatreds, prejudices and dissensions into the
one continent that has escaped these horrors
didn’t move the Chamber to declare its loathing
of the whole accursed and infamous project. The
trading community seems to be to-day just what
it was when it jobbed in slaves on the Guinea
coast and sold odd lines of flesh and blood in
Savannah or New Orleans. Its soul is all trade.
The cordial assurance of the chairman of the
Chamber of Commerce that, if we gave away one-
sixth of the Commonwealth to be made into a
nigger province, it wouldn’t cause any serious
interference with our trade, showed the com-
mercial soul in its best state of development.
The sale of the bale of calico wouldn’t
be interfered with. And if the sale of the
bale of calico won’t be interfered with, then, to the
commercial mind, all doubts are removed.
EXIT MR. JOHN FORREST.
It cannot be said of John Forrest that “ nothing
beseemed his Cabinet life better than the going
out of it.” Though most are agreed that John
Forrest had no proper place in the ranks of the
present Ministry, and should have resigned im-
mediately after the last general elections, there will
be an even more complete agreement that the pro-
per time to force a Cabinet crisis is not when
one’s chief is ill and already harassed with
over-much care. Mr. Forrest’s conscience
enabled him to continue in office for seven
or more months under singulaily undignified
conditions ; it might, with an effort, have
tolerated a few more days or weeks of im-
propriety. Even a virtuous resolution may
be ill-timed, as the Judge remarked to the lady,
who, having lived with a protector, under condi-
tions not strictly honorable, took advantage of his
illness to abandon him. However, that aspect of
the case is unimportant. John Forrest has at
last torn himself from the Treasury benches and
has gone over to the Opposition. With his
passion for being a prominent personage, he will
probably soon be heard of as a candidate for the
leadership of that motley gathering, and possibly
it may be his fate to settle the pretensions of G.
Bkid, J. Cook, W. Irvine, and Knox."
* * * *
With John Forrest in Opposition, the Aus-
tralian Parliament has advanced a step nearer to
a natural settlement of issues and parties. He
was always an element of weakness in the Pro-
tectionist camp, because, whilst possessing
enough pliancy to remain in office with a party
with which he was in vital disagreement, he yet
had enough strength of character to make him
seek to turn his colleagues to his views, and to
succeed at least in exercising a kind of negative
influence. He was able often to prevent progress,
though never actually to secure retrogression. To
the John Forrest influence was due the fact that
the Liberal Protectionist Government insisted,
in its declaration of Australianism as a policy, in
standing on one leg only, and not on two.
Prime Minister Deakin, Lyne, Chapman, and
Groom are all understood to favor land reform
by the taxation of vast estates into profitable
use; indeed Deakin, at one Sydney meeting,
practically committed the Cabinet to some action
to throw open to the people the lands now held
by monopolists. But John Forrest was able
to paralyse that good intention, and to keep the
Australian Government in the illogical position of
clamoring for more population, and yet refusing to
adopt one sternly necessary step towards getting
and keeping that population. To him mainly was
due the fact that whilst the Prime Minister spoke
at immigration meetings and subscribed on behalf
of the Commonwealth to immigration societies,
the chief agricultural State of Australia (Victoria)
was actually losing its farmer population. In
many other matters of government the sinister in-
fluence of John Forrest blocked progress. A
High Commissioner for Australia would have
been appointed five years ago were it not that the
Forrest influence was strong enough to prevent
the appointment of anyone but Forrest, and the
Australian section of Parliament was reso-
lute that there should not be sent to
London a man whose sympathies were altogether
anti-Australian. It did not improve the posi-
tion —rather otherwise —that John Forrest was
both liked and respected. His personal influ-
ence was great with members, and they condoned
even his curious and undignified greed for office
out of liking for the big burly man with the small-
minded w r ay of looking at most things, and
tolerated this survival of the Crown Colony and
convict days floundering hopelessly among the
men of the new era.
* * * *
But that now is of the past. John Forrest is
not likely ever again to have a place in a
Democratic administration in Australia. The
danger that, to get rid of him as a Cabinet
Minister, a complaisant Prime Minister might
send him to London seems also to be past. Mr.
Forrest will now go into his natural place among
the Anti-Australian opposition, and the chances
that that combination will ever again climb to office
are very, very slight. But the issues arising out of
the incident do not end there. The Cabinet which
Forrest deserted has had to be reconstructed.
In doing that the simple method has been
followed of moving some Ministers up a
step. William Lyne becomes Treasurer,
Austin Chapman Minister for Customs, and
Samuel Mauger, formerly honorary Minister,
is now Postmaster-General. And probably a new
honorary Minister will be sought for among
those who are wholly unfit and those who are
only partly unfit. There is not much other
choice. The Cabinet represents about one-half
of the Progressive forces; the personal standard
of the Parliament is steadily degenerating. It is
difficult enough to get a satisfactorily strong
Cabinet from a half of the House, practically
impossible from a quarter or a third.
* * * »
Of course, the obvious remedy is a reform of
the system to do away with the methods of party
government, and allow all the talents to group
together in one elected Cabinet. That, with a
sufficient increase of salaries (say to members
£lOOO a year and Ministers £5OOO a year), would
allow of a business-like utilisation of the best
governing talent of the community. But for the
present, taking the party system as it is, the
defection of John Forrest accentuates again the
absurdity of a Labor party which shares the
power without sharing the responsibility of
government. A Deakin-Watson combination
would command the support of two-thirds of the
House of Representatives, and would allow of a
really strong Cabinet —strong in personnel and
strong in policy, since it could command a sufficient
majority to give it a soul of its own. The Deakin
Government, as things are, can hardly have more
than half a soul, and half-souls are not satisfac-
tory. What stands in the way of such a combina-
tion is not the portfolio-greed of the Protectionist
wing of the Progressives. It is the intransigean t
attitude forced on the Labor wing by the outside
organisations. When the Deakin Government
was formed, the Labor party might have had half
the portfolios. Before and after the general elec-
tions that share of responsibility was available to
them. At a sign now it would doubtless be
made available again. It is probably a fact that
a majority of the Labor party—and that majority
embracing all the best of the party—recognise
that for them to share in the task of Govern-
ment is the only stable solution of the present
absurd position. But the outside organisations
object, and their objection is fatal. For the pre-
sent, things can remain as they are, for the
urgency of the tariff issue unites enough con-
flicting elements to make a majority until that
business is disposed of. When the tariff is settled
for a while, a readjustment seems inevitable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Curious Discovery about an Australian
Capital.
At the annual meeting of Sydney Chamber of
Commerce an excited member named Rogers
moved an excited motion about the Ausf/alian
capital and the “ wrongs” of N.S.W. ; ar.d much
discussion ensued. In the course of that dis-
cussion Australia, so far as the printed report
shows, wasn’t even mentioned onco by anybody.
N.S.W. was spoken about; Sydney was wept
about; but the narrow-minded boss bagmen—the
last class in the community to 'earn the meaning
of patriotism or national spirit apparently
haven’t heard of Australia wen yet. And they
put up some extraordinary records in other ways
—witness this for instance :
Mr. G. S. Littlejohn considered the Federal capital should
be in Sydney. That was the Federal compact (Daily
Telegraph report).
Mr. G. S. Littlejohn thought the motion should be
altered to provide that the Federal capital should be in
Sydney. That was r/ally the Federal compact (S.M.
Herald report).
So, apparently the very newest lie is that the
Federal bargain fixed the Australian capital in
Sydney, arid that Ma State is wronged again
by not having /t there. It sets one wondering
whether there is any other State on earth which
is so disgraced by having reckless slanderers and
Anti-Australians to represent it in high places as
is unfortunate N.S.W. The Chamber of Com-
merce, by tolerating Littlejohn, more or less
identified itself with him, and its attitude in the
matter raises the question whether the com-
mercial class was born without a conscience in
political matters ? If anyone falsely accused
Littlejohn of not meeting his business obliga-
tions, and of issuing P.N.s which he didn’t pay,
he would probably be an infuriated man, and
would take wild and whirling proceedings for
slander. Yet he is himself reported by two
friendly papers as standing up before a crowd of
business men, and falsely accusing Australia, of
having made a solemn obligation to place
the capital in Sydney, which alleged obligation
everybody knows hasn’t been met —he is reported
to have put forward this charge as deliberately
as any common Hindu perjurer might do, or any
false witness who is now serving a spell in Dar-
linghurst. If there is any “ Federal compact” to
place the Australian capital in Sydney, then
Australia, by completely ignoring it, is a false and
perjured country, untrue to its obligations and in-
capable of keeping faith. Littlejohn, unless
there was an amazing combination to misrepre-
sent him, threw that charge at his country, just
as any dirty little boy might throw a clod of mud
at a cathedral. It is now up to Littlejohn to
produce the “compact”—which no one else has
ever heard of—to place the capital in Sydney, and
it is also up to him to explain away that clause
in the Constitution which, in the clearest words,
fixes the capital outside Sydney. If he can’t pro-
duce the one, or explain away the other, then
there seem to be two possible explanations of his
language (unless, of course, Mr. Littlejohn
denies having used the language, despite the
Telegraph and Herald reports): One is that,
though the Chamber of Commerce man fully
recognises that it is a most serious offence
to falsely allege that a petty trader tias
dishonored his written promise to pay £2l
3s. Bd., he doesn’t see any harm at all in falsely
accusing his country of having dishonored a great
national obligation. If that is so, then it is useless
to say more. Any person who considers it a matter
of no importance to throw the mud of falsehood
at the good name of his country, is so dead to all
patriotic sentiment, and so oblivious of the ninth
commandment, that it would be just as serviceable
to reason with an ourang-outang. The other
possible explanation is that the statement was
made with a full knowledge that it was a shame-
less and low-down proceeding to falsely accuse
Australia of breach of faith—even to do so
indirectly or by implication; and that doesn’t
seem to make things any better. This one ques
tion of the Australian capital has enabled a smail
section of Sydney Tories and parochialists
to reveal a depth of political degradation
hitherto unsuspected. In speeches, printed
correspondence and newspaper articles it has
shown itself a reckless manufacturer of false
evidence, and revealed itself as a crowd
which seems to consider any fiction, slander or
misrepresentation justifiable and commendable
so long as it heaped discredit upon Australia.
The man who considers it justifiable to lie on
behalf of his country is to be found in most places.
The man who only thinks lying justifiable when
it is against his country and when it tends to drag
the good name of Australia in the mire seems to
be a special product of Sydney parochialism.
And he is a most discreditable product.

The Bounties Bill.
Ip the half-apologetic fashion in which the Boun-
ties Bill is being taken through the Reps, is a
sample of how the tariff is to be dealt with, there
is going to be a lot of trouble for Australians who
believe in their country and want it to go ahead.
As item after item came up, “ Dr. Wilson moved


the omission,” or “Mr. Johnson moved that the
item be omitted.” Mostly it was Wilson, and
when it wasn’t Wilson it was Johnson, and when
Johnson was too busy—thinking of the alligator
that wouldn’t eat him—it was Little Willie or
Liddell. Periodically Greid arrived to pass some
elephantine jokes or work otf a couple of tons of
heavy sarcasm. Attorney-General Groom made
little effort; directly he saw an apparent majority
against him he bowed to it, and this naturally en-
couraged the Foreign-traders to propose some more
omissions. So the proposed bonuses for the pro-
duction of olive oil, palm oil, and several other pro-
ducts were dropped; yet on their election pledges,
there must have been a majority of the Reps, in
favor of encouraging these “ natural industries.
Some of the industries would have been com-
paratively small, doubtless, and in that case
wouldn’t have called for much bonus. Yet they
would have provided a little more much-needed
employment; they would have kept in the aggre-
gate many thousands of pounds in the country,
which thousands now go elsewhere, and they
might have led on to much bigger things. But
the barrackers for the foreign grower and manu-
facturer were blatant and persistent, and for want
of a determined advocate on the other side the
industrial advancement of Australia gets a further
set-back. If the Tariff Bill isn’t to be fought for
better than that, the result will be very sorrowful.
Johnson’s Aunt.
Johnson, M.H.R., and the rest of the party
which holds the creed that Australia can do
nothing and should attempt nothing, had a
roaring time over the proposed bonus in aid
of the coffee industry in this country. Johnson
started on the theory that coffee can only be
grown on the rim of Hell, and that, for some
reason or other, if it is cultivated in Aus-
tralia it must be cultivated by women because
women work in the coffee plantations of certain
other countries. (There was a time when women
and children worked naked in the English coal
mines, and then British Johnsons talked in
exactly the same fashion about the coal-mining
industry as a woman’s job.) “ Would any man,”
shouted Johnson, “like to see his female rela-
tions working all day under a vertical sun,” etc. ?
Probably nobody wants to see Johnson’s vertical
relations fainting beneath the sun, but as a matter
of fact there is no special reason why women
should do the work in the coffee fields; and
even if they do it, it isn’t half so hard as cow
work. Moreover, a great deal of the coffee crop is
picked in cool weather, and a great deal of it is
grown in a temperature lower than that of Bris-
bane. It grows best where the climate is cooler
than that of Brisbane, and that is why it is mostly
planted at great elevation. Also, if women in-
sisted on doing the work, they could possibly carry
umbrellas, or wear big shady hats, and also have
a spell in the heat of the day. And just here it is
worth pointing out that any silly cackler like
Johnson can laugh one industry out of court just
as well as another by the Johnson mode of argu-
ment. Johnson holds that there should be no
coffee industry in Australia because the members
of the House wouldn’t like to see their own women
folk working in the coffee fields. And by the
same logic there should also be no clothes in Aus-
tralia because Johnson probably wouldn’t like to
see his mother toiling in a shirt factory, and there
should be no cooking because Johnson wouldn’t
like to see his wife going out to be a “ cook and
general,” and no pickles because Johnson wouldn’t
like to see his daughter toiling as a “factory rat ”
in a pickle workshop. In fact, there shouldn’t be
any work done unless it is of such a kind that
Johnson would like to see his sister or grand-
mother doing it. Even ploughing might have
to be abandoned because Johnson couldn’t bear
to see his maiden aunt harnessed to the plough.
There are countries where women drag the agri-
cultural implement, just as there are countries
where women pick coffee; consequently the blight
of Johnson’s relatives is as liable to fall on one
variety of agriculture as on the other. It is hardly
possible to find one single industry except whale-
fishing that isn’t, in some part of the world, a
woman’s industry; even nail-making, chain-
making, and carrying heavy luggage at railway
stations constitute, here and there, woman’s share
of the world’s burden. But man can’t always
carry his own goods because Johnson wouldn’t
like to see his sister-in-law toiling as a railway
porter, nor can strips of bark be used in place of
chains because Johnson wouldn’t like his niece to
toil, stripped to the waist in a smithy, as girls and
women do in the Black Country of England. The
country can’t measure its industries by the likes
and dislikes of Johnson’s feminine belongings.

A Fire Insurance Bill.
Frazer, the Westralian M.H.R., knows a little,
but not enough to keep him from making himself
ridiculous. His latest is a Fire Insurance Bill.
The idea is that if a man insures his property for
£5OO, and it is all burned, the insurance co. ought
to pay up the whole £5OO. Now, that looks all
right.if you look at it quickly, and Frazer knows
enough to know what it looks like if you look at a
thing in that way. But if he knew a little more and
looked a little longer he would see that what he
proposes is quite unworkable. Supposing a
man insures his stock. He takes out a
policy for £lOOO because that is about as
much as his store ever holds. Next week,
however, he holds a great winter clearing sale,
and the stock is reduced to £5OO. If he has a
fire then, why should he recover £1000? Why
should he be paid twice the value of his stock ? A
fire insurance policy is a guarantee against loss,
and an honest man can’t want more than that.
If he could get an insurance co. to pay for his
stock at double its value—well, fires would be
pretty frequent, and the worst of fires is that they
have a habit of spreading. Either that, or the
man wouldn’t be able to get a policy to cover
more than his minimum holding, so if he had a
fire while his stock was high he would lose heavily.
There , would be less difficulty, no doubt, with
regard to risks whose value doesn’t vary like that
of merchandise stock. A suburban cottage, for
instance, isn’t very liable to shrink in value from
£5OO to £3OO in the course of a few months. Still,
if the insurance co. has to pay over the
full amount of the policy in the event
of total loss, it will have to make a
thorough valuation at least once a year to see that
the property is worth what the owner wants to
insure it for. The average suburban policy at
present costs about 10s.; if a valuation had to be
made no oftener than once a year it would cost at
least twice as much —in many cases three times as
much. Frazer’s bill wouldn’t seriously affect
the big risk; it would cost very little moi-e to
value the big £5OOO premises in the city than the
small, remote £2OO cottage, and a good deal less
than to value the farmer’s stacks or other property,
which may be miles from anywhere. Frazer’s
extraordinary production may therefore be called
“A Bill to Encourage Incendiarism, and to Make
the Rates for Insuring Workmen’s Cottages and
Farmer’s Property Prohibitive.” And the irony
of it is that Frazer is a country Labor member.
Defence.
Theke should be some move in the matter of
defence during this session of the Australian
Parliament. The present Defence Minister
shows that he recognises the true requirements of
the defence problem by attaching himself to the
National Defence League, which advocates the
compulsory military or naval training of all males
in the community. The Prime Minister has
just returned from London, where he spoke
strongly in advocacy of that same defence
programme, and forecasted its early adoption
in Australia. W. M. Hughes, who in this matter
represents the Australian Labor Party, has
also just returned from London where he spoke,
with a force and eloquence that moved the
Englishmen to enthusiasm, in favor of universal
compulsory service. Everybody who is worth
considering (the little knot of Foreign-trade anti-
Australians, who wish to make defence also a
matter of Foreign-trade and to import the
guardians of our shores, really do not count) seems
to be agreed. With all this agreement some-
thing should be done. Since the Deakin
party, speaking through the Prime Minister
and the Defence Minister is agreed; since,
also, the Labor party, speaking through J. C.
Watson and W. M. Hughes is agreed, what
remains except to make a beginning ? A beginning
must be made, or these men stand convicted of a
cynical insincerity on this most important of
national issues. There is not even the excuse to
be urged that some doubt exists as to how a
beginning should be made. That is clear as any
axiom of Euclid. The first step is to establish at
once an arms and ammunition factory capable of
making, within a reasonable time, a rifle for every
Australian citizen, and keeping him supplied
with practice cartridges ; and fitted also to pro-
duce big guns of every description that can
be wanted. The second step is the compulsory
enrolment, for military training, of the
youths now growing to manhood ; if a com-
mencement were made with those between
18 and 21, in a very few years all the best man-
hood of the country would be trained to arms,
and after our citizens had passed the age of 21 a
few days annually in a military camp would keep
them in training. The cost, in the aggre-
gate, would be large, but it would be very, very
small compared with the wealth and tax-paying
powers of the community, and very, very small
compared with the losses such a policy would
almost certainly avert later on. The fact that so
far Australia has been a cadging blackfellow
in the matter of defence gives no shadow of a
reason why it should remain so for another year, or
another month. The habit of relying on a cheap
cadged defence is the only real difficulty in the
way of vigorous action, and surely that can be got
over.
Wages and Widows.
The judgment of the N.S.W. Arbitration Court
in the Shop Assistants’ matter was the signal for
loud lamentation, and the air was filled with the
strong cry of a stout person in his agony. Sydney
Telegraph made a few masterly remarks on the
matter. It admitted that, on the evidence, a
good many owners of so-called “emporiums”
were disgraceful sweaters, and it was necessary
that something should be done. It didn’t say
what should be done, although it mildly suggested
that the matter might have been allowed to stand
over till Mr. Cauruthers brought along his
Wages Board Bill. It felt almost certain that,
no matter what was done, the shop assistants
would suffer in some indirect way unspecified, and
it seemed half inclined to think that the low
wages should be left low lest an increase of pay
should, in some unaccountable way, make the
remuneration really less while it appeared more.
It was ever thus. The Tory always admits that
something should be done, but, if anyone pro-
poses to do something tangible, the Tory shakes
his cranium till his top-hat falls off, and
declares that the proposed reform will do
more harm than good, and that the worker
is bound to suffer. Far back in the years the
British House of Commons appointed a com-
mission to inquire into the conditions of the
coal-mining industry. The commission came back
from South Wales with an appalling story of how
British men and women worked naked in the
mines—tales of a subterranean inferno where
children were reared to be beasts of burden, totally
uneducated, knowing hardly anything of sunlight,
and nothing of religion or morality—of a dreadful
place where a vast population hardly came to the
surface except for burial. It even brought back
legends of children born in the mines. And the
hardy coal-mining member stood up and said it
was shocking, but if Parliament interfered the
mines would have to close down, and the unfortu-
nate slaves below would be worse off than ever.
Under the existing system they at least got food—
when the mines closed they would die of starva-
tion. Then he sat down with a rent in his face
that he mistook for a smile of triumph. That sort
of individual is with us still, and when the Shop
Assistants’ award was made public, one of the
species, reported to be Crossman, of Arnold and
Co. (Sydney), arose in sorrow and told a grief-
stricken reporter from the Telegraph how the re-
ward had dried up the firm’ssprings of benevolence.
Crossman’s great grief was that the firm would no
longer be able to employ widows, and this is how
he delivered himself on the subject of the pore
widdy : “A lady comes to us whose husband has
died ” (he meant that a lady whose husband has
died sometimes goes to him). “She has all the
necessary educational qualifications, but she lacks
the practical experience. We give her an oppor-
tunity of gaining that experience, but under the
award she has to be paid full wages at the end of
12 months, or we pass her out, and get someone
else, although she lias given every satisfaction.
It is necessary that a woman should have
four years’ training before she can take
charge of a department, and then she
would have an opportunity of earning up
to £4 or £5 per week.” All this is very sad,
but the situation seems to be just this : Either the
firm employs the widow because it requires an-
other employee, or it sacks someone to make room
for her because she is cheaper. In the - former
case it will probably have to go on employing her,
only at better wages; in the latter case, it will
possibly leave off a very dubious species of philan-
thropy. Anyhow, this habit of dragging in alleged
widows as an excuse for low wages, or long hours,
or insanitary conditions, is becoming stale. Even
the proposed graduated land-tax caused some-
body to weep over the imaginary poor starving
widow with £lO,OOO worth of land who was going
to be taxed £lO Bs. 4d. a year. By way of compari-
son, Colonel Lassetter spoke like a soldier and a
man and an upstanding draper. He said he was
glad of the award all round ; that he was
especially glad because of the proposed increase
in women’s wages; and that he considered the
employers would benefit because, with better
conditions, a superior class of men and women
would go into the business. And it is pleasant to
relate that Lassetter didn’t conceal himself be-
hind any widow.
Maoriland’s Loan Madness.
Maoriland closed its financial year on 31st
March, and had the usual superficial stock-taking.
And, as usual, the country seemed fairly well
content with itself, though the dry-rot in its
public finances sticks out so conspicuously that
even the most fatuous islander should be able to
see that something is amiss. Maoriland closed
its year with a splendid paper surplus of £704,030,
of which £700,000 goes into the Public Works
Fund. Maoriland surpluses, nowadays, are
apparently created by charging about £1,000,000
a year of absolutely necessary expenses to the
Public Works Fund instead of to ordinary ex-
penditure. That makes the ordinary expendi-
ture £1,000,000 less, and causes a £700,000
surplus to appear when there is really
a £300,000 shortage. Then the £700,000 sur-
plus is proudly paid over to the Public Works
Fund to show that Maoriland isn’t wholly de-
pendent on loans for its new undertakings, and
with it and £300,000 of loan money, the £1,000,000
or so which should have been paid as ordinary ex-
penditure is liquidated. It is just the same as if
a man with an income of £2 a week spent only
15s. a week, and so had a clear surplus of 255. a
week, and bought his food out of it. Then the
extraordinary soundness of the finances, as shown
by the £700,000 surplus paid into the Public
Works Fund, is held to justify Maoriland in bor-
rowing another £1,700,000 or so to keep things
going. Last year this little State, despite the
gorgeous surplus and all the rest of the shiny
magnificence, added £1,988,000 to its debt, and it
now owes £64,179,040 in all. This is the largest
debt in Australasia bar that of N.S.W., and
Maoriland is doing its best to overtake N.S.W.
At the earliest possible date Premier Ward pro-
poses to borrow another million, and probably a
second one will happen later on in the financial
year. And for its debt Maoriland has less to show
in the way of reproductive works than any other
State. It builds shoddy wooden structures out
of loans, and when they wear out it replaces them
out of loans, and when they wear out again it
replaces them some more out of loans. Railways
and tramways, which are the great reproductive
assets, represent over 12s. in the £ of the Aus-
tralian and Tasmanian public debt; in Maori-
land there is about 7s. worth of railway and
tramway to show for every £1 of debt. On the
great unproductive item of “ Public Buildings,”
in which are included schools, offices, gaols, court
houses, lunatic asylums, and the like, all properly
chargeable to revenue, the Federated States Lave
wasted Is. 6d. in the £of their loan money ; this
same unproductive item stands for nearly 6s. in
the £of Maoriland’s loans. And where the Com-
monwealth has mostly bricks and mortar to show
for its Is. 6d. of debt, Maoriland has to a great
extent only frail and temporary timber to show
for its 6s. of liabilities. Yet Premier Ward
deliberately proposes to do worse in the future
instead of better. He has come back from Eng-
land in an alarming state of complaisance.
He threatens to vastly increase the naval
subsidy for one thing. Then, just at a time when
Maoriland manufacturers, who have to carry the
burden of high wages, short hours, and the ob-
servance of rigid factory laws, begin to find that
the tariff wall is too low, and that the products of
foreign cheap labor are climbing over in an
alarming fashion—just when higher Protection is
urgently needed to prevent Maoriland losing the
ground it has gained and starting on a period of
industrial decay—a yearning to show his “loyalty”
to England by giving large preferences to British
goods has seized upon Joseph Ward. In a few
lines he proposes to increase duties; but the
general effect of his proposals is to encourage im-
ports from Britain at the expense of the local
manufacturer, and in doing so he expects to re-
duce the tariff revenue very seriously. This
year’s sham surplus, after all Mr. Ward’s new
British enthusiasms are provided for, is expected
to be only £212,000 instead of the £717,000, which
was last year's figure. That necessarily means
that the loan accounts, which are shamefully
dishonest now, are expected to become much
worse ; and that Maoriland anticipates charging
still more of the repairs, patches, replacements,
wooden buildings, and utterly unproductive
works, such as “preservation of scenery,” to
loans. Then there are extensive proposals in Mr.
Ward’s programme for the further reduction of
postal rates, but there is not a word about a public
debt sinking fund; and though cheap postage
is good enough when it is provided out of revenue,
there isn’t much to be said for cheap postage that
is. indirectly paid for with borrowed money*
Maoriland is still highly prosperous, thanks to its
graduated land-tax, its policy of breaking up big
estates, its small settlement system, and the tariff
which has, in the past, done so much to build up
its manufactures. And, on top of that, there is
an extra and sham and diseased prosperity or
boom, caused by an utterly reckless and con-
scienceless throwing about of loan moneys. Now
Joseph Ward, with his yearning to manifest his
“loyalty” and devotion to England, begins to
hack at one of the roots of the real prosperity.
