Buncoing Australia.
Those who gloat over the fact that Australia has
a big export trade in wheat will do well to draw
in their horns and think a while. From investi-
gations made by an expert Board which recently
inquired into a lot of things for the Australian
Government, it appears that Australia is being
buncoed to the extent of £5OOO on every mil-
lion bushels exported. That is an estimate,
but as the money goes in the shape of some-
thing which it is very difficult to replace,
the loss may be much more than £5OOO. Where-
fore, ye wheat exporters, listen ! One of the
chief constituents absorbed by wheat from the
soil is phosphoric acid, most of which is located
in the husk of the wheat. Therefore, when wheat
is exported there is being exported as well the
phosphoric acid which has been taken from the
soil. The loss to Australia is put down at
£125,000 a year for the last four years. All this
loss can be avoided and more money made if no
wheat is exported at all. Wheat is sent away
merely to be ground into flour. The offal—bran
and pollard—is used to fatten stock in other
countries, and that valuable phosphoric acid of
ours goes to enrich somebody else’s soil. That
somebody else uses it to grow butter or apples or
something which will compete with Australia’s
other produce. The remedy is for Australia to
grind all its wheat into flour, and export the
flour. Then we could keep our bran and pollard
in the country, fatten our own stock with
them, and enrich our own soil. Australian
wheat is looked on as, if not the best, yet
among the best in the world. When bought
by millers abroad it is not ground up into “ Aus-
tralian flour.” It is used to strengthen inferior
wheats from other parts of the world. There
is no reason under Heaven why Australia
should not get all the profit out of its
own wheat, and all the benefits of the mill-
ing industry. There is no reason why we
should not have our own mills and our own
millers on a big scale. They would give a shove
along to the industrial development of this coun-
try that would make people like Bruce Smtth
wild-eyed with astonishment. Of course Australian
flour, being of a superior quality, would have to
command a superior price. How to start the
industry is the question; but the experts
mentioned above have suggested a way. All that
is needed in their opinion is an export bonus of
4s. per ton for a few years. Large quantities of
flour would then be shipped to England, and
would soon make for itself a name and a market.
This would cost a good deal—perhaps £50,000 to
£lOO,OOO a year for four years. But we are losing
that much in the phosphoric acid. So the bonus
"White.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pure blood is more than gain,
Of luscious sugar or rich ripened grain,
Of wool or gold.
Hate none, oh men of this new Southern race !
But hold your kindred in the foremost place,
And guard the fold.
Great lovely continent,
Prom whose wide lap the native nation, spent,
Degenerate,
Slips to the gulf that takes all outworn things,
The tale unwrit of its poor wanderings,
Except by Fate.
Unto their little end
Their crude wants fill, their failing bodies tend —
They were the first—
Tend till the aborigine’s last bone
Be under gravel mound or sacred stone,
All Nature-nurst.
And if so be, limbs black
And ebon frames, ’mid every grace’s lack,
A soul contain,
A Something destined to survive,
Help it, the deathless seed vet primitive
The flower to gain.
Bring no new Yellow race,
Or Black, or Brown, to spot Australia’s face,
The White man’s field ;
Won for the generations long,
Of White man’s children, unto whom belong
The future’s yield.
Old Northern stem and root,
And young strong-fibred branch, and vigorous
shoot,
From English tree,
Awake and see if greedy menace loom,
That the brave flower of noble courage bloom
Here oversea.
For they, the grasping few,
Who keen for Self, to all would be untrue ;
They would prepare
A thorny cradle for our children’s sleep,
A bitter stubble where they wandering weep
Who drink despair.
Pure blood were greater loss
Than barn or bank beneath the Southern Cross ;
And land unploughed
Were lesser waste than Austral’s whiteness
stained
By alien blood of robbers ebon-veined,
And heavy-browed.
Oh, planter, strong and rich,
And you for whom the poor and pale girls stitch,
Be warned in time,
Ere “ Traitor” ’s writ in blood upon your brow,
Ere fools and knaves who pander to you now
Abhor your crime.
Ere private lust for gold
Fill our dear land with faces fierce and bold,
Of dusky slave
(Filled with their old-time master’s savage lust),
To rain rude war alike on false and just,
The weak, the brave.
Ere come that awful day
Forget your watchword, “ Toil for little pay,”
The selfish lie,
That weak and feeble is the White Man’s hand
To till the garden of his own wide land,
Beneath blue sky.
Oh, rich man, know you not
Th’ Eternal Difference shall not be forgot
By sleepless Fate,
’Twixt Race and Race. For when one race has
rule
And makes the other man a human mule,
There gathers Hate.
Oh, rich man, know as well
In the slave’s Hour, upleaps the slumbering Hell,
And kind helps kind ;
His brother from the over-ocean land,
Would in that desperate time beside him stand
With Hate behind.
What then the false pretence?
Your barn and purse, swelled with their opulence,
Can these protect ?
Shield from the wages of your sin
Your children in the Revolution’s din
In horror wrecked ?
Nay, in the screed of God
(On which for long the men of Greed have trod),
These words are writ:—
Kind keep to kind , and race keep unto race —
Enslave nor wed those of a foreign place,
It is not fit.
Lest our last day be born,
And all our after tale be night forlorn,
Awake and see,
Oh rich, and poor, indifferent, and wise ;
The selfishness of Greed, the cruelty of Lies,
Their tyranny.
For pure blood’s more than gain,
Of luscious sugar, or of ripened grain,
Of wool or gold.
Hate none, but know that Breed.and Blood
Dictate the limits of man’s Brotherhood
And bound each fold.
VICTORIA
M.E.F.


About a good public servant:—
The death of the Rev. W. N. Wollaston, at the age of
85, serves to draw attention to his remarkable son, Dr.
Wollaston, Controller-General of Customs, and probably
the most important civil servant in the Commonwealth.
Years ago the doctor occupied a subordinate position in
the Victorian Customs. He had spare time on his hands,
and utilised it by close study of Customs and other law,
with the result that he became a lawyer, Controller-
General of the Department, and the greatest authority on
Customs procedure and practice in the Commonwealth.
To him belongs the credit of arranging that mass of
details incidental to the inauguration of the Federal
Customs laws. During those troublous times others may
have been in the limelight, but it was the ordinary-look-
ing man who prepared the plan of campaign, and arranged
the defence when the infuriated, swindling importers
rushed to assault the Department. The Commerce Act,
for which lie is largely responsible, will be a splendid
monument to Wollaston’s memory w’hen he goes. The
success of Lyne and Hughes at the Navigation Conference
in London was also partly due to him ; he was beside
them all the time, and Wollaston couldn’t be beside
Dagon’s image for a week without Dagon getting some
points from him. The doctor is without pomp, ceremony,
or swelled head. He is extremely deaf, and has the quiet,
soft voice and manner frequently noticed in those who
have long ceased to hear the tramcar pass. It is rumored
that he will rest from his labors after the Navigation Laws
are passed and regulations framed. Australia will miss
him, although he has some apt pupils amongst his subor-
dinates.
According to the Sydney dailies— the S.M.
Herald and Daily Telegraph—the results of recent
public experiments held in that city with the Tyree
automatic sprayer, the invention of a Sydney business
gentleman whose name it bears, amply demonstrate
that the machine is destined to prove a boon to the
farmer and pastoralist for destroying prickly pear, lan-
tana, and other noxious weeds, and likewise to city cor-
porations in the matter of dust-laying and tar-sprinkling.
As evidencing its utility as a noxious weed destroyer, a
demonstration was given at the Paddington tip on
Tuesday of last week before a number of Queensland
gentlemen and others owning country over-run with the
prickly pear and lantana w'eed pests. A clump of pear
(treated with a special chemical liquid, applied by
the Tyree sprayer, the force-power of which is generated at
a small cost by carbide of calcium) was first shown to the
onlookers. It had been sprayed some eight days pre-
viously, and, notwithstanding the fact that rain had
fallen almost continuously since the experiment, the
plant was quite dead, and in a few days would be ready
for burning off. A patch of lantana alongside, which had
also been sprayed, was likewise dead, and ready for
burning off.*

The Unsatisfactory Situation in Queensland..
The political crisis that recently came and went,
in Queensland dug a little deeper the grave in
which Party Government must needs be buried
one of these days, unless the whole Parliamentary
system is to break down. The new Assembly
mustered three nearly equal bodies. No party
could govern unless with the aid of another,
and no party wanted to aid another.
The system of government which we inherited
from England—and which was perfectly suitable
for English conditions at the time England in-
vented it—says that the man with the majority
must rule, and in face of the fact that no leader
had anything dimly resembling a majority it was
quite useless. The extreme Labor party, appa-
rently out of sheer cussedness, had wandered into
Opposition, and sat beside Philp, whose policy is
wholly Tory, and was apparently prepared
to vote with Philp, the Tory, in order
to oust Kidston. the advanced Demo-
crat. Of course, if Kidston had resigned,
being thus left in a hopeless minority, and Philp
had come in, then Kidston and the extreme
Laborists would have sat side by side in Opposi-
tion, and Philp would have gone out. And if
Bowman had then got a turn at the Premiership,
he also would have gone out from the same causes
as the other two. The situation was saved
simply because Kidston didn’t play the orthodox
game. Instead of resigning, he procured a few
days’ adjournment, and sat down heavily to
wait while things turned up, and while he
waited it dawned on the extreme Laborists that
it was a fool of a business to play Philp’s game
for him. So a temporary working agreement was
fixed up with Kidston, and for the time the
Government machine lumbers along. The aggra-
vating feature of the case, however, is the utter
needlessness of this kind of crisis. There
might not be a majority for either Kidston,
Philp or Bowman ; but for every measure
that any one of them or anybody else could
propose, and for any act of administration
that any one of them or anyone else could do,
there was and is and must be a definite majority
for or against. Therefore the machine of State,
except for the unworkable system of creating
Ministries, was in as good order as ever. If
the House elected its Ministry for a fixed
term of three years, then the one element of
discord—the question whether this man should
combine with that man to keep in or to throw out
the other man—would be removed, and business
would go on exactly the same, no matter how parties
multiplied. The remedy is so simple, and the
need of it so great, that it is a marvel how the
obsolete old method still keeps tottering above
ground.
“Vigil” writes: —
Melbourne patent attorneys have fallen in badly over
their allegations of incapacity and negligence against the
Commonwealth Patents Commissioner. At any rate, the
report of the Secretary of the Attorney-General’s Depart-
ment practically exonerates the Commissioner from the
charges. What would be much more to the purpose,
and to the advantage of Australian inventors and indus-
tries, would be an inquiry into the doings of the said
patent attorneys. Not a few of these gentry extract
incomes of over £lOOO per annum out of the inventor, but
since the introduction of the Commonwealth Patents Act
they have been squealing continuously that their oppor-
tunities for private enterprise are hampered. As the
Government fee for a complete patent only amounts to a
few pounds, the agent can only shove on a mere matter
of about £l2 for his more or less valuable services. When
a separate application (with a separate fee) had to be
made in each State, the opportunities for the agent were
more enticing-. Added to this, the present Commissioner
heaps insult on injury by casting a kindly, sympathetic
eye on the inventor who desires to lodge an application
without the intervention of the learned agent. Hence
the discontent. In theory the agent is the guide,
philosopher, and friend of the inventor, hut in prac-
tice his mission is to demonstrate the truth of
the ancient axiom regarding the unwise person and his
money. Not long since an unsophisticated youth, who
thought the mantle of Edison just fitted his shoulders,
was cajoled by a plausible agent into spending about
£l5O, the savings of ten years’ toil, in patenting an idea
that is utterly worthless for commercial purposes.
“ There’s a fortune in it,” said the guileless agent, and he
counselled secrecy and haste lest some unscrupulous
capitalist got wind of the affair. Out of the spoils the
pushful patent agent roped in over half for his assistance,
and the misguided inventor has some imposing documents
announcing that he has the sole right and privilege to
manufacture and sell his invention in various countries,
none of which has any use for it. To save budding
Edi Sons from the snares of the artful agent, the Govern-
ment should go further than a mere reduction of fees,
and should appoint an officer to assist the man with an
idea in drawing up a specification in accordance with the
requirements of the Act.

The Rand Daily Mail tells in an interesting
fashion of how Premier Botha rang down the
curtain on the Chinese Labor curse in the Trans-
vaal. The big man isn’t much of a success as an
orator. He read his policy speech on the opening
of Parliament, and the speech started with plati-
tudes. It went on to mention, through the
rather dreary and wooden voice of the General,
a multitude of items about company law, pen-
sions, civil service reform—all manner of things
for which the Chow Labor party, then sitting
miserably on the spike of apprehension, cared not
one straw. It went on and on as if it would
never stop. And just about the end the wooden
voice stated casually, as if it were blowing away
a wooden fly, that the three years’ indentures of
the first batch of Chinese expired that month,
and that Ah Sin would start for home as his
agreement ran out. There would be no renewal
of indentures, said the wooden voice. And the
curse being thus lifted the voice went on its unim-
passioned way to mention something about
stamps or the like, while frantic and dishevelled
Toryism rushed out to state that mining on the
Rand was doomed —and wrapped itself in denunci-
ation as in a garment.
Every other day or thereabouts the Tory papers
throw their most offensive brick at the Vic.
Immigration League’s recent pamphlet, in which
barrister Marshall Lyle and his party point
out that the first thing to be done is to make land
available for immigrants. The S.M. Herald
last week printed one of these Tory jeers under
three headings—“ Encouraging Immigrants. An
Extraordinary Way. Victorian Deficiencies.”
Yet, on the same page, the Herald had big, black
headings on top of a sensational article—“ Civic
Disruption. Two Departments Embroiled. Sen-
sational Disclosures Probable,” etc. ; also its first
leader was on “Re-making Sydney,” and the
second was on “Trouble at the Town Hall.”
The Herald, in short, has started out on a cam-
paign to get Sydney put in order, and it proceeds
in the very same “extraordinary way” that the
Vic. Immigration League adopts. It remarks
Until there is a very definite clearing up in this way,
the city will not be properly cleansed, nor will the streets
he effectively maintained.
S ill, as the Herald could tell you in her beauty-
sleep, there’s all the difference in the world
between the two cases. The “ very definite
clearing up ” which the Hkrald advocates as a
preliminary consists of emptying out officers and
workmen who stand in the road :
If it should become apparent that certain officials have
outlived their usefulness . . . there is but one course
open. They must go or stand down. The interests of
the city must come first.
But if the big landlord blocks the road, the
attempt to shift him is “extraordinary,” and not
to be thought of—it is the country that must
.stand down in that case.

If the coming of these “contract” Britishers were
not such a vital matter, and if Australia’s need of
white population were not so urgent, one might
laugh at the fuss made whenever a hundred or so
arrive in a batch. The interviewer comes along,
and asks these Cold Country farm hands such
questions as—“Do you think you’ll be strong
enough ? ” “ Have you got a stout heart ? ” “ Are
you going to stay here when your contract is
finished ? ” “ How did they treat you on the way
out?” and so forth. The men who crowded into
Victoria in the ’fifties, like those who left that
.State and rushed “ the West ” in the ’nineties,
had a far tougher time and much more dubious
prospects; but nobody bothered to ask them
had they pluck enough for what was before them.
The fact is that the foreign immigrant has become
such a rare bird that, when he does arrive, we are
quite sure there must be something extra about
him. A country with the vast natural re-
sources that everybody says Australia has, must
have had something extra bad in the way of tariffs
and land laws when it stares at an immigrant as
though he were an early Christian martyr going
to fight the lion by hand.

Carruthers’ latest pose is as the giver of work.
Helping his supporter, W. Anderson, who .is
likely to have rather a bad time at the coming
N.S.W. election, he “recalled the manner in
which additional employment had been provided
for the people, by giving the Clyde Engineering
Co. a contract for the construction of locomo-
tives.” Then: —
He regretted that it had been found necessary to invite
tenders in Europe for additional locomotives. The con-
tract was offered to the Clyde Engineering Co., but it
replied that the price of the original contract was too
low. On the advice of the Chief Railway Commissioner,
he had offered the contract at an advance of £'2so per
engine, but the company still declined, on the ground
that the works were so fully occupied it could not buiid
the engines.
If the Clyde works were full, the Railway De-
partment might have taken the job on at its own
Eveleigh works; but the Giver of Work doesn’t
mention that he fought and schemed against the
manufacture of locomotives there. He offered
the job to a co. that couldn’t take it, and then.he
was so desperately anxious that it should be done
in the State that he sent the order to foreign
workshops; and now he reckons that the local
workman ought to be very grateful because the
foreigner has got the job and the local workman
will get the bill.

Some more curious remarks from the Federal
Capital debate in Sydney Chamber of Commerce:
The suggestion that the area should be 900 square
miles was simply made to delay the question.—-E. H.
Rogers.
No member of Parliament could be sincere in recom-
mending a place like Dalgety.— A. W. Meeks, M.L.C.
No suggestion had ever emanated from N.S.W. to build
a capital in the backblocks.— E. H. Rogers.
Wonderful is the large and god-like way these
traders have of throwing things about. As a
matter of easily prqvable fact, it was from
N.S.W., and from Sydney itself, that there came
the first suggestion that Monaro was the best place
for a Federal capital. As regards the gigantic find
reckless announcement to the effect that every-
body who voted for the 900-square-mile area was
a hypocrite, who voted as he did from hidden
motives, and a kind of fraud or swindler who
was actuated by a desire to do N.S.W;,
for the time at least, out of its rights—
that wholesale sort of Domain or Yarra Bank
denunciation is too ridiculous. Quite a number
of members laboriously figured out the area re-
quired for a city and suburbs built on the best
principles, with a park, a water catchment area
and reservoir, an outlet for drainage, and
room to keep noxious trades, should such
arise, at a reasonable distance from the
residential quarters; and decided that an
area of 30 miles by 30 was the minimum. As
for the large statement that every member
who said he regarded the site by the ever-flowing
Snowy River as the best for the purposes of a
capital was telling falsehoods, a short way to get
level would be to state in reply that every mem-
ber of the Sydney Chamber of Commerce is a
drunkard and a wife-beater. Then one slander
would about balance the other.

Glaciarium Bal Masque (Bth).
Memories of “La Mascotte ” come floating
up from the valley where the dead years are
buried, misty yet fragrant as those of the stolen
quinces we ate in school-time. But after the
resurrection of the piece at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) on Saturday night, one begins to doubt
whether “Mascotte” is much worthier of the
memories than the quinces were. There is some
coherence, certainly, in the plot, comparing it
with the multitude of comic operas and musical
comedies that have crowded in since ; but there is
no sparkle in the dialogue—even George Lauri’s
gags improved it.
* * * *
Florence roving is the luck-bringer, pro-
moted from the care of the farmyard geese to be
countess in Lauri’s Imperial palace; and Reginald
Roberts is the shepherd, turned troubadour and
then soldier, who makes love to her. The whole
story hangs on the legend of the Mascotte, that
should she wed whom she loves, then she won’t
be a Mascotte any more; wherefore everybody
to whom she brings luck is interested in dropping
snags into the stream of her affections. There
should be great possibilities in this idea, but “ La
Mascotte ” doesn’t make nearly so much of the
possibilities as it might do, though the author
produced some delightful results—especially in
the “ Glou Glou ” duet. Yet even that item has
to be very well done to escape being ridiculous.
And it is well done ; on Saturday it seemed hardly
the same Roberts who sang through his nose to
the distress of the audience’s ears in “Dorothy,”
a fortnight before. Arthur Crane, too, though
still but a poor actor, was in fine voice on that same
date; and Bantock, taking Howard Vernon’s old
part of “ Rocky,” was responsible for some almost
gay fooling. Lauri was the Duke, or Emperor,
or Sultan —anything that so supreme a monarch
pleased—and he did very well; and so did Miss
Evelyn Scott, as Fiametta, the ducal daughter.
There wasn’t any fault to be found with the prin-
cipals then ; and there never is—or hardly ever
—with a Williamson comic-opera chorus. “ Mas-
cotte,” by the way, shows more of the charms of
the chorus than usual; a considerate librettist
keeps the ladies in tights throughout the three
acts. Our fathers wanted a good deal for their
money.
*# * *
The action is among the Smart Set in
London. There is a Duchess who sees her hus-
band occasionally and her cavalier servente with
irreligious regularity ; a Duke who is enslaved to
somebody else’s wife; a Lord who is a co-re. by
habit, and only runs away with a single woman
in the end as a desperate relief from ennui ;
an Earl who, having exhausted his capacity as a
co-re., cants and grows fat in a way that is posi-
tively disgusting; a titled young woman who
rushes the Lord of many divorce suits into
running, or rather feebly sauntering, away with
her; some servants to match, and—“Hoppy.”
Hoppy is a cheesemonger, with the soul of a
cheese-mite, who has come into money, and wishes
to crawl into “society,” and is plucked by the
harpies of the Smart Set. That’s the story of ‘“Mr.
Hopkinson,” produced by the Willoughby-Ward
co. at Sydney Criterion on Saturday. It’s a
savage, rnordantly humorous satire upon Lon-
don society ; and if such accounts as the
divorce courts, the society papers, and current
gossip provide are true, it is not one whit too
severe. Very cleverly written is “Mr. Hopkin-
son.” Some of the dialogue might be by Wilde,
and the situations are sufficiently strong and novel
to give freshness to the old theme of the ambitious
parvenu. As played by the Willoughby-Ward
co., it is notably the best of that crowd’s pro-
ductions, and proves the capacity of these players
for far higher things than farce flavored with
songandance. In places the comedy is broadened
out towards farce—probably a little more so than
the author intended. But that adds to the fun,
and brings a hearty laugh to relieve the half-
wicked chuckle that most of the humor calls for.
• * *
Hugh Ward impersonates Hopkinson, the
boundless little cad who aspires to high and fancy
society, with adroitness. His make-up is perfect,
and his growth from the timid bounder of the
first act, with apology in his soul, to the soaring
bounder of the third act, when he has learnt
enough to be a vulgar rogue among a set of
polished scoundrels, is most natural. Miss Grace
Palotta, as the Duchess of Braceborough, makes
a most undesirable character into a pleasing cir-
cumstance. “Her Grace” is all grace, and
she seems to put a little human sympathy
into the schemes and sins of the smart
lady. Her delightfully clear enunciation
gives full value to her clever lines. Not
quite the same can be said of Reginald Wykdham,
who slurs over some points by too rapid speaking!
But otherwise he is a handsome and impressive
Duke, with hair grown beautifully grey in the
service of Satan. A character sketch of the Earl
of Addleton—a fatted child of Baal, the sins of
whose youth have taught him censoriousness in
his old age, and who suffers from gout, sea-sick-
ness and pomposity—adds much to the joy of the
play. The Earl of Addleton’s groans appeal to
some people whose sense of humor doesn’t respond
to the Duchess’s epigrams. A smart sketch of
what a Smart Set maid ought to be if she isn’t
is given by Miss Lillian Scaife, and half a dozen
minor parts are well enough portrayed. Al-
together “Mr. Hopkinson” is a clever play,
cleverly acted.
#* * *
Perhaps the fact is that Miss Maud Hildyard
is a comedy genius with a mission to pour elabo-
rate sarcasm over the modern horror-drama. It
seems the most natural explanation of the fact
that the lady, who dresses with a great deal of
cleverness, and therefore can’t be altogether a
fool, acts such parts as Lola Selby, in “ The
Great Awakening.” It is in the main the lady’s
own play, too, so she can’t plead not guilty
to any of the responsibility. Yes, it must be that
Miss Hildyard is enticing: the trusting patrons of
bellowdrama to Sydney Royal, so that she may
“ rag ” their taste ; and one night she will come
down to the footlights and explain the joke.
* » • .
In “ The Great Awakening,” Miss Maud
Hildyard, in an elegant fawn-colored riding-suit,
chops up Jack Merton with a tomahawk, and
steals his diamonds and his marriage certificate
(it is usual for the suffering heroine who is in-no-
cent and will “ never, so help me Heaven, give up
me chee-ild,” to carry about this document; part
of Miss Hildyard’s subtle “guff” is to have the
man chopped up by a person who wants to steal
his marriage lines). That is the prologue. In
the first act, Miss Maud Hildyard, in a snaky-
red dress that makes her look like 5| feet of a
drunkard’s dream, murders Sir Henry Merton,
Baronet. She has by this time acquired
several husbands, most of whom are assembled
at the semi-baronial hall of Sir H. M., Bart.
One of them the lady sends to gaol for her
murder ; a plurality of husbands is sometimes of
value. In the second act, Miss Maud Hildyard,
in a Wedgwood blue snaky costume like a poet’s
spring phantasy, doesn’t murder anyone, but she
throws pie at the comic relief man, makes a grace-
ful allusion to her “pants” when she is out of
breath (the house shakes with laughter, for some
reason, at this, though it is natural that a lady
should get out of breath throwing pie at the
comic relief). Also she chloroforms the virtuous
heroine, and delivers her into the arms of one of
her numerous ex-husbands. A slight confusion
as to the crowding wealth of incident in the next
two acts must be confessed to. But in Act IV.,
Miss Maud Hildyard is choked by one of her ex-
husbands. She chokes exceedingly well.
• * • *
Presuming that the object is to satirise
melodrama, “ The Great Awakening ” can be re-
commended as a hilarious farce. The only sad
thing about it is that, since Miss Hildyard is her
own comic relief, so to speak, and exhausts all the
legitimate opportunities of the humorous charac-
ters even to the extent of saying “damn” fre-
quently, the confessed comedians have to fall
back on a sort of harlequinade to make their
fun ; and they are very mournful compared with
the leading lady.

The plot of “ Miss Lancashire, Limited,”
the co. that was floated on Saturday night at
Sydney Palace, isn’t as new as it might have been
hundreds of years ago. An heiress and a serving
maid who change places so that the moneyed girl
may learn the true nature of the man she is to
marry is hackneyed enough, but Sydney has
never before seen such a serving maid in such cir-
cumstances as Mary Ellen Thompson, alias
Miss Lancashire, in reality Miss Florence Baines.
Enter an adipose, yellow-haired young woman in
shortish skirts, with a broad smile and a broader
Lancashire dialect. She carries her luggage, on
which is written as with a thumbnail dipped in
tar, “ Mary ellen Thompson her boxx.” She
has come from her native province to be a ser-
vant in the house of a London gentleman named
Higgins (not Buggins—Higgins). This Higgins
is the guardian of the heiress, Eva Lancashire.
The heiress is as personally unknown to the house-
hold as is “Mary ellen,” but they arrive simul-
taneously, and, through the amazing and incredi-
ble mistake of a butler, the Lancashire-accented
slavey is addressed as the heiress, and the latter
induces Mary Ellen to take on the heiress-ship
pro tem. The lass proves a human earthquake
at which the guardian, Higgins, and his son and
daughter stand aghast. Thenceforth all is broad
farce, tempered by music. Miss Baines’ soprano
voice is bright and flexible, and her interpolations
of popular ballads of the “ Home, Sweet Home ”
kind are received—as are her draggings of the
butler’s whiskers and her back kick—with shouts
of laughter. An outrageous stage Jew compli-
cates matters by trying to get a racing debt out
of the spineless young Higgins. The real heiress
is meanwhile filling the billet of housemaid in
a capable fashion, and falling in love with
Higgins, junior, just as their elders had pre-
arranged years ago. The masquerader’s property
having been floated into a Limited Liability Co.,
Miss Lancashire wallops the butler for calling her
“a nasty name.” Under pressure, she confesses
that he said she was “a debenture holder.” The
coachman, having said something in her presence
about a “decree nisi,” next appears with a
bandaged head and a badly-damaged eye.
She has given him—so she explains as she
brandishes a rolling-pin—“ a nice eye.” The
outrageous stage Jew, the Lancashire lass’s Irish
stepmother, and her blizzard of a farmer father,
and, above all, herself, rush and rage and damage
furniture, and make jokes, and if the definition of
humor as “ Whatever is out of time and place
without danger ” be true, then Sydney has struck
something very h umorous. Miss Baines’ gifts as a
farcical-singing artiste are considerable, and
Johnson Weir, as the elder Higgins, is worthy of
much mention. “Little Billee ” Higgins looks
well, but cannot act or sing. Mr. Bert Burton’s
Jew is too absurd to win him sympathy, but he
warbles a clever topical set of verses. The girls
of the company are as flat as cold tea on a billiard
table.
%* * *
Every theatre in Sydney staged a new
piece or revived an old favorite on Saturday
night, but spite of the competition a fine, healthy-
sized audience faced Mrs. Blanche Arral at the
Town Hall, and made enough flattering din
for twice its bulk. The brilliant French
artiste sang up to the reputation she made
here on her first visit, going throngh the
heavy programme of classical and modern
music as a skylark throws off morning
song. The “Bird Waltz,” given by her as an
encore, met with an extra joyous outburst of ap-
plause. The visitor song-bird was supported by
an orchestra conducted by Miss Ruby Guest, and
assisted by Mr. Torzillow, the well-known harpist.
Blanche Arral will give further recitals during the
current week, altering her programme for each
appearance.

This week at Sydney Lyceum the Towns-
Webb championship boat race is being nightly
re-contested with all the vigor of the real, with
never a capsize nor a flick. Added to this, “West’s
Pictures” have the usual scenic and humorous
films, and things in general look bright and
prosperous.

develop something new in billiard-table acrobat-
ism, and a small girl contortionist weaves her-

Hall Caine’s widely read story of “ The
Bondman” differs considerably in details from
his dramatised version of it which came to Aus-
tralian light at Melbourne Royal, on Saturday.
But in its central figure of the wild brutal brother
converted into a self-sacrificing Christian through
his passion for the girl who befriends him, the
play appeals to the same spiritual vanity of
human nature that made the novel popular. It
pleases men and women alike. A slow, wordy
opening is followed by a rush of picturesque inci-
dent towards the end of the first act. There is a
strong conclusion to the 2nd act. The 3rd, where
the plot is transferred to Sicily for good, has a
lively scene of political disturbance at the house
of revolutionary President Michael, and a second
scene—the Sulphur Mines—which is the steamiest
and most luridly sensational work of stagecraft
that Bland Holt has produced. The 4th act drags
a somewhat long tale to a suddenly happy end
(the finale being the cheapest of clap-trap) on
Lonely Island, in the diocese of the redeemed
priest who has abandoned the bottle.
* » * *
A powerful acting play in its three princi-
pal parts, “The Bondman ” introduces two or
three clever character sketches and realistic bits
of business. The performance is bound to improve
considerably on the first-night result, but the first
night, nevertheless, was got through without any
hitches. Considering that the burden of the
trouble rested on the aged shoulders of George
Rignold, who had been in retirement for seven
years, the play went with rather surprising free-
dom in its trial canter. Twenty years ago Rignold
would have been “immense ”as Jason. On Satur-
day night he almost succeeded in looking the
character most of the time, and the art of the
masterful actor made him interesting all the time.
The strength of his personality, the impressiveness
of his voice, and his talent for creating a part were
as apparent as of yore. Jason is supposed to be a
man of 30 or so, and a thick, herculean fellow at
that. At the outset, and again in the last act,
veteran Rignold got near to a complete realisa-
tion of the character. Miss Ross rose to the
occasion when a burst of feeling was expected of
her. Maxwell, as Michael, carried a more
weighty manner than usual. Styan did fairly
well in the small, effective part of the reverend
penitent. Bland Holt impersonated a Manx
grandfather, and Miss Maggie Knight scored a
striking success as a hard-shell matron of elastic
principles. Twenty other people helped to swell
the triumph of the scenic effects.
** * *
“Lady Madcap” dashed on at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, on Saturday, for a fortnight’s
sprint, and, if all the running is to be as fast as
the first night’s pace-making, the co. will need ice
on its head and a pick-me-up at the finish. The
principals follow each other in song-and-dance
like swallows homeward flying. Watching their
wild endeavors, an audience has no time to gasp
or think, so it has perforce to laugh. If the
“business” is all old, it is often transacted in a
new way, and with such vim that the house,
under protest, gives it the glad hand every time.
But to consider “Lady Madcap ” in cold blood
is to admit that a lot of the music is very indif-
ferently warbled, and that the main strength of
the company is in the lower extremities. The
show is danced into favor.
* * * •
Nimble Daisie Wallace takes the name
part. She is Lady Betty, daughter of a Marquis,
and the curtain rises on the high and mighty
dwelling what time Betty is being severely scolded
by popper. The servants on the doorstep ex-
plain that Madcap Betty has merely asked
a whole regiment to camp in the park, and
invited the officers to “lunch, garden-part,
dine and dance” at Egbert Castle, without
mentioning the trivial matter to her aged
parent. The maiden is shut in her room and
makes faces from the window. Before explana-
tions can be sent, an advance officer arrives. He
mistakes pretty Gwenny Holden (Maud Thorne)
for the daughter of the house, and, for appear-
ances’ sake, the Madcap (having got rid of popper
by forging a telegram from the Prime Minister,
and sending him to town), disguises herself as a
maid, and the castle-full goes into ecstasies of frivol.
The cast is possessed with a mania for disguise
—Gwenny is disguised as Lady Betty, Lady
Betty as her own maid, and Majah Miles Clifton
(who is a wealthy aristocrat in the Royal Guards)
becomes a common trooper because of a wild
desire to “rough it, don’t-you-know.” The
deplorable majah enamors himself of the disguised
Betty-maid, and complicates matters further by
becoming a butler at the castle in order to hover
near his adored one. Two bounder fortune-
hunters arrive, disguised as millionaires, in answer
to a mad advertisement of the Madcap, and the
Betty-maid turns herself into a Guardsman. This
dizzy complication of disguise continues until
popper hurls himself in at the finale and sorts
things out.
• * « *
Gourier gets in good work as a Cockney
tuft-hunter, and Miles Clifton is versatile in his
many disguises—copying closely, but rather
crudely, the G. P. Huntley model. Pretty
Maud Thorne drops much of her former stiffness,
and uses up her weak little voice and her skilful
little feet with some dash and charm. A new
man, Cyril McKay (aforetime with the Waldron-
Humphrey Co.), puts his transfer to good account,
and turns his part neatly, in spite of an extra-
small singing voice. Baritone Kenneth, as a
captain in the Hussars, vocalises effectively
enough as in “The Blue Moon,” and two or three
valuable people, notably Miss Ghiloni and Alf
Stephens, figure pleasantly in small parts.
** * *
In obedience to an order from General
Desire, the Besses o’ th’ Barn are prolonging
their boom in Melbourne until Saturday. The
musical taste of the city, which is fitful and un-
certain where violinists and pianists are con-
cerned, seems to be firmly decided in favor
of the Band. There’s a solidity about the
brazen music that suits the world at large,
and draws enthusiastic masses of public to the
Town Hall twice a day. Some of the perform-
ances have happened at the Bijou Theatre because
the Town Hall was otherwise engaged, but _ for
the last three days of this week the municipal
Barn will be available to the Besses. Saturday
is said to be their “positively last day in Mel-
bourne.”
** * *
Talking of the Bijou, that newly gilded
and profusely mirrored home of the passing show-
man is to be reopened, on Saturday, for a season
of Magic-cum-Biograph. Czerny, a wizard who
has produced innumerable articles from the empty
bell-topper, in Sydney and other centres of popu-
lation, takes the floor under Anderson’s manage-
ment, and the bright surroundings will give the
show a good chance.
* -* * #
The blarney-drama of “ The Ragged
Earl ” is dealing out comic relief and sintimintality
to crowded audiences at Her Majesty’s (Mel-
bourne). Roars of laughter and shrieks of
applause rend the air whenever Andrew Mack, in
his Irish lordly capacity, takes a rise out of a
farcical English footman, or invites the perfidious
English baronet to step outside and do his worst.
A fine expansive smile of appreciation breaks
out upon the countenance of Mack’s fondest
admirers when he remarks that the con-
quest of Ireland was started in the year
eleven hundred and something, and isn’t
finished yet. Also he narrowly escapes a
double encore for stating that the only nation
which ate the Irish flag has had a severe stomach-
ache ever since. Oneway and another Mack is
scoring a complete success for a truly remarkable
drama. Probably the two principal ladies of the
co. are important considerations in this case,_ for
the heroine appears as an angel in boy’s clothing,
and Miss Berlein proves her devotion to the hard-
up Earl by loaning him all the wages that he owes
her, so that he can buy a lovely outfit of pink and
lavender satin. But the bulk of the entertain-
ment is Mack, with a capital M. He provides
the sense of harmony in the proceedings. He
strikes a sweet falsetto keynote, and acts up to it.
** * *
At Melbourne Athenaeum Hall the short
season of West’s Pictures with the Brescians has
already stretched into a long season. The pictures
are subject to variation, and the musical pro-
gramme is changed from time to time, but the
pay-box remains open exactly as before, and the
end of the season is not yet.

The Town Hall is about the liveliest place
in Adelaide just now. It has had a Bach carnival,
and is getting ready for Albani’s visit. Also it
has been the scene of a demonstration by 0.8.1.,
which letters stand for one of Adelaide’s most
useful training-grounds for future citizens : Our
Boys’ Institute. The Tivoli has brightened up
its programme with Granville and the humorous
Carlton ; and at the Royal the Holloway Co.,
which is worthy of far better plays than it mostly
gets, has rummaged in the cupboard of the past
and brought out the good old “ Colleen Bawn.”
THE BONDMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To the Isle of Man, where the play began,
Came Michael’s bastard brother,
For the brethren both with a solemn oath
Had sworn to find each other.
But Mick went away on the same sad day
That Jason came to find him,
When he left the Isle for a weary while—
O ! he left his girl behind him.
Far across the sea in fair Sici-lee,
Brave Michael searched for Jason,
Whilst the latter stayed by the winsome maid,
Her charms to fondly gaze on.
She pledged her troth to the brethren both
(She was not a cold i-cycle),
But the Bastard’s luck was to get the “chuck ” ;
Yes, she gave her hand to Michael.
So, across the sea, in fair Sici-lee,
The man of birth unlawful
Turned up one day with intent to slay
His father’s son (How awful!).
But his purpose stern took a god-like turn
ln the Sulphur Mines infernal;
There the Bastard’s hate for an ill-used mate
Was changed to love fraternal.
And he risked his life for the Mick whose wife
He had set his broken heart on,
And he shone and shone, as the play went on,
Till he looked like Sydney Carton.
He d ha,ve fain gone dead, in his brother’s stead,
But his end was thwarted —rather !
For the vengeful toff who’d have ticked him off
Was his mother’s long-lost father.
VIC.
O.K.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The Andersonian chorus angel, who blossomed out as
a lady of leisure and means some months ago (per medium
of a legacy from an uncle who conveniently died), has
been missed from St. Kilda lately. Her smart dogcart,
with its “ tiger,” was for long a feature of the grey
esplanade, and her accompanying swain—the manager of
a well-known station—usually figured as a well-dressed
feature in the procession. When the dogcart was not in
requisition there was the gorgeous spectacle of a per-
fectly tailored woman, mounted on a striking chestnut
horse, with the groom in correct pacement behind, or
the swain in devoted attendance alongside.
“Mother Goose” laid her one-thousandth egg last
week ; she is now at the Crystal Theatre, Broken Hill, and
the hen-fruit was deposited before a large audience. The
management tried a new device with the seats at the
Hill, but it didn’t work. “Owing to heavy expense” it
offered the pick of the seats at auction, and failed miser-
ably at anything like a sale. It is doubtful if the increased
prices obtained paid for the advertising, etc. Two seats
went at Bs., some at 7s. 6d., and a fair number at 6s. 6d.
Seats may be booked for 6s. at any time, so the difference
wasn’t worth mentioning. The joke of the local humorist
who bid 6s. in a hollow churchyard voice for each seat as
it was put up wasn’t worth mentioning either.
“ S.” .' Andrew Mack made a mistake in “ The Ragged
Earl,” by introducing five little boys to sing one of his
best songs. The youngsters were stationed in the
gallery, and broke into melody in the first interval,
attacking their job with the vim and power of silver
cornets. Their fresh young voices, like the warbling of
ecstatic cherubs up aloft, sounded very fine indeed, but
brought out conspicuously the marked elderly strain in
Andrew’s quaint tenor-falsetto organ.
KENNERLEY RUM FORD AND HIS
BETTER HALF, MISS CLARA BUTT.
It appears that the living statuary now barred by the
London County Council is not the mere girl-sewn-into-a-
pink-bag business that Australia knows. La Milo claims
that she was always “ fully clothed,” but one of her pro-
minent rivals was (by the admission of the management)
attired only in a strip of cloth across the loins —attached
by a bit of gold-beater’s skin—with a copious dusting of
white powder all over her. And, in the case of the so-
called bronze statuary, it was apparently hard to say
whether anything at all save girl was beneath the shin-
ing tinselly outer-coating of powder.
“ Ruru,” from Auckland (M.L.): The Bulletin recently
remarked that one of the pleasures of attending the Mus-
grove grand opera co. was a delightful uncertainty as to
which opera would turn up. This attraction has not
been lost in the voyage across the Tasman Sea. The
Maoriland season was inaugurated with the old game of
toss-up. The advertisements had arranged the first
week thus: Monday, “Lohengrin”; Tuesday, “Car-
men ” ; Wednesday, “ Tannhauser,” with “ Hansel and
Gretel ” as a matinee ; Thursday, “Romeo and Juliet” ;
Friday, “ Flying Dutchman ”; and Saturday, “Faust.”
The booking was very heavy, some enthusiasts waiting
around from 6.30 till 9 on the morning the plan opened.
The crowd at the box-office suggested a football scrum.
Owing to gales the boat did not arrive till Monday morn-
ing. The co. did not feel up to it, so Monday’s perform-
ance was cut right out. The people in charge of the
booking-office had a great day. “ Carmen ” came
off all right, but the matinee didn’t, though
no intimation of its withdrawal was given. It
just perished unaccountably like a swagman out beyond
Milparinka(N.S. W.). Late on Wednesday—toolateforthe
evening papers to advertise the fact —it was announced
that, owing to the indisposition of Mr. Studemund,
“Tannhauser” was off. “Faust” went on instead.
More fun for the booking-office. As to the stalls and
gallery people, if they hadn’t the luck to hear in time
they just had to make the best of it, or toil off home
again. On Thursday “Romeo and Juliet ” went to the
wall, owing to something (Musgrove was apparently past
the, explanation stage by this time), and “ Carmen ” was
repeated. All day Friday and Saturday the office was
besieged with inquirers, and the telephone never got a
rest all day. However, “ The Flying Dutchman ” flew,
and Dr. Faustus bartered his soul according to advertise-
ment, to the openly expressed astonishment of those who
had assembled to see them do it. For an erratic week, I
think this record would take some beating.
“ Reka ” from Auckland (M.L.) : There is a penny-in-
the-slot suggestion about the grand opera co. that is
unique ; you pay your money' and a.vait developments,
and the unexpected happens till you. begin to expect the
unexpected, and then it doesn’t happen owing to the
cussedness of inanimate objects. Illness is rife in the co.
(the Germans don’t look a fragile lot, and they eat like
boa constrictors), and out of seven operas three have
come off as advertised. Other times there’s been
the sudden attack by an incurable complaint,
the apologetic notice, and a demand for money back.
Musgrove told an irate booker that he didn’t know him-
self until just before the curtain rose what the co.
intended to play, and a piece of white paper waving about
gives him the horrors- it looks so like a doctor’s certifi-
cate. He spends strenuous days in argument with Herr
and Her, and sleeps not o’ nights for wondering what the
day will bring forth. When the people rolled up at 7.40
on Thursday to see “ Tannhauser ” they were met
with a notice that “owing to the illness of Herr
Something, ‘ Faust ’ would be played instead.”
Part went angrily home, and the rest angrily
stayed. Opera began at 8.10, and progressed
moderately till the end of Act 11. Then came a lengthy
interval, and the crowd grew anxious. “Isn’t there
goingto be any more?” queried the gods. “ Then give us
our money back 1 ” Interval grew lengthier (it stretched
to half-an-hour), and the audience grew riotous. It
worked itself into a fury, and shied its programme and
empty chocolate-box at the curtain, and told Musgrove
in rude terms its opinion of his German co. Then it left
in contingents. At 11.15 the curtain rose on Act 111, and
the audience stumped out all through the act, and stalked
abusively home. People who lived near managed to get
to their domiciles about midnight, but remote dwellers
didn’t reach their distracted families till one in the morn-
ing. It was a memorable night. And it is asserted
widely that the record interval was due to a free fight
behind the scenes ; but whether Herr Somebody licked
Herr Something, or whether Something knocked Some-
body out in one round, hasn’t transpired.
The art of booming- a faded prima donna is skilfully,
but not too successfully, practised by the crowd behind
Albani. A Ballarat interview contained unlimited gush
over a cabman’s alleged gift of a bunch of violets to the
singer as she was leaving Bendigo. Albani rushed from the
railway car to effusively thank the cabby, but, overcome
with bashfulness, he had disappeared from view. Probably
he was afterwards arrested for furious driving ; a bashful
cabman, fleeing from the spot with a blush on his counte-
nance, would naturally drive like Phaeton in his car. For
advertisement and other purposes, the humble Jehu’s
tuft of blossoms constitutes, we are told, the lady’s most
cherished possession. That seems to be going it rather
strong.
Latest letters from Melba appear to re-affirm the state-
ment (which had been contradicted) of her intention to
come along to Australia for a “ rest cure.” Possibly she
will arrive early next month, bringing her son and his
young wife with her, and will keep house at Ercildoune
(the late “ Salmon ” Wilson’s estate) for six months.
Melba has evidently made up her mind to nurse her voice
until next European spring season, but the resolution to
go and get bored to death in the Ballarat district seems
rather too rash to be true. If it be true, it means that
the tired prima donna’s visit is due partly to a natural
liking for her own Australian folk and birthland, and
more partly to a strong-minded desire for a prolongation
of her supremacy as a vocalist. Although Melba is now
firmly resolved “ not to accept an Australian engagement
on any account,” the chances are that she will try the
effect of her rested Voice on the native dog ere she
returns to grand opera in London.
Richard Wagner had literary aspirations till he heard
some of Beethoven’s symphonies, and then he became
smitten with a passion for music. At the age of 14 he
was attracted by the works
of Shakspeare, and he com-
menced a grand tragedy, in
which Lear and Hamlet were
the leading characters. In
four acts he had butchered
in one way and another 42
people, and almost all his
other characters were in hos-
pital. In fact, so badly was
he stranded that Act V. con-
sisted of cripples and ghosts.
Unfortunately, this great
effort was never staged.
Some opinions from
“Adam’s Olay,” a recent
novel by Cosmo Hamilton,
who married into the pro-
fession, and has been a not-
too-popular playwright:—
“What is personality as far
as actors are concerned ? It
merely consists in their hav-
ing a good set of teeth, or
bandy legs, or indistinct
enunciation, or a moustache
and eyeglass, or a bull neck,
or a lisp, or calves in front
of the leg. That’s a 11.... If
t he critics tell an actor when
he begins—they n< ver tell
the truth when he has ar-
rived—of some glaring and
atrocious fault, he seizes
upon it, exaggerates it,
nurses it, works it into every
part, until, in quite a short
time, it is no longer called
a fault, but a personality.
... Acting isn’t an art. It is
merely the habit of being
able to repeat lines with cor-
rect emphasis without ap-
pearing to act; and most
actors completely ruin the
characters they are given to
represent—by acting.”
“Billy T.” : Somebody
starts a recent Bulletin
story with an assertion of the
superiority of Irishmen over
all other men as love-makers.
To be sure only a woman can
pose as a first-hand authority
on this point, and a woman
of vast experience at that, but I have studied the matter
for fully half-an-hour, reviewing the Irish lovers in popu-
lar drama and successful fiction, and either Mike and
Patrick are most artificial lovers, or the representative
Bog authors have done them grievous wrong. The Irish
stage lover is a rare devil for “purt.v” speeches. He
stands his girl off, so to speak, and uses her as an
Aunt Sally to hurl his compliments at, and those compli-
ments are the purest blarney. All the time you are con-
scious that the blackguard is taking, more pride in his
neat flatteries than in the proper person of the girl her-
self. He irritates me so much with his oratory that I
frequently find myself impelled to yell : “ For the love of
Heaven, man, get into holts ! ” Hang it, a girl wants to
be kissed now and then —frequently sometimes—generally
always, I mig-ht say. 1
Stated that'Miss “ Alda’’—formerly Francie Adler cf
Melbourne—has been engaged by Mr.'Rupert Clarke for

productions of Meynell and Gunn. The statement is

about her.
“ Minndhdha,” on a family of strenuous mimes:
Our own Tittell is not the only pebble of her family' on
the theatrical beach. She owns two sisters who have
also won out in the mumming line. One, Charlotte, was
leading woman with E. H. Sothern before he took Vir-
ginia Harned (whom he afterwards married) to fill that
position. The other, Esther, was for five seasons with
Charles Frohman, and later was leading woman in “ Are
You a Mason?” It was as Mrs. Clarence Brune that
Minnie Tittell first secured a measure of fame in her native
U.S.A. Saw a picture of her in a Munsey Magazine of
1900—Mrs. Clarence Brune as Portia in Fred Warde’s
production of “ A Merchant of Venice.” It is, indeed,
our own “Tittell,” and shows her more cheerful
and less intense than nowadays. Tittell, like her rival
Ada Ward, has settled definitely on her poise here, which
is to be weepful and very intense. She looks in Munsey as
though she were wont to supply but a mildish brand in
those effective lines seven years agone.
“ Wang” : Par in recent Bulletin re Mr. Hayfork and
his ticket for a trip to Ireland, reminds me that I also
took a trip to the O’Sullivan Isle in the Pullman touring-
car in Bourke-street, Melbourne. A big, brass-buttoned
individual informed me and other gapers that it was the
greatest illusion of modern times. So I tore myself from
a sprat, and taking my seat along with others saw the
greatest delusion of modern or ancient times. A man in
a nicelv-starched white suit, who was advertised as the
world-renowned raconteur, stood in a corner, and
now and again impressed upon us that we were
passing through some of the most beautiful
spots on earth. If railway's were in the habit of career-
ing over the tops of mountains we might have seen some-
thing ; but they' have a provoking habit of crawling
along deep gullies and other low-lying places. Conse-
quently' the scenery was composed of two rails in front of
us, and about 2ft. of mountain on either side. Now and
again we’d see a sweet colleen, attired as a stage
peasant, standing alongside the line, waving an Irish-
linen handkerchief. Then suddenly we were trans-
ported into Wales, where, so said the gentle-
man in white, we’d see some of the historical
castles. I didn’t see any cattles; but a fly got oh my
nose, and we may have passed the castles while I was
knocking it off. After a whole ten minutes we stopped,
and were told that the exciting and enervating journey
was at an end. We were too much ashamed of ourselves
to even say anything severe to the world-renowned
raconteur.
A recent “ star turn ” at the New York Hippodrome
and other ’Murkan vaudevillaries are the Famous Dunedin
Troupe of Cyclists, “ whose feats of daring commence
where all others stop.”
After all, there’s not much difference between stage
barnstormers and pulpit John Stormers.
THIEVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have stolen a day from Fate,
We have stolen it, you and I!
Now, while shadows are long and late,
Let us linger to say good-bye.
For night peers in at the sunset’s gate,
And a slow white moon swings high
Into the eastern sky.
We have filched a cup from Life,
A splendid chalice of gold,
Filled w 7 ith a marvellous potion—rife
With magic of ages old !
And though at the end of the strife,
Short space was it ours to hold,
Yet we quaffed it blithe and bold.
We have snatched from the hand of Joy,
A crimson, passionate rose ;
Perchance for some happier girl and boy
Did its radiant heart unclose.
But we stole it, the scented toy !
And only the wild wind knows
Whither each petal blows.
For we flung them to deck the way
Of the bright hours, shining and brief,
Hours that were born to our laughter gay,
And faded too fast for grief ;
In a fairy land, where you held sway,
And I was a robber-chief.
Dost sorrow, dear fellow-thief ?
Nay, weep not! The world grows old,
And nothing is worth a sigh ;
Though the dusk of life grow dim and cold,
’Tis immortal, our day gone by ;
A jewel ashine in memory’s gold,
And—the iast red fades in the sky,
Sweet siiia r, good-night, good-bye !
S.A.

PENEOLD'S
WINES
A Leichhardt (Sydney) milk vendor was
recently convicted for the ninth time of selling
watered milk, his most recent mixture being five
parts milk and one of Prospect reservoir. This
offender’s previous fines—not counting costs—
totalled £BB, and though the magistrate had the
option of sending him to gaol he let him off with
a bill for £2O. Anyone can find the cash to pay
fines by selling doubtful water at the price of good
milk if he only sells enough water; and the
Government which augments its revenue by taking
nine separate fines from one milk adulterator is a
partner in the job, and is itself selling water and
dealing in the murder of the infant milk-drinker.
When it has become evident that fining a swindler
won’t stop him from pursuing his fraud, then the
authority which continues fining him, when it
should send him to gaol, is a mere blackmailer who
goes shares in the loot, the same as the policeman
who fines the Sunday pub a free beer for himself.
TOTALLY IRRESPONSIBLE.
CUSTOMER : "Ere, waiter, wot the dickens is
this—tea or coffee ?”
WAITER : “ Blowed if I know. I’m leavin’ next
week.”
As a rule, when the dilapidated land
boiler bursts, it destroys some poor devil who
was driven to stoke it for a living, and a mighty
poor living at that. It is a wise boiler that knows
how to select its victim, and most boilers seem to
befools. Their conduct almost makes one think
that ftehoboam was a boiler. Still there are ex-
ceptions. In one N.S.W. town recently the
threadbare boiler hit its owner with an accuracy
which —assuming it had to hit somebody—did the
old ruin credit.
The haste of two Australians is responsible
for some recent criticism of the Victorian marriage
laws. On June 3rd, John Halliday, having a
spare half-hour, strolled into a Melbourne wine-
shop and imbibed a trifle. Presently entered a
perfect stranger named Catherine Monaghan,
who also imbibed some, at John’s expense. After
conversing a space, they separated, but fore-
gathered again in the evening and, both of them
having another half-hour to spare, they adjourned
to a matrimonial agency and were married at
9.30 p.m. The hasty couple seems to have rushed
to repentance with equal speed; anyway, Kate
presently asked the North Melbourne Court for a
maintenance order, on the ground that her John
had deserted her before the honeymoon was over—
within 14 days, in fact. John averred that his bride
interviewed his employer while she was in a very
imbibed condition, and thereby lost him his
billet; also he was quite drunk when he married
her, and saw two of her, if not three, and which
of her he married he hardly knew. The Court in-
directly assured Mr. Halliday that he was a great
ass, and desired him to continue keeping Kitty
at the rate of 10s. a week, with costs. John’s
assertion that he was completely unsober is dis-
counted by the parson who did the 9.30 p.m.
marriage; he remembered the occasion clearly
because the lady seemed very anxious to be cer-
tain the marriage was legal and binding; also he
produced the marriage certificate butt showing
Halliaay’s signature three times in a firm, round
hand. Nevertheless, various persons are now
advocating the restriction of marriage celebrations
to “ business hours”—10 a.m. to 7 p.m., or there-
abouts. That would be something ; but they might
as well go the whole quadruped and advocate that
a marriage should not be a marriage unless a civil
officer—registrar or some such—was present.
That would be better protection than to fix the
hours from 10 a.m. to 7 p.m. The Bulletin has
known men to be very unsober as early as
9.45 a.m.
.* * # *
The Sydney “ Society of Artists ” is, at
last, to be revived. Leading members in the
Society are D. H. Souter, Norman Lindsay,
Lionel Lindsay, Alice Muskett, J. J. Hilder,
Howard Ashton, Fred Leist, Harry J. Weston,
Douglas Fry, Sid Long, Julian Ashton, and
Arthur Streeton. With such an array of artists
the Society’s forthcoming exhibition should prove
interesting. The show opens early next week, in
the gallery (No. 127) next the Public Lending
Library in the buildings where the late Queen
Victoria was wont to go marketing on Saturday
nights. These rooms were “discovered” by
Streeton, and made familiar to art-lovers by his
recent show. The Society is following the sen-
sible arrangement of allotting a panel to each
artist, thus keeping each exhibitor’s work
separate. It is prophesied that the sections
allotted to Norman Lindsay’s black-and-white
work, and to a new exhibitor in the water-color
section, J. J. Hilder, will be worth scrutiny.

Methodism was a novelty in Australia
when “Father” John Watsford, lately!passed
ou t, was a youth, and his descriptions of how they
converted one another in Parramatta in those
early days were vivid pictures of holy riot. Some
of the preachers roared like a cattle-sale in
Bashan, and people went up and down the streets
alternately weeping and yelling. Probably it is
the reaction from those early excesses that makes
the Parramattans of to-day so tired and slow.
* *■ * »
There ought to be a future for a contri-
vance evolved by a couple of Australians —one
Captain Grainger and the other Dr. Harker—for
the purpose of fighting fires at sea. The
idea is to lay hold of the waste gases from
the uptake of a vessel’s boilers, and, after
cooling and partially cleaning them, to use
them to smother any fire that may break
out. Harker, the scientist of the pair of
inventors, figures it out that a ton of coal
produces about 400,000 cubic feet of gas,
so that in ordinary circumstances a steamer
always has enough gas on tap to put the pro-
cess to the test; about all that is needed is a
contrivance to suck up the gas and a fan to
drive it where it is needed. The other day
Sydney fire-superintendent Webb and a
cargo of fire-insurance men watched efforts
made to "burn out an old hulk lying in
Kerosene Bay (Sydney Harbor). An area
was bulk-headed off, and sacking, saturated
with kerosene, was lighted in it. Then the
gas was turned in, and the blaze subsided
and died as dead as Carruthers’ proposal to
build a three-legged bridge to North Syd-
ney. After that, other methods of incen-
diarism were tried, but the gas won every
time. Of course, if any person happened
to be wandering round the hold while the
gas was busy thereabouts, he would go out
as quickly as the fire; but, after all, it
isn’t the practice to use a burning hold as
a promenade deck. Grainger and Harker
claim that, while the big passenger steamer
would always have the material on hand, the
average sailing-ship mostly has a donkey-
engine capable of supplying enough gas to
beat any ordinary fire amongst any ordi-
nary cargo. It’s a scheme with great possi-
bilities in it, anyhow, and the Harbor tests came
out all right.

Why is the social purity person always so
wild and reckless a purveyor of the thing that most
obviously is not ? Consider the howl and shout
of the Rev. Wilbur B. Crafts
When I see £600,000 go to Tasmania for Tattersall’s
sweeps in one year, I feel that Monte Carlo is not entitled
to its fame. Three or four times as much money goes to
Tattersall’s from Australia in a year as goes to Monte
Carlo from the whole world in the same time.
Ed. Blanc, the multi-millionaire racing man, all
of whose cash comes from the Monte Carlo
Casino, spends, according to the French press,
over £500,000 per annum merely on horse breed-
ing and on his estate and stables, near Paris. He
has a brother Henri, also a millionaire, whose
income is also derived entirely from the little
Riviera gaming hell. And before these two men
may handle one solitary bean of their dividends,
they have to pay, by the terms of their well-
known business arrangement with the Monaco
princelet, for the upkeep of the entire princi-
pality of Monaco, which is in consequence un-
taxed. Also, they have to maintain their palatial :
gambling hells, and to pay the winners what they
have won. Gambling may be bad in Australia, but
in Europe, and in the country Wilbur B. Crafts
has imported himself from, it is immeasurably
worse. Why should people like Crafts come to
hurl decayed fish inaccuracies at a land which
already has a local press panting to do the job?
You never know your luck. Last week a
Victorian, weary of things, climbed about 40ft.
up to the gallery of a windmill and dived off it,
intending to break his neck. But the swaying
bough of a tree broke his fall, and he only
managed to scratch himself. On the same day
another Victorian, a carriage-cleaner at Spencer-
street (Melbourne), fell off a trestle about 3ft.
high. He broke his neck all right, though it was
probably the last neck in the world that he
wanted broken.

The committee of the Anglican diocesan
festival m Melbourne, being “ impressed with the
necessity of cultivating Australian sentiment ”
has offered a prize of two guineas for the best set
of four original hymns and one Australian
national anthem. Eighty-four sixpences doesn’t
seem much for four hymns and a stirring patriotic
chant, but then the kind of national anthem that
would please a church festival might not be worth
more. One would hardly offer the Marseillaise to
an institution like that.
The importing disease breaks out in strange
places in N.S.W. National Park, 18 miles from
Sydney, is supposed to be a great preserve of our
natural flora, and yet its approaches are already
dotted over with great clumps of imported Scotch
firs—ugly, ragged-looking, funereal hearse-plume
trees—and American pines. Now, as a crowning
atrocity, some miscreant has commenced to cut
down the gums to make way for an avenue of
imported trees. What do we want with imported
trees in an Australian National Park? If the
trees of the park are not good enough, then let
other Australian trees, such as Moreton Bay figs,
or kurrajongs, or graceful white gums be brought
there and planted. Every morning for the best
part of a week writer has been moved to deep
curses at the sight of a fine, old, weather-scarred
gum waving its branches against the sky, while a
fire was nourished at its butt to bring it down. A
man who burned a fine old living tree like that
would kill dogs, and ought to be placed under
restraint. About time Frank Farnell, chairman
of the Trust, who is a good Australian in some
things, went out to his domain and chained up
some of these men who are turning the National
Park into a sample plot of Yankee and other
foreign woods.
* * * *
Forty-eight Kanakas, booked for deporta-
tion by the Makambo, recently asked, on various
pretexts, to be allowed to remain in Queensland,
and, as usual, got some parsons to back them up.
One colored brother wanted exemption on the
ground that he had given £3O to a local store-
keeper to take care of for him, and' the grocery
man had failed to pay up. Immigration Agent
Brennan promised to collect the brother’s money,
if there was any such money; but the brother
explained that if the cash wasn’t immediately
forthcoming he would murder the first missionary
he met on his island home. Now the parson
knows exactly his own value in the eyes of the
Kanaka whom he has made “gentle and civil-
ised,” and over whose return to his “savage
island” he has wept so copiously. The value is not
more than £3O and it may be less. The Kanaka
might have made the same row over £5, and it
may be that the only reason why he didn’t pro-
mise to kill six missionaries for the £3O was that
he knew there was only one sin-shifter on his
island.
AUSTRALIA’S KIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let us people the Northern Territory with Indians—
people of our own kin.— Chairman of N.S.W. Chamber
of Commerce., Merivale, at the annual meeting.
A fraud is Herbert Spencer proved,
And Huxley’s out of date ;
We’ve got no use for Darwin with
The news we have of late.
For there’s a new ethnology
That’s never known to fail;
There’s a new interpretation
Of the station
Of a nation
From the very merry Merivale.
Though he’s grown a trifle darker
Out in India’s torrid clime,
And you’d fail to recognise him,
He’s a Itriton all the time.
The coon who’s driving oonts is from
Stern Caledonia’s shore,
While the dusky Irish laddie—
Lightly clad, he
Tills the “ paddy”
With an ebon Anglo-Saxon from Lahore.
So praises raise to Merivale,
The new ethnologist,
Who’s pointed out a lot of things
The other savants missed ;
And shown the Briton spindle-shanked
Attired in sable skin,
And proved that climate’s rigor
Turns the figure
To a nigger
Of the man who is Australia’s kin.
N.S.W.
MILTON MACGREGOR.
THE HAT OF PERDITION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. A. Crockett, speaking at the Presbyterian
Church Workers’ Conference (Melbourne), claimed that a
great many people were driven from the church by the
fine dresses habitually worn in the House of God by
their well-to-do neighbors. The gorgeous raiment
of the rich makes the ordinary habiliments of the
poor appear shabby, and so the poor stay away to avoid
odious comparisons. The Rev. Crockett asks the well-
to-do worshippers to exercise a little “ Christian self-
denial,” and attend the House of God, decently and
cleanly dressed, but not in their very best. It is assumed
that the good man is addressing himself mainly to
ladies :
Now, having heard this hot appeal,
We trust that Mistress Tallophat
Will see ’tis needful to conceal
Her very latest Paris hat.
The tile that suits her face so well
Is sending erring souls to Hell.
To church on Sunday she should go
In cheaper skirts—a dowdy rig ;
A shilling “gem,” with just a bow,
And shoes at least a size too big,
That poorer sisters far from smart
May worship God with all their heart.
Too long have worldly women ’mid
The wreck of fellow creatures’ souls
Worshipped in gowns worth fifty “ quid,”
Or hats that cost a brigand’s toll,
And made the 20-guinea toque
Across the pew a sorry joke.
For no church built will matrons strive
To rid themselves of pride accurst,
And do their utmost to contrive
To dress the cheapest and the worst.
In Heaven the angels do not brawl
Because forbid to dress at all!
VIC.
S. SNELL.
