SYDNEY.
The cave-man has cropped up again—this
time at Quirindi (N.S.W.), where George Tofft, a
kangaroo shooter, aged 20, shot Maud Fletcher,
aged 17, and then battered in her head to make
quite certain of his work. Maud Fletcher, described
as “ a fine girl, rather prepossessing, and well-
developed,” seems to have been Tofft’s sweet-
heart in moderation ; the cave-man apparently
“kept company” with her more by a system
of terrorism than by the influence of poetry and
soft blandishments. He is even said to have
beaten her more than once, probably to let her
understand that, unless she walked the chalk-line
laid down for her, extra copious punishment
might be expected when they were married, and
after he had dragged her by the hair to his
cavern. Probably she decided at last not to
share any cavern with a relic of the Stone Age,
and then he issued his pakeozoic ukase that she
must wed him or pass out. So he killed her, and
took to the hills like a deer, just as his immediate
ancestor might have taken naturally to the tree-
top under like conditions. And when this was
written he was still in the hills, and a police
force that was almost dead with ’flu’ was looking
for him.
WINTER FASHIONS FOR 1907.
SLAUGHTER-HOUSE MODE.
A meeting of Old Theologists, with Rev.
Fitchett prominent in the foreground, was held
at Melb. Town Hall, last week, for the purpose
of “reaffirming” the wicked foolishness of
Advanced Thought concerning Christian dogmas.
The reaffirmation was a complete success. An
Old Theologian explained that there are “three
alternatives of belief as to the historic Christ,”
one of which is profane, and, therefore, “un-
thinkable.” When it comes to a question of
good hard logic, the Old Theology scores every
time. It turns up its eyes, and dismisses the
unorthodox alternative as “unthinkable,” and
there you are ! You mustn’t think it.
SARCASTIC.
HOSTESS (sweetly): “Have you got all you require?”
Police Magistrate Cresswell, who came
from Gippsland to take brother Panton’s place on
Melbourne City Bench, raised some hopes as soon
as he arrived by speaking firmly to a culprit who
had resisted the arresting constable. In future,
he said, assaults on the police, and blackguardism
generally, would be dealt with drastically. Next
day there appeared before him two ruffians, with
records as long as they were disgraceful, who had
badly damaged two constables while resisting
arrest, and for whose subj ugation a posse had to
be brought from Russell-street lest the struggle
should end in murder. That piece of blackguard-
ism Cresswell deemed worth “one month”—
exactly the same as he forthwith proceeded to
award to a casual street mendicant. Magistrates
seem to be all much the same.
This next warm weather season Sydney
will see a mighty battle between Manly and
Coogee for the honor of having the most shapely
youths and damsels disporting themselves in their
respective waters. The surf-bathing rivalry has
come to a head. Coogee has decided to build
a number of dressing-rooms, beside which the
famous baths of Caracalla will be but a mere cir-
cumstance in magnificence. Manly is,
meantime, divided on the question
whether it should charge the public a
fee for the privilege of washing itself.
Behind both places are strong bodies
of local patriots armed with further
proposals for attracting the people.
One party desires to carry duly accre-
dited surf-bathers from the city free of
charge. Another wants to give them
costumes free. A maniac has a pro-
ject whereby every person who visits
the shore of his lunatic asylum for the
laudable purpose of “shooting ’em,”
shall receive afternoon tea gratis.
Manly has the advantage, because a
harbor trip is vastly more pleasant than
a tram journey, but the stupid restric-
tions which govern bathing at the
village get in the way. Why bathers
should be prohibited from lying on the
beach while they are permitted to
stand up on it is beyond any but the
aldermanic understanding. The cos-
tume restrictions are also ridiculous.
The lady who intends to adopt surf
bathing as a profession has necessarily
to reveal something of her form; and,
while the skirts that are mandatory at
Manly fail utterly to conceal the lady
as they are supposed to do, they add
considerably to her chance of drowning.
Further, the tight costumes worn at
Coogee and Bondi, while making no
pretence to conventional modesty, can
always be relied upon to give no
further revelations; while those of
Manly are liable to all sorts of acci-
dents. But whatever else happens
it is certain that the surf bathers of
the coming summer will largely out-
number those of the past season, and
before another five years it seems likely
that the whole population of Sydney
will be able to shoot the biggest breakers
with the best of them.
A Melbourne accused announced in court,
the other day, “ I object to being tried by Roman
Catholics. I want to be tried by a j ury of Pro-
testants, and I will stand or fall by their verdict.”
The idea seems capable of very useful extension.
For instance, a prisoner whose conscience would
only allow him to be tried by Culdees would
probably Be out on bail all his life, Culdees being
extinct at present. And there would be a fine
prospect for the man who refused to have tram-
drivers on the jury, and insisted that they should
be replaced by link-boys and sedan-chair carriers.
» * * *
I token Hill had 30 points of rain the
otheday, and it postponed a race meeting in
consequence. It illustrates the development of
the human species. Broken Hill wouldn’t post-
pone as much as a dog-fight if the sun blazed out
till the thermometer climbed up to 116 degrees;
but a shower measuring something well under a
third of an inch sends a demoralised Broken Hill
into shelter, where it stays till the last drop has
sizzled into the droughty earth. The cocksure
people who live away down in the South, and who
say that “ the white man can’t live in the North, ”
because it doesn’t happen to be the climate they
are used to themselves, ought to fasten the inci-
dent somewhere in the empty spaces of their
brains.
• * * *
A recent inquest on a Melbourne woman
who died in childbirth should be noted by unim-
portant matrons as a warning against the danger
of pre-engaging a medical nian for a midwifery
case. In this instance the pre-engaged doctor
was. summoned at 8 o’clock, but could not leave
another serious case until after midnight. Mean-
while, the husband went around in a cab seeking
the assistance of other doctors, who mostly excused
themselves when told that a brother practitioner
had been booked for the job. The critical condi-
tion of the patient didn’t justify a breach of
medical etiquette they said. Finally, the hus-
band went back home, and learned from the
belated pre-engaged doctor that his wife was
dead. There ought to be a law strong
enough to gaol some of these other doctors
for manslaughter, but probably there isn’t.
When a medico hangs out his plate and his
danger signal, it is understood to signify the
presence of a man who can be relied upon, if
not engaged on another patient, to come and save
your wife’s life, if it is capable of being saved, on
certain fairly definite cash terms. The medico
doesn’t hang out a book of etiquette to explain
that there are other conditions. In this case a
woman died while her husband was wasting his
time rushing round among doctors who had hung
out the red lamp on something very like false
pretences. It was as wretched a joke as that of
the lout who reverses a finger-post, so that the
man who is riding in red-hot haste to Boom-
anoomanah to get medical aid in an urgent case,
brings up 20 miles off his track at (say) Gobuekan-
idgery, where there is nothing to fall back uponsa ve
a publican. A doctor who has the professional
etiquette complaint very badly should hang an
inscription on his bell: “ Wait here while your
wife dies.”

S.M. Herald (27/7,/’O7)
PURVEYOR requires Field Assistant, able to use
theodolite, chain, calculate, and plot, six months’
engagement; wages, 255. per week and keep. Apply
F. I. Soiling, Survey Camp, Moree ; or personally at
46 Edgecliff-road, Woollahra.
The above enables one to understand the scarcity
(recently commented on by the Queensland Minis-
ter for Lands) of surveyors in Australia. To
hump a heavy theodolite from daylight till dark,
swing an axe or pick as required, and then bring
a well-stored mathematical brain and a guttering
candle to bear on abstruse calculations till far
into the night—in short, to be a surveyor in every-
thing but name for, say, £2 a week—isn’t a bright,
lure to entice the intelligent youth of the country
into the profession. An eight hour, Bs.-a-day
dirt-shoveller’s job looks roseate beside it.

Dorothy McCrae from the Suez Canal: —
Slowly, slowly, we creep up the Canal. Suez on one
side ; the desert on the other. But it is the desert that
claims me. So wide, so silent, so golden, so mysterious.
Above there is a faint blue sky; beyond, some azure
hills ; here a violet shadow ; there a clump of tents, a
cluster of camels, a handful of Arabs. We take the
glasses and look closer, and see the bronze skin, the
colored robes. Heliotrope, yellow, vivid blue. Blue that
burns. Then we hang over the boat-side, and stare at
the water—peacock green (or is it blue again ?), a hue
perfect, unpaintable. A dhow, with snowy sails out-
spread, passes silently, steered by an Arab with a turban
of vivid scarlet. We feel color-dazed, and seek relief in
the soberer tints of Suez. Suez with its mosques and
palms and patches of water and scanty green. At last
twilight comes—a twilight whose cheeks are still rosy
with the kisses of sunset. More dhows pass, and we note
bundles of rushes in the bows. The world is bathed in
an opal light. Stars come out, and the sky deepens in
tint. Jewel-like lights spring to life on the great
steamer passing so close to us, and the search-light in
the bows of our own vessel makes mysterious pictures of
the heaped-up sand that gleams white and ghostly on the
water’s brink.
Fitzroy is not the only place that has
suffered through the act of ex-P.M. Panton in
hunting Tottie and her kind out of Lonsdale and
Little Lonsdale streets, Melbourne. Bendigo has
been favored with a considerable influx of the
sisterhood of Mrs. Rahab of Jericho. Bendigo,
too, was just shaking hands with itself and saying
that the social evil was almost stamped out there.
One street that was once almost given over to the
unrepented and fluffy Magdalene had become
the acme of respectability. Even its former name
was discarded to prove that it had put on the
snowy clobber of righteousness, and it was re-
named Chapel-street. But Magdalene is back,
and the old Rahab has taken unto herself seven
other Totties each worse than the olden Flossie,
and the street that had cleaned itself up is no
longer large enough to hold Cleopatra, conse-
quently Phryne pursues her unsteady job all over
the city.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Chief Justice was moved
recently to lay it down that the evidence of the
private detective is dangerous, and sh< uld have
credence only if corroborated. Mostly the private
’tec. is a person suffering from a great disinclina-
tion for honest toil, or an unfortunate whose
features tend to preclude him from every other
sort of job. He is paid to see things (not snakes),
and he is paid by somebody who wants those
things seen very badly indeed. His continued
prosperity, such as it is, demands that he should
see them, so he sees them. If what he looks for
doesn’t happen, he has either to make it happen
and see it, or to see it in his mind’s eye, as Ham-
let said to Horatio. It is difficult to guess how
he would live otherwise. So many people can see
the thing that is, that there is little money in it;
but to see convincingly and plausibly the thing
that isn’t is rare. It is in the shady ramifications
of unwholesome JDivorce Court proceedings that
tie private ’tec. moves and finds his sustenance.
He is often on a par with the ancient Old Bailey
institution, who wore straw in his boots as an
indication that evidence—any evidence —was
available on the shortest notice. And he deserves
the same consideration, or worse.

A curiously circuitous statement comes
here via Cairns (Queensland). It is alleged that
“ Mr. G. Caldersmith, headmaster of the Central
School, Townsville, Queensland,” wrote to the
Musical Herald, London, and it published his
screed, and the Cairns paper republished it, and
then a disgusted resident cut it out and sent it
along. The writing of Mr. G. Caldersmith, of
Townsville, was a proud statement to the effect
that his school choir had learned to sing the
Japanese National Anthem to the original Jap
words. It doesn’t seem so very much to be proud
of, but there are a few queer cattle in the North
Queensland coastal towns. Admiral Kamimura,
it is stated, was so glad to find Japanese influence
spreading in this fashion in Australia that he
gave £5 to the school prize fund. Well, the
school deserved it. The wages of grovel are cash.

“ D.” : Walking homeward t’other night
I heard an outcry, like all Wagner let loose, pro-
ceeding from an ordinarily-deserted side street of
my suburb. Thinking for the moment that a
German opera co. had burst out of its cage, I
hurried to the storm-centre, which was already
surrounded by a deeply-interested crowd. The
outcry came from a stout lady (partially
drunk), who reposed agitatedly upon the footpath
while a dangerous-looking male acquaintance
(rather more than partially drunk) kicked her
violently, daring all and sundry the while to
stop him by joining issue in open combat.
The contract was past the majority of us; I would
as soon, for my own part, have tackled a Bengal
tiger. But just as I was girding my loins to go
away and seek the elusive Law, the entire diffi-
culty resolved itself into nothingness, like a mail
cqntract grievance. Gently through the shudder-
ing press came a small, calm, ugly boy. In his
hand he poised half a stone gingerbeer bottle. For
the manyth time the defiant one kicked and
turned, clamoring for stoush. All in a flash his
desire was accorded him, full measure and brim-
ming over. Hurled by a practised arm, the
brown substance that had once contained the
liquor Judkins best loves struck the brawler
between the eyes. No Burns knock-out could
have been more effective. As the gentle lad had
come, so he went —not waiting even to salve his
fragment of pottery—and was seen no more.
Gathered subsequently from the lady, while we
jointly ministered to her sorely-injured friend,
that the bottle-hurler was a total stranger. Nor
were her feelings towards him those of the conven-
tional rescued female. In fact, my pen wobbles
with horror as I remember the blank she called
him, and the dash and the asterisk that she
accused him of.
* * * *
The John W. Dare, a coal-lighter, owned
by the Melbourne Steamship Company, is the
oldest hulk in Melbourne waters. Although only
76 years of age, the build and generally-antiquated
appearance of this vessel suggests that it had been
part of the Invincible Armada, or had helped
Columbus to find America. It was originally an
armed brig in the China tea trade, where an
encounter with Penang pirates and other blood-
thirsty casuals was an everyday affair. The old
wooden warrior has been following the useful
occupation of floating coal-yard since somewhere
about 1864.
A “BULLETIN” GATHERING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a gathering recently at the Hotel Fasoli
Melbourne. Thk Bulletin was very much in evidence. The
following is an unfaithful report of the proceedings:—
Hark to the bards of Tiie Bulletin,
Gathered Fasoli’s walls within.
Wine has flowed, and the pipe of peace
Gives the imprisoned thoughts release—
Talk ! How they talk when they once begin,
The bards of the red-covered Bulletin.
They yarn of the men who have gone before ;
l)aley, and Boake, and the many more
Who have passed to the Shades where all must go—
There’s a little pause—and the smoke comes slow—
But soon they’re in eager cry again
For they are living, and they are men,
And there’s work to do and there’s what’s been done,
And a voice that’s the voice of everyone
Fills the room, and above the din
Is heard the stuff that did not get in
To that blanky rag, The Bulletin.
0 brave Romance with heart of gold,
None knoweth thee and groweth old !
Strenuous, passionate, eager-eved,
They are back in the days when the world was wide,
When the moon hung low in the Summer air,
And Amaryllis was wholly fair,
And the game was afoot to lose or to win—
Who cared for Fate or The Bulletin !
Hush’d is the talk, and the glasses clink
As the liquor’s poured ; there’s a toast to drink.
The toast of the Red Rag strong and fit
That has fashioned them—as they fashioned it;
Flesh of their flesh, yet a thing apart,
Moulder of mind and the source of art—
Deep, deep they drink as the shadows thin :
Good luck to the good old Bulletin !
VICTORIA.
BARAK.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The importers don’t know what day the new
duties may arrive now, so there have been very heavy
Customs clearances during the last three weeks ; hence
a comparison of the State revenues for July with those
of other months isn’t worth much. In M. S. Wales the
total was £1,126,286, or £205,579 more than last year;
and, of that excess, £133,141 came from Commonwealth
refunds, which practically mean Customs collections.
In Victoria the total was £765,181, an increase of £122,977,
for £70,852 of which the Commonwealth refunds were
responsible ; and in Queensland, £360,347, an increase of
£61,405, refunds being £32,318 ahead of the figures for
July, 1906. Tasmania’s revenue was £70,227; Wes-
tralia’s, £190,005. In July, 1906, Westralia figured for
£200,697; and that there should have been a decline
this year, notwithstanding the special circumstance
which has made every other State’s revenue bound
upwards, is only another proof of the bad way the profli-
gate Western State is in. It is cutting down expenditure
on revenue account, certainly (in 1906 the July ex-
penditure was £211,095 and this year it is £201,910); but
the cut isn’t deep enough to prevent a deficit. And the
real state of affairs isn’t exposed because of the heavy loan
expenditure, of which no return is made till long after
the end of the financial year. Among all the States
Westralia now presents the most serious financial
problem ; and there isn’t the smallest sign that the
problem is being grappled with. There isn’t much evi-
dence, in fact, that many local politicians even realise
that there is a problem to be grappled with. The State’s
accumulated deficit on revenue account is £220,633, of
which £11,905 was added during the one month of July.
The railway revenue reflects the continued
prosperity of the three big Eastern States under the
“ blighting influence ” of Federation. In July, the
N.S.W. total was £411,686, or £54,845 more than it was
for July last year; in Victoria, £291,165, or £25,597
more; in Queensland, £149,841, or £19,393 more. In
N. S. Wales, the land “ revenue ” is also still mounting ;
it was £154,534 for the month, a spring of £20,687. But
even where that doesn’t represent the sale of assets—
which means the reduction of future true revenue from
rents —it to a considerable extent represents the instal-
ments on the sale of lands bought for subdivision with
borrowed money ; and as no profit is made out of these
transactions, the figures are little better than cross
entries. It is interest received on one side and
interest paid on the other—and the payments
are a lot more certain than the receipts. It is to a great
extent the same with the railways as extensions are
made. Therefore, though the N.S.W. revenue figures
show the extraordinary increase of £205,579, the expen-
diture jumps £182,114, and lands at £1,118,775. The
figures are really very unsatisfactory. In consequence of
the rush of importers to clear the Customs, N. S. Wales
•rot £133,141 more from the Commonwealth ; in other
words, but fbr that rush (and those g#ods can’t be im-
ported and cleared twice) the month’s revenue, allowing
for some natural expansion, would have been only about
£95,000 ahead of last year’s. Yet the expenditure was
£182,114 more on revenue account, and £45,738 more on
loan account. It is pretty evident that a general election
is coming. But it is very deplorable that the public
Treasury should be used to prove what a good fellow the
ruling political party is.

The paid-up capital, which for many years stood at
£190,000, has now been increased to £230,000, but
it does not appear from the accounts or report
whether the new capital carries dividend for the
past half-year. The liabilities (deposits, etc.) amount
to £1,176,783, so that, in spite of its large reserve
fund, the co. has still over £3 of the public’s
money in the business for every £1 of its own. The re-
cent additions to reserves have no doubt been very
tine ; but, then, the increase of deposits, etc., which are
very largely used in the risky pastoral finance business,
calls for a large provision in this way ; and it is very
doubtful whether shareholders are entitled to regard
these reserves as being there for good. In 1892-3, after
all had seemed smooth sailing and the reserves had got
up to £45,081, it was found necessary to provide for
£78,982 of bad debts ; and the business was not in those
days what it has grown to now. The Board, then, is to
be congratulated on keeping its dividend down to 10 per
cent., even though 10 per cent, absorbs less than one-
third of the apparent profits. Some idea of the rush of
the business figures is given by this comparison :
1899. 1905. 1906. 1907.
Deposits, etc. .. £477,552 £631,584 £724,732 £980,249
Other liabilities .. 305,983 120,196 106,043 196,534
Premises, plant .. 71,551 48,640 54,885 70,677
Govt, stock, cash .. 164,239 247,966 287,304 456,636
Advances, etc. .. 463,992 604,655 617,149 839,740
Other assets.. .. 309,103 130,299 171,359 189,826
The total liabilities to the public are £1,176,783 ; the
liquid assets represent about 39 per cent, of that total.
The finances appear to have been admirably managed ;
writing down of premises has been continuous ; and the
original goodwill item disappeared long ago. “Land,
premises, plant, furniture and goodwill ” cost originally
£165,035 ; they are down now for a total of £70,677.
Certainly, as the co. was formed in 1882, the original
doormat is no doubt the worse for wear now ; but even so
the writing down out of profits has been very emphatic,
and since that much money is available for other pur-
poses, the policy makes it so much easier for the co. to
pay dividends in poor times.

This energetic society is now in a big way of business, as

The debentures are practically deposit receipts, which
are limited in size by a wise Building- Societies Act. Thus
while the share capital, as shown above, is £76,291, the
borrowing power of the society is £95,941. Throughout,
the maximum borrowing power has been equal to about
125 per cent, of the capital; that is to say, the society
has been legally entitled to borrow 255. for ever £1 of its
own capital. The society offers to take money at call;
so that presumably some of the debentures are payable
on demand, and it would be some advantage if these
were distinguished from the rest. It is pretty
clear that a concern with £50,000 that may be
called for at any moment ought to have more cash in
hand than one whose deposits only fall due at known
dates. A fine feature of the accounts is the absence of a
foreclosed property item among the assets. Now and
again, of course, properties have fallen in ; but the policy
appears to have been to realise promptly, and cut the
loss ; and the losses have been small, as the size of the
amounts written off testify. Nevertheless, it is a mighty
little reserve fund to protect £145,162 of advances,
£143,912 of which are mortgages ; and until it is very
much larger shareholders are not entitled to consider
their capital as safe. The larger the deposits (practically
all in use), the bigger the risk the capital has to run.
Ten years ago £1 of capital only had to safeguard
£1 2s. 6d. of risks ; now £f has to safeguard £1 19s. of
risks. Yet the growth of reserves, to assist capital in
that matter, has been insignificant. To put up the divi-
dend, then, to 6 per cent, was very doubtful policy.

This co. furnishes very little information about its doings.
All its earnings are lumped into one item, and shown
“after providing for contingencies.” Presumably the
“ contingencies ” mean that something is put aside out of
corpus commissions, to assist profits during the realisa-
tion of the estate in respect of which these commissions
are charged. But those reserved commissions—if they
exist —are not shown. It is another case of trusting the
directors. Apparently they think that as you have to
trust them when you’re dead, you might as well trust
them when you’re alive ; yet the argument doesn’t seem
as good as it might be. The co. has no debts (apart from
a few pounds on ordinary trade accounts) ; on the other
hand, besides paid capital (£25,000) and reserves, share-
holders carry a liability of £lOO,OOO for calls, as a further
protection for clients. The business, as far as it is dis-
closed, is, as most trustee businesses are, highly profit-
able. Although profits are charged with £2OO as bonus to
staff, the net return for the half-year is at the rate of
£l4 14s. lid. percent, per annum—and this in spite of
the fact that about £40,000 of the capital and reserves is
in public securities and office premises, which are proba-
bly not yielding more than 4 per cent, all round.

apart from new ships account, it stands now at £192,640,

£8539 has been written off out of profits in the

reserve. Great changes have occurred in the matter
of the co.’s capital, and greater are being-
provided for. Hitherto 250,000 £1 shares have been
authorised. Twelvemonths ago 200,000 had been issued
and paid to 17s. 6d. Another 50,000 were then offered to
existing shareholders, and all but 2378 are now issued
and paid up to 17s. 6d. But, though £33,331 might be
got in from the unissued shares and the uncalled capital,
the co. has carried through the preliminaries authorising
the issue of another £250,000 of capital as circumstances
demand ; and as a vigorous ship-building campaign is
still in progress, more capital will, no doubt, be got in, in
spite of the surplus of about £20,000 of liquid assets. For
all this, of course, the remarkable activity in the North
Coast butter country is responsible. There is still room
for great expansion there ; at the same time, the wave
which has been sweeping that way is liable to ebb,
for a while at least. Already, though much of the
country is still new, it is found that thfe yields
are not what they were. The heavily-used land
is getting no help, and in those circumstances
the cream may soon be off it. On very similar country
in North Queensland Ah Sin has added Australian ex-
perience to the guile of Asia. The Australian is amazed
at the rent Ah Sin will pay for the five years’ lease of
such land. If it is worth £1 an acre a year for five years,
he says, what must the capital value be 1 And he proceeds
to pay fabulous sums. But Ah Sin doesn’t. Five years
is good enough for him ; somebody else may have the land
then. He knows that after five years the returns will sag
badly. If similar results follow on the N.S.VV. North
Coast there will be a reaction ; and that is one of the
possibilities which this co. has to face.

The Newcastle and Hunter River Steamship

Out of the half-year’s profits an insurance reserve is started
with £lotio, and £lOOO each is added to the repairs and
boiler replacement funds ; and as these funds are not true
reserves, but have to pay for renewing things which are
partly worn out, the profits as stated above are not
wholly true profits either. Still, apart from the additions
to these funds, the assets appear to be severely written
down out of profits. This comparison shows that, and
also some other things :
June, June, Dec., June,
1897. 1906. 1906. 1907.
Ship property .. £47,550 £40,595 £59,363 £81,480
Freeholds, etc. .. 19,172 5,308 4,122 3,580
Plant .. .. 1,651 3,069 3,503 5,492
Liquid assets .. 39,467 67,551 52,109 34,218
Debts to public .. 4,307 4,332 4,778 7,105
Note that ship property had a smaller book value in 1906
than in 1897. At the end of 1904, too, it was down for
£41,300 ; at the end of 1906, apart from new-steamers
account, the item had been reduced to £35,700. Assuming
that no steamers were sold meantime, then, in addition to
the profits shown for those three years, £5600 was made
and employed to write down the book value of the fleet.
That process and the sale of freeholds account for the
growth of liquid assets from £39,467 in 1597 to £67,551
in 1906, though nominally the reserves had only'
increased by £B6SS in those nine years. Whether
any large sums are being “ socked ” away now,
avowedly as a set-off against wear and tear, it is impos-
sible to say, because the recent purchases of new steamers
at unknown prices disturb the comparisons. The policy
of adding to the fleet is being continued ; so that, within
a year, the balance-sheet will be very unlike what it was
three years ago. Then liquid assets represented nearly
twice as much as ship property, whereas now they don’t
represent half as much. Recent experience is, therefore,
not much of a guide to this co.’s future.

This co. pays the extra 5 per cent, (which was more than
half expected six months ago) as a bonus—the directors’
non-committal way of making a larger distribution. The
table doesn’t show the full extent of the co.’s ups and
downs, especially the “ups.” For instance, the divi-
dends once reached 42J per cent. ; but that was nearly
20 years ago. Of the profit of £10,567 for the half-year,
£l5OO is not shown in profit and loss, but is added direct
to the reserve fund. That may mean that the reserve
fund is a set-off against actual depreciation. A mining
property must depreciate, in the ordinary course, as the
mineral is dragged out of it; but there are not many
mining cos. that are frank enough to admit it in this
practical way. This co. has, it is true, much plant, etc.,
to also depreciate ; and there is no sign of any profit
being used to write these assets down, apart from the
reserve. The cash position is still strong ; liquid assets
(including sundry debtors) are £63,523 in excess of liabili-
ties. Six months ago they were £60,171 in excess—before
paying dividend in each case. Five years ago(also before
paying the 35 per cent. div. then declared) they were
£90,140; but since then £15,767 has been added to the book
value of the mining property, plant, etc. Whether that
has added to the life and value of the mine t)te accounts
can’t tell. The history of the concern has been brilliant;
but the past is an unreliable guide to a mine’s future.
The time comes when it is all past and no future. All
that can be said about the Wallsend is that that time is a
long way off ; and, on its past record, and looking at the
size of its liquid assets, it isn’t likely to work out one
mine till it has acquired another. Also for 20 years, at
any rate, it hasn’t paid less than 10 per cent. ; in the very
few half-years when it has earned less, the substantial
reserves have come in handy.

The co. began with a deficit of £57,600 representing
goodwill in the old firm’s property agency. The present
co. has set itself to the tough job of clearing out this
great pile of paper, and it has reduced the item in the
books to £42,000 ; there is also undivided profit £1572,
leaving the net deficit £40,428, as shown above. As the
goodwill item is written off, liquid assets are accumu-
lated to take its place ; the surplus of these liquid assets
over liabilities now amounts to £19,924. At June, 1900,
the surplus was £9740; so that there has been an im-
provement of £10,184 in the cash position in seven years.
This does not, however, necessarily mean that the co. is
£10,184 to the good ; because, assuming that the good-
will has a value, it is possible that it is worth less—owing
to the competition of younger agencies, etc.—than it was
in 1900. There are 00,000 shares in the co. ; the surplus
of liquid assets over liabilities could pay 6s. Bd. per share,
out of which current div. has to be met. The market
value of the shares is about 14s. Bd. ; so that, on this
market valuation, Bs. per share, or in all £24,000, is now-
paid for goodwill.

It is never reassuring to find that profits work out to the
exact amount required to pay a co.’s regular dividend. It
generally means either that there is a large hole to be
filled up somewhere, and that every available penny
is being collared to write down assets, and so fill
up the orifice ; or that the true profits were not enough
to pay the dividend, and that just enough to make up
the balance has been got by starving the provision for
bad debts—in other words, by reckoning so much bad
debt as good debt. It isn’t, however, anything serious
in this case, on account of the co.’s large reserves—at the
worst, they can stand a little chip cut off them. The
fact seems to be that times are really too prosperous for
the “Monty Peet ” ; fewer want accommodation, and
those that do want it find that banks are much more
friendly than they were awhile ago. In conse-
quence of this stress of competition, whereas in
the first half of 1903 £87,244 of advances brought
in £10,239, in the past half-year £80,429 of ad-
vances brought in only £8104; yet working expenses
have, as in most establishments, gone up. Between the
two movements the 10 per cent, would be impossible but
for the big reserves ; and even with them, it is very
doubtful whether it ought to be maintained. The posi-
tion illustrates again that it’s a good wind that blows ill
to nobody.

doesn’t seem any larger than it ought to be to cover the
heavy wear and tear on the things that are used about a
quarry.

The small amount written off comes out of the reserve for
the renewal of retorts, and isn’t the best sort of writing
off. Apparently it is merely a trifle spent to replace
something that has been worn out. If anything is pro-
vided out of profits, apart from the general reserves, to
counterbalance current wear and tear, there is no
way of ascertaining what it amounts to. It seems,
however, to be the co.’s policy to write down assets out-
of undisclosed profits; for instance, the furniture item
comes down in this six months from £195 to £lB5. Pro-
bably, the same plan is adopted as to the larger items of
plant, etc. The matter could be traced if no additions
were being made to the fixed assets. This past half-year
they go up £369 ; but whether that was the full amount
of the expenditure, or whether something more was spent
and paid for out of profits but not shown as such, is un-
known. The profit and loss account merely shows the
one charge, “Manufacture, depreciation, etc., £4187,”
and the allowance for depreciation may be much or iittle.
If it is too little, then some day, when large renewals are
called for, there will have to be heavy drafts upon
either current profits or the formal reserves. But the
accounts don’t allow one to speak with any certainty at
all. In nine cases out of ten the published accounts of
Gas cos. give only vague hints as to their actual standing
and progress. Nearly all of them act as though they
couldn’t trust the public with the facts; and hiding
things from the public they have to keep the general
body of their shareholders in the dark also.

Broken Hill, hanging on to the unstable silver and lead
markets, is an erratic place. This co. very often passes
years in which no provision at all is made for current
depreciation. Then a good time conies, profits jump, and
something large has to be written off in a lump, mostly
to pay for replacements. The stated profits for any one
year have therefore to be viewed witii some suspicion ,
they are only tentative profits, waiting for a lump to
come off them to provide for the wear and tear and rust
and decay that nave happened while the alleged profits
were being earned. As far as the records avail-
able go back, the fixed investments (buildings,
mains, plant, etc.) cost £33,418, and they now stand
in the books at £27,172; and whether the difference is
sufficient to cover the depreciation it is impossible to say.

not absorbed most of the amount written off. Ihere

The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These, two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches ” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Hock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 16lbs.
and has only one working part—the hammer—which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. Ir some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2£in. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working-
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Sha w-
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne,' where
the machine may be seen working.* [c]

The reserves, with the amounts added to them during
the half-year put in brackets, are: —Reserve fund,
£21,310 (£650) ; renewals reserve, £30,975 (£650); meter
renewal, £ll5l (_228); insurance, £5OO (£25); and
undivided profit, £1207 (as against £l2lO six months
before). Some of these are not true reserves : they are a
provision to cover partly-expired risks. Still, in addition
to the provision for future renewals, current repairs and
renewals have been paid for out of revenue ; so most
likely part at least of the amount added to reserve is true
reserve—in other words, more than current decay is pro-
vided for. The financial position is strong : liquid assets
(apart from stores, etc.) exceed liabilities by £20,351,
after paying the current dividend.
The City Mutual Assurance Society (Sydney)
has issued its eighth actuarial report, and finds for the
year a surplus of £8935, of which £7503 will be divided,
or £19,867 for the three years. The premiums amounted
to £167,034 ;so the bonuses are equal to 11 - 9 per cent, of
the premiums. This is much below the rate in some of
the bigger societies; but the young society, having
smaller reserves (or accumulations of premiums), neces-
sarily earns less, in proportion to annual premiums, by
way of excess interest. The City Mutual, however, cuts
down that handicap, by assuming that it will earn 4 per
cent., whereas other offices assume as low as 2£ per
cent. It is no doubt earning much more than 4 per cent,
now ; but its high rate is due to the fact that it carries
on a small-advance business which would not be possible
on a large scale. Whether, then, it is justified in assum-
ing that a 4 per cent, rate will be earned throughout the
life of all existing policies is very doubtful.
* * * *
The Westralian Government is getting anxious
about the future of the gold industry, and is promising
Capital all sorts of concessions if it will only come along and
bring its relations. The labor covenant in leases is to be
knocked on the head, Bull Cohen is to be treated with
greater respect than formerly, and the conditions under
which mining areas may be taken up are to be as near
fee simple as it is possible to make them. The Govern-
inent may succeed in getting its foolish way in these
matters, but if it does, the day will come w'hen those who
allowed the new order of things to be brought about
without an emphatic protest will curse in a loud voice.
Present labor covenants in the West may not be neces-
sary in the case of some mining companies, in which case
they do no harm ; but they are not nearly stringent
enough in the case of others. The Hannan Star is a
gloomy example. This co. has now over £25,000 (mostly
got out of the pockets of Australian shareholders) in hand ;
yet the mine is closed down, and the plant is being
allowed to rust. If the property is no good (and strong
evidence points that way) liquidation is the proper
course to pursue. The directors, however, appear to
think differently, and will probably go on drawing fees. In
the meantime, shares, which on a forced realisation should
be worth between 2s. 6d. and Ss., are quoted at a few
pence. Yet conditions calculated to give mining com-
panies even more liberty to do this sort of thing are now
proposed.
♦ * * *
In the campaign against the gold-stealers, a
Bill providing for the licensing of treatment plants has
been drafted to go on with in Westralia ; but unless an Act
is framed making such plants liable to forfeiture on con-
viction of the owner for knowingly handling stolen stuff,
and giving the authorities power to sample the ground
from which the gold is alleged to come, prosecutions are
bound to fail. The Labor party takes its own stand in
the matter ; it threatens to oppose any increase in the
detective staff to look after the property of the foreign
capitalist till the Government provides extra inspectors
to look after the lives and limbs of the underground
workers.
* * # *
Associated (W.A.) seems to still have an
attraction for speculators out of proportion to its value.
Recent deve.opments in the bottom workings have failed
to reveal anything but of the ordinary, while at No. 7
shaft, in the north-east corner of the property, the short
but rich shoot exposed at the Nos. 1 and 2 levels has
petered out in depth. As in thecase of the Perseverance,
there is too great a discrepancy between the profit in
sight and the market valuation of this mine. AlO per
cent, return is not enough when there is barely three
years’ ore actually in sight, and only six years or there-
abouts more or less reasonably conjectured to exist so far
as the mine is proved.
* * » *
Two or three fair developments are reported
from the Boulder Main Reef (W.A.); but, coming right
on top of the announcement of another reconstruction,
they must be viewed with some suspicion. Since the
j-ich telluride shoot cut out at the sixth level, the most
strenuous efforts of successive managers have not shown
a profit. Possibly an improvement may take place
when the property is developed still deeper, but it has
been such a rank disappointment in the last 1000 ft. or so
of sinking that the odds against it seem overwhelming.
* * * *
R. E. Tyler, late chairman of the Murchison
Associated, has let the cat out. In a circular issued
recently he states (1) that since the last reconstruction
(there have been several) £50,000 has been expended
without result; (2) that the general manager condemned
the property as worthless twelve months ago ; (3) that
since then £9OOO has gone in useless prospecting ; and (4)
that more money has been spent on the property than
circumstances warranted. Yet on the plea that recon-
struction is the only chance the co. has of ever getting
its money back, shareholders are being hard pressed by
i he new chairman and his colleagues to provide more
funds. If they do, their credulity must be boundless.
* * * •
Blackboy Hill goldfield (W.A.) has been blasted
by the local inspector of mines. He reports that, although
some quartz seams embedded in the formation undoubt-
edly carry rich values, they are too small to be worked
separately by hand picking ; and when broken with the
remainder of the lodestuff the bulk is too poor to pay.
On the strength of glowing reports published in Perth, a
good deal of wild speculation had taken place in con-
nection with this field.
■* * * *
A mostly-Adelaide co. has bought the Kyloe
mine, two miles from Adaminaby, out in the snowy
regions of Kosciusko (N.S.W.). Capital is £35,000, in
10s. shares, of which 50,000 have been issued, fully paid.
The promoters appear to be the people who passed the
Lobb’s Hole on to the other people who floated it. The
only prospectus (“for private circulation”) available is
really a report by Chas. Taylor, a copper metallurgist,
who appears also to have had something to do with pro-
moting the co. He says the ore-body is proved
450 ft. long, and “ taking the ore on" the dumps
and filled-in old stopes, there is above the 100 ft.
level 8000 tons, which alone will give a net profit of
£20,000.” The width of the ore-body isn’t mentioned,
except this: “The vein consists of quartz and
small seams, impregnating the porphyry itself with ore
for several feet on each side of the vein.” The new co.
proposes to concentrate, treating 800 tons monthly, and,
taking copper at £75 per ton, it figures profit out at
£lBBO on that output. Well, if the new men can do any-
thing like that, it is a good deal more than the old
owners did. Last year’s Mines Department’s report men-
tioned that Schaefer and party, of the Kilo (the spelling
has been altered by the new co. on the Smith-Smythe
principle), raised ore which they dressed to 14 per cent.
“ During the year 105 tons were disposed of, for a return
of £9'H.” The report on which the new co. proceeds
seems to Im been written by a very opti-
mistic man. > co. has to sell its conceu-
trates ; vet, in reckoning up the revenue, he
doesn’t allow for that nice habit copper-smelters have
of knocking one unit off for a start, and in some cases
two units. Also, though the price of copper may be £75
per ton (15s. per unit), the copper smelter takes, as a
rule, from Is. 9d. to 2s. 6d. per unit off that; so that the
copper-miner who sends 10 per cent, ore gets paid for 9
units; and, though the price may be £75 per ton (15s per
unit), he gets, not 10 X 15s. = 1505., but 9 X 12s. 6d. =
112 s. 6d. Occasionally the deduction isn’t quite 2s. 6d. per
unit; but it often is. It is little things like this that in
practice spoil all the beautiful calculations of the men
who write the pretty reports about copper properties
when the copper properties have to rely upon a custom
smelter.
* * * *
N.S.W. has another div.-paying gold-mine—
the New Homeward Bound, one of the few Wyalong
mines held by public cos. The present co. has only been
in existence about eight months ; it is in 28,000 shares of
55., and is a reconstruction of the Homeward Bound,
which was making calls for equipment and development
until less than 12 months ago. This first div. of 3d. per
share conies out of the recent treatment of 221 tons of
ore for a net return (after paying smelting and railway
charges) of £3161. The mine is now provided with a good
rock-drill equipment, and looks as though it had started
on a profitable career in a small way—it must be small,
for the sulphide veins, though rich, only run a few inches
in width.
* * * *
Broken Hill has had good rains—good, that is,
for that droughty part; something under 2in. altogether.
It might have been better for it if it hadn’t had any. The
question of a second water supply is still hung up while
the interested parties quarrel over representation on the
Trust, and the present rain may result in nothing at all
being done, as usual. In the matter of water suppty
Broken Hill is about the most improvident place in Aus-
tralia, and the place that can least afford to be improvi-
dent ; it is always balancing on the rim of a famine.
It fell in once of late years, and then the Anti-Sosh
bosses of its mining cos. made their throats sore crying
out for Socialistic help.
* * * *
Block 10, Broken Hill, is wearing a cheerful
smile just now, in spite of various old sores, which must
still be smarting. Some unusually good sulphide ore is
being mined in a stope about the 600 ft. level, and the
mill has been kept going more regularly than before.
The sliming trouble is also being overcome to a great ex-
tent. The deepening of the main shaft below the
1315 ft. is in hand, but as the lode at that level was found
to be considerably narrower than at the 1215 ft. (where it
is about 50ft. from wall to wall), developments at the
1415 ft. will be watched with more than usual interest.
No other outside show along the supposed con-
tinuation of the Broken Hill main line of lode has com-
manded the faith and spare coin of the working miner
as the Globe has done. Nevertheless, for the present its
star is dimmed. The old manager has resigned, having
failed to find the alleged large bodies of ore left un-
touched by the pioneers ; and new schemes are afoot.
Also a story is afoot of a surreptitious visit to the mine
which nearly ended disastrously for an active Adelaide
mining speculator. Under cover of darkness, two inter-
ested mining men and this speculator, accompanied by
the engine-driver from another small mine, visited the
Globe when all was quiet to make a secret inspection.
The engine-driver lowered the three others to the bottom
level. But the water had risen above the plat, and none
of the party knew it till the cage dropped into the unex-
pected bath, and half-drowned the lot of them. Then
the driver tried to pull them to the upper level, but the
engine got “ centred,” with the cage stuck midway in
the shaft. Shivering with cold and fright, the three
explorers had to clamber out of the cage and claw a
passage to the surface by the ladder way."
* • • *
North British Blocks, Broken Hill, have been
given a fair show to produce the much-talked-of lode ;
but they haven’t done so yet. Some pipes of ore have
been met with, and there has been a good deal of talk
about “ raising sulphides for shipment.” But so far as
development has gone, the country is too tumbled to
harbor any extensive and continuous shoots of ore. The
management is still pegging away, however, in the hope
that something may turn up.
* * * *
The prospectus of the Brookman’s United Gr.M.
Go. was issued in Sydney last week. The provisional
directors are Abrahams, and Isaacs, and Jacobs. Esau
seems to be still under a cloud, and no good for prospec
tus purposes, and Ephraim and Manasseh are apparently
still sticking to sheep.
* * * *
“Follower” :Be Mount David (N.S.W.). No
wonder the public jumped out at the first chance. In
1903 the co. paid a div., much against the desire of the
manager, the late John Pearce, and in a few weeks a call
was made. The finance is a mystery. Nobody seems to
know what rent the co. pays. A shareholder was in-
formed (at the last meeting, I think) that a director had
a mortgage over the assets at 10 per cent, interest; yet
at the same time a call was made before it was needed to
buy a cyanide plant. The tramway has not justified
itself, the underlay shaft has been a ghastly
failure, and the promises as to costs have been
unfulfilled—the last return works out on the wrong
side of 30s. After saying that, it is some plea-
sure to add that the cyanide plant is good, and a first-
class extraction is made ; that the battery plant is also
satisfactory, and that the drive at the lowest level, where
the good stuff for the last crushing came from, is in
more settled country ; and the reef looks like going down.
More men are being put on as they are obtainable. As
showing the change which has occurred, in 1904 the
battery treated 12,578 tons for 23760 z., or an average of
3dwt. 18’6gr. ; in 1905 the average for 11,611 tons was
2dwt. 15'9gr. ; for 1906, 2669 tons averaged 4dwt. 14’5gr. ;
while the last crushing showed 2640 z. from 300 tons, or
17dwt. 14gr. per ton.
* * * *
J. V. Bartlett, “king” of Yerranderie, is get-
ting in suction-gas engines for general mine purposes—to
work drills, rock-breakers, and concentrating mill, etc.
This will be the first big suction-gas mine plant in
N. S. Wales, and the results of the test, in the matter of
fuel economy, will be watched with very great interest.
■* * * »
In order to give Chillagoe shareholders some
idea of the advantages of the Etheridge railway con-
cession, chairman J. S. Reid shows what the Govern-
ment would have to pay for the Chillagoe line if
resumption of that were provided for on the same scale.
The Chillagoe line cost £381,902; but, taking last
year’s profit (£61,153) as a basis, the cost of resump-
tion under the terms of the Etheridge Railway Act would
be £1,064,563, The Etheridge line is not likely to make
such large profits as the Chillagoe line is doing at pre-
sent, but the example serves to show what a slippery
business these time bargains are. Still there is nothing
permanent about a sudden boom of prosperity over the
Chillagoe line : looking backwards over the whole period
since the line was built, the average returns are not
nearly so wonderful as they seem on top of a metal and
mining boom.
* * * *
The first crushing from the old Durham (N.
Queensland), reopened after a slumber of 15 years, re-
sulted in £462 from 200 tons—concentrates and sands to
come. Whatever happens, it is safe betting that the
officials under the present co. will not have the glorious
time that prevailed when J. Bull held the property, and
lashed the cash so splendidly on the surface, many years
ago. The change, however, won’t do the mine any harm.
The gaudiness of J. Bull’s imported officials has wrecked
better mines than the Durham.
* * * »
From Irvinebank (N.Q.) : Tin mining around
Reid’s Creek is sick, owing to the unsatisfactory develop-
ments in Dalziell’s Boulder. Although a good body of
tin stone showed on the surface, prospecting, so far, has
proved only a narrow and erratic pipe ; and results have
coiqe up to about half the prediction? as to output and
returns. One mine that should have a chance is the Nil
Desperandum, owned by the Globe (Townsville) Syndi-
cate. The ore body is chlorite, and looks permanent, but
the “ one manager to two men ” system is followed. The
Village Blacksmith, lately on the market, is another
greisen body, the tin lying in floors. The deposits are
rich, but, as far as the old workings show, very limited.
As for the Boulder No. 2, included in the float, the tin-
bearing stone is probably only a thin flake. The diamond
drill is working in the Vulcan, and has finished one
lateral 150 ft. hole, and is in over 100 ft. in another.
Nothing of any value has been found. Last month’s out-
put totalled nearly £6OOO worth of tin, but only one team
is carting. A contract has been called for sinking the
main shaft another 100 ft., making the total 1250 ft.
“ Ikona,” from Cloncurry : “ Quartpot ” says
that less ore is going from this field now than formerly.
Natural enough, in view of the nearing completion of the
raihva3 7 ; men don’t want to pay £6 a ton to cart to Julia
Creek, the present head of the line, making total charges
from £l2 to £l4 per ton. From a prospector’s point of
view, the wholesale exemptions which are granted are the
curse of the field. “Business ” people form syndicates,
acquire large areas, and hold them for speculative pur-
poses. Many instances could be quoted where leases
have been held for three years, and no work done on them.
Most of the lodes pinch out at from 10ft. to 50ft. There-
fore, these “ prospectors ” say, “What! Break the reef
and spoil the mine!” These “squibs,” as they are
locally termed, are at times very rich, and would provide
remunerative employment for hundreds of workingmen
if the Government would cause the Mining Regulations
to be reasonably administered. Either make these
“prospectors” work or forfeit. Fancy, 1100 leases
pegged, and the principal (in fact, the only) town re-
sembling a teamsters’camp ! Taking a radius of 80 miles,
I feel sure the entire population does not exceed 500
souls, or half a soul to the lease. It is scandalous. From
a miner’s point of view, Cloncurry district is fine-
looking country, though somewhat difficult to prospect.
It is exceedingly rough. In many instances the ore
would have to be packed off the rangt s to where a dray
could reach it. It is very poorly timbered and, at pre-
sent, badly watered. Looking into the future, when
better facilities of transport are provided, or when smelters
are erected locally, it should come to the front, especially
as a home for the small working mine-owner. But before
that day can arrive, these “prospectors” who don’t
prospect must be hanged, drowned, or dynamited. For
myself, 1 don’t care if it is all three.
* * * *
John Bull, during 1906, put £104,000 under-
ground in Victoria in quartz-mining, and £144,300 in
deep-lead mining, and got only one div. to keep up his
enthusiasm, and that was from Victorian Cornish, Dayles-
ford. Quartz shows in which he is interested are Vic-
toria Prop. (Bendigo), Forest Creek Gold Reefs (Castle -
maine), Daylesford Mines and Victorian Cornish
(Daylesford), South No. 1 (Gaffney’s Creek), and Eagle-
hawk Consolidated (Maldon). Of these the Victorian
Cornish took £45,000, and led to no small amount of
tough talk in London, because after a few good yields
and high share values came bad times and a slump.
The deep-lead show’s comprise the six mines
in Bewick, Moreing and Co.’s office, and the Charlotte
Plains, under Drvsdale Brown. To the list could be
added the Junction Deep Leads and a few others, partly
held in England. Certain it is that Bull picks up very
few odd pence on the south-eastern slope of Australia,
whatever he may do in the West.
* * * *
Burnt Creek, Maryborough (Vic.), has spelt
burnt fingers for so long that the public smiled when
four headlines were bumped out in the Age' telling that a ,
prosperous future was ahead. As this appeared in a
paper that had just been reviling London journals for
doing the same sort of thing, a smile as wide as Collins-
street lit up 8., M. and Co.’s staff. Burnt Greek, so far,
hasn’t gone over 10dwt., but it has been a model of
cheapness and hard work ; and the manager has now just
touched wash which he says prospects' 14dwt. to 16dwt.
After the many disappointments over deep-lead “pro-
spects,” it was hoped that no more would be heard about
such things for a while. Still as the public is not “on
the feed,” the announcement won’t make much difference.

New men, new manners. " Victor’s Quartz,
Wood’s Point (Vic.), has been put under a Bendigo
manager. His backers already sing louder about the
future than Ada Ward does about her past. The claim
was under a cloud till the tributors got it, and it has been
in the dark ever since they let it go. Now the gentle
prophet is tooting the loud trumpet to the effect that the
co. will get on to rich stone in a short time ; but this
paper doesn’t believe that even a prophet can see far into
a diorite dyke. It is much safer, then, to wait for yields.
» * » *
“ Stone has been struck in the Florence mine,
Daylesford (Vic.). It is reported to be the healthiest
seen in the mine.” For this, oh Ballarat, we thank thee !
True, the remark is only first cousin to that other beauti-
ful but vague mining observation—“ The lode goes strong
underfoot”—which is twin sister to the other indefinite
one that “ The stone in the face looks kindly.’.’
has been a sad jade, and needed something stimulating
to help her along, both in the mining and the market
way. And now she’s got “healthy” stone. Well, it’s
some satisfaction to find something that has escaped
both the ’flu’ and whooping cough.
* * * *
Poseidon rush (Vic.) is a rush no longer. It
has got down to 50ft. ground, and to a little trickle
of diggers the wrong way—that is, to other fields.
The odd part about the field is that all the best
gold, particularly big nuggets, was got only’ a
few feet from the surface. The hope to-day is that
Mackenzie and mate, who have the claim crossing the
Woolshed reef, will get on to good gold in the deep
ground. Should this happen, there may be a bit of a
flutter. But it cannot last. Poseidon to-day is in the
stable after his race, and to all appearance is a tired
hero, incapable in the future of anything more than a
short sprint.
* * » *
The deep lead at the Glenfine South, Pitfield
(Vic.), was heralded with fanfares, and between times
stories were told of half ounce prospects to the dish. One
or two divs. were paid, and then calls were made, and
now there is a solid overdraft. It isn’t much use having
rich prospects if they don’t crystallise in the puddling
machine. Lately the mine has been mainly a matter
of “prospects,” for following the gutter came the
quartz, over which the market had a fall, the
worst known at Ballarat for many a day till the
Ajax came along. There must be some reputations
to mend over that “ go,” and it is a pity that the share-
holders don’t have them out for purposes of identifica-
tion. At present Glenfine South is worth the battery and
plant as scrap machinery, and it will need a lot of solid
development—without the stereotyped “prospects”—to
get it on to the next stage.
* * * *
Mackay’s Quartz is the newest dividend-payer
in the Gaffney’s Creek district (Vic.). The show was
found about'two years ago by the prospector for a local
syndicate. A shaft was put down on the reef and a couple
of 3oz. crushings were obtained. With this money a
lower tunnel was driven and a battery bought ; but it
was found that the reef had pinched and become poor.
Latterly, however, another run of loz.. stone has been
picked up in the tunnel, and with returns of about 1950 z.
per month the little co. has got to its first div. A deeper
shaft is now in prospect.
* *
Welsh and Slater, Rocky Lead, Ballarat dis-
trict, seems to be “ up against it,” which is bad business
for the scrippers who were guided by the enthusiastic
speeches made at the inauguration of the co. a few
months back. Developments at 100 ft., bottom level,
have not been satisfactory ; and, higher up, the lode has
pinched considerably. Shares have been squeezed down
to a few pence, and a good many have changed hands.
Those in the know want paid-ups, because at the worst
the sale of the machinery, etc., will recoup them.

A shaft sunk 25ft. in the Ballarat graveyard
showed that the golden stone shrieked about lately was
merely a surface patch. The syndicate formed to work
the Old Cemetery lease disbanded on the first loss of £2
10s. each.
* * * #
New Oorinella, Eganstown, near Daylesford
(Vic.), about which fabulous tales were recently told, is
already in trouble. Calls have not been well responded
to, and work has been suspended. The rich shoot at the
bottom level extended for about 40ft., and was then lost.
Sinking will be resumed “ when confidence is restored.”
But confidence is a shy bird in the Daylesford district
just now, and you have to take a very long shot at him.
An agent has been rounding up a big party of
Victorian alluvial miners at £3OO a year and quarters to
work in the Tronoh mine, Uintah Valley, Perak, Malay
States. This mine, owned mostly by Chinese, employs
about 4000 coolies, and produces 200 tons of tin oxide per
month, averaging 74 per cent, of metallic tin. It paid
66 per cent, on the par value of shares last year. The
Australians are to boss the coolies.'

Of the 52 members of the S.A. Assembly
of 1890, only seven are still in politics. Coles
(Speaker and Father of the House), Larry
O'Loughlin (Crown Lands),, Burgoyne (octo-
genarian, leader of the Country Party and sound
Democrat), and McDonald and Solomon (fossils)
remain in the Assembly. John Downer and
Jamie Howe are ghosts in the House of Political
Death, and drift about at nights, each with his
tombstone under his arm and his coffin on his
head, lamenting at the progress things hare made
since they died.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“ Tantanoola ” : At the entrance to Beach-
port Harbor (S.A.) stands Penguin Island, on
which the lighthouse is built. The earthquake
shocks that occurred on May 10, 1897, caused the
little isle to split in two; a wide chasm now
sunders what was once a solid mass of rock, and
20ft. of water flows between the parts. On the
portion nearest the mainland two falls of rock
took place lately, and several hundred tons
dropped on the reef below. The island is sand-
stone, and in many places the water has eaten
large, submarine holes in the formation. It is
the opinion of many that after the next earthquake
Penguin Island will be no more. What worries
the lighthouse people is that they will also, in all
probability, be no more, and they reckon that the
salary isn’t at all commensurate with the shakiness
of the rock. The salary is fixed, certainly, which
the island isn’t; but they reckon that it is
fixed too low. On the other hand, the Govern-
ment points out that if a thing is low its centre of
gravity is more secure in a time of earth tremor ;
but the keepers reckon that if the lighthouse was
lower and the salary higher it would meet the
case. The lighthouse wouldn’t have so far to
drop, and as for the salary, they don’t care how
far it has to drop so long as it doesn’t drop in their
time.

“ The Bandicoot ” : Those shell deposits
above high-water mark again. “Rhaupo” (B.
(25/7/ 07) gives the age (indirectly) of these de-
posits as 12 years, and he blames a grub or a
disease. (He hues his doots, as it were.) He also
drags tides, currents, and trawlers’ nets in by the
scruff of the neck, and makes them carry the
shell from Port Stephens to the Hawkesbury
River, and thence, in some instances, 50ft. above
high-water mark. Then his grub spread the shells
neatly several feet deep for hundreds of yards
along the shores, and plants elderly gum-trees in
the deposit. Verily “Rhaupo’s” views re shell
deposits are shrouded in the veil of mystery and
wrapped in the impenetrable blanket of gloom.
* » * «
“D.H.It.” : “Phoenix” is right (B.
18/7/’O7) re the matter of arresting the wandering
ophidian by depositing head-gear in front of him.
Not only does the proceeding give time to
find a sapling, but it is interesting and amusing
to the snake, which will lie still and regard that
gaping hat in silent wonderment for half a day.
At any rate, I once knew a brown snake that
stared at mine for three hours, and it would
probably have gone on staring had not a
young gum tree unfortunately fallen across it.
I have heard of many other cases also. Curiously
enough, the snake never attacks the hat. Whether
the snake, if left alone, will die of old age still
looking at the hat I cannot tell.
* * * *
“ Barb ” : I always had an idea that
children in N.S.W. were obliged to attend school
until they were 14, or, at any rate, until they had
reached a certain standard. But a family out this
way has managed to dodge the inspecting bodies,
whoever or whatever they are. They—l say they
advisedly—deliver our mail. A couple of years
back a small atom under an extraordinary big
hat was the individual who brought our letters.
With a microscope you might have found him in
the sulky when the mailbags were aboard. I got
to know him well, and I knew his hat better. But
one day there seemed to be a change in him—he
seemed to have shrunk. “ Don’t you feel well,
Johnny?” I asked, sympathetically. “I ain’t
Johnny; I’m Tommy,” the mite informed me.
“Good gracious! are you?” 1 exclaimed, in
amazement. “Then where’s Johnny ?” “’E’s
too big to drive the mail now. ’E’s work in’
at the mine.” Tommy went on driving for some
time. He got no bigger—only older. His small,
freckled face was hidden under Johnny’s dear
old hat, and one still required the microscope.
Tommy had become my very good friend, and
one day I went to meet him as usual. “ Cold drive
out, Tommy?” I inquired, genially. “I’m
Billy,” the two-foot-nothing of humanity informed
me. “Are you, really ? Then, where’s Tommy ?
Sick ? ” “No ; ’e’s too big to drive the mails now.
’E’s fencin’.” I swallowed my amazement, and,
noticing joyfully that with the mails Billy had
taken on Johnny’s and Tommy’s tremendous hat,
felt him to be my friend. Billy tells me that
Frankie will be driving the mails soon—he is
quite six, and Billy thinks of getting a bit of
scrub clearing. A more honest and manly set of
pigmies than this amazing family never lived, and,
in spite of lack of schooling, they are omnivorous
readers.

“L. Crade ” : The enthusiasm of Tom
Mann’s revolutionists is sometimes overdone.
Last Sunday morning, at about 12 o’clock, I
strolled past the Port Melbourne Pier. On
one side of it was a bunch of about 10 propa-
gandists, one of whom, mounted on a box, was
violently haranguing the audience on the
other side. The audience was not large,
nor was it enthusiastic. It consisted of
one man, who sat stolidly smoking,
quite unmoved by the earnestness
of the speaker. I leaned against
a post and looked on. Four
speakers in succession mount-
ed the box, and each uttered
long strings of ungramma-
tical sentences without
making any impression on
the audience. Each urged
him passionately to rend
his chains and throw off
his shackles—and he
smoked stolidly. But
when thefourth man,
after a long diatribe
on things in general,
told the audience to
consider himself a
mere hound if he didn’t
rise in a body against
the tyrants who crushed
him under foot, I decided
to go over and give him my moral support. “ Are
you enjoying yourself ? ” I inquired. He looked
at me in a puzzled sortof way and said, “Plait-il ?”
He was a French sailor.

“Prospector Bill” : “ Carrandotta ”
Frayne, whose 30 years’ moving over and under
the surface of Australia have made him known in
almost every mining camp in the country, drifted
for a spell into Adelaide recently, after delving
for eight years in the Northern Territory. From
a mining viewpoint he described the Territory
this way :
I left the Territory because I was prospecting for
Chinese, and I object. I don’t mind opening up new
country for my fellow-countrymen. To find a new patch
in new country where hundreds of our own race can
follow on and share the good luck is, in my opinion, one
of the best uses to which a man can devote his life ; but
when you are trying the value of your latest discovery
with a pair of dry-blowing dishes, and rub the dust out
of your eyes to see a string of jabbering, basket-
bearing Chinamen, headed by a nigger tracker,
bearing down on you, it’s time to quit the busi-
ness in these parts. That’s what happened to me,
and that’s why I am not prospecting in the Northern
Territory now.' lam keeping my knowledge of the good
gold up there in the hope that the Commonwealth
Government may, later on, be able to give the white
prospector a show. What the value of the Territory as a
gold-bearing country is you may guess from the fact that
for eight years I have lived wholly upon alluvial gold I
found while travelling about in it. Only a little while
ago I brought in an Boz. Slug from country never tried ;
but, if I showed the gold, I didn’t dare to disclose the
locality, for I knew a swarm of Chinese were just waiting
to rush it. But the true value of the Northern Territory
lies in its reefs and lodes of gold and other minerals.
The alluvial is merely proof of the existence of gold in
reefs over an enormous area. Unless some steps are
taken to induce the prospector to open them by giving
him the right to peg, hold, and work all unpegged
ground, the Territory will continue to be a vast, unuti-
lised blank space, a danger to Australia, and an earthly
paradise for a horde of Chinamen.

“Shearman”: The other day a bit of
land (three acres of kitchen garden) near Ballarat
(Vic.) was offered for lease on the tender system,
which is brother to the other curse described as
“ state salary expected.” It came into the mar-
ket because one of the pair of gaunt Chinamen
who formerly leased it had died, and the other
yellow bag of bones had been consigned to the
benevolent asylum. The highest offer sent in by
any white competitor was £2 10s. per acre per
annum, which seems liberal enough in all con-
science ; but the offer staggered the owner—-the
two vanished Chows, it seemed, had paid £6 per
acre. Then people began to have an inkling as
to what the dead Chow died of. That owner
reckons that White Australia is a fool of a policy,
and many other landowners reckon the same
thing for the same sort of reason.

“ Knuckler,” from Maoriland : I write
seeking the assistance of your Financial Editor.
I had occasion to dismiss our Maori lady help for
wandering off the premises in search of dried
shark —she invariably brought back the smell. I
sent her a cheque for 17 weeks at 10s. per week,
and received this letter
Kia Mr. Dash,—-Your counting which you was sent to me.
I say that you and it are mistaken, and I say that 1 work
there for 17 weeks, and in my count I say that I work for
six days in a week—Sundays including—Because I don’t
stay away on Sundays. So then I told you now. lam
25s coming back. What you doing about it?—Yours
tr U ly, Hinemoa Waitangiete.
I can’t make this out at all. Is the 255. to
be carried to reserve fund, or is i't a sum
written off for the depreciation of the lady, or is
it gross profit (the lady is very gross, weighing
16 stone, and there is a prophet among her
relatives)? When she arrives, should I throw
her out, or should I declare a dividend ? If I
floated her (she floats best in beer) Would you
take up any preference shares? Would you re-
commend me to reconstruct the 255. as a deposit
at 3h per cent., payable in 1917, or to treat the
lady with silent contempt, or to turn it into
debentures? \Vou 1d it be best to make a fresh
issue of shares, or to let the dog issuefrom the
front gate at top speed when Mrs. Hinemoa
appears on the horizon ? Do you think it is worth
sinking any deeper into the subject, or would you
drive (to the police station), or would you stope
out when the infuriated female comes, and leave
some one else to deal with her? It seems to me
the expenses are high ; 255. for 16 stone is at the
rate of £l2 10s. per ton, and I don’t think the
reef will pay at that rate. As the recent frost
destroyed my cyanide plant just as it was begin-
ning to blossom, I suppose I had better charge the
huge female to petty cash, though if I could time
things so as to meet her just at the top of the
hill as she comes back, I think a good push would
convert her into rolling stock. In that case, of
course, she would be an asset, but she would be
so angry before she struck the bottom of the
valley that goodwill would have to be written off.
THE STRANDED TOPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There rings a question through his brain
VVhen hearts are hard and purses low,
When Drought is fierce on hill and plain,
And prospects black and blacker grow—
Where are the lads who formed a row
When pots were full and hearts were gay ?
When eyes could flash and hearts could glow,
Where are the Beers of Yesterday ?
Where are the jests that rose amain?
Where are the songs whose genial flow
Could drown the dirge of Autumn rain,
Or warm the chill of Winter’s snow ?
Where’s Hebe, too—who long ago
Dispensed the dews to mortal clay,
And fooled the smiling toper so?
Where are the Beers of Yesterday ?
The miser counts his loss and gain,
And measures thus his weal and woe ;
The lover’s hopes—they wax and wane ;
The merchant’s fortunes come and go ;
But deeper grief no man may know
Than he who weeps beside the way,
And asks in vain of friend and foe :
Where are the Beers of Yesterday?
Envoi.
Friends, of your kindness pity show —
One cup would cheer the toper gray ;
Ye feel his bitter need, I trow :
Where are his Beers of Yesterday?
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
SAINTLY SIMON’S WILD OATS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. Simon was a saint,
He’d held his passions in restraint
Until they one and all were cowed,
And he, of their subjection proud,
Made boast: “ Behold me ! Now so strong,
Can you believe I once ‘ went wrong ’ ?
“ You ask how this thing came to pass?
’Twas thus : Our Sabb.ith Bible class
Had been dismissed, and at the gate
We stood in knots, the hour was late
(Maybe a quarter after eight).
Yet thus vve stood as I have said,
Instead of going home to bed.
“ And there was one amongst us there
Who was a thorough deit-may-care.
He’d never learn his Sunday text,
And, oh, it often made me vexed
To think —the thought was worse than gall—
He’d failed to look it up at all.
“ I’d taxed him time and time again
With his neglect, but all in vain ;
Yet still I’d hopes I might redeem
This youth—he’d sometimes sorry seem.
But on the night I write about
I rather think I put him out.
“ He called me 'Saintly Sim,’ and said
I ought to go and ‘ bag my head ’ ; ■
He next observed he had a mind
To kick my something blank—be kind
And spare a repetition crude
Of words both boisterous and rude.
“He then removed his Sunday coat,
Rolled up his Sunday sleeves and smote
The circumambient atmosphere,
And said : ‘ You canting cow ; I’m here
To make you sick ; you must allow
You’ve often sickened me, you cow 1
“ ‘ I fancy I could flatten you
With just an upper-cut or two,
You cow ! ’ (again he called me that)
And cried, as on the ground he spat,
‘ Come on ! You make me tired I’m set;
So fight, ox—smoke a cigarette !’
“ Ah, never shall I quite forget
The night I smoked a cigarette !
Alas, dear friends, in very truth,
I was a harum-scarum youth,
When I—God help me !—was not more
Than twenty-three or twenty-four 1”

“Jimmie Pannikin ” : Yass (N.S.W.) dis-
trict is experiencing one of the coldest winters
known for years. It’s common to see smoke-rings
frozen in mid-air. I could not understand how
these ice-rings managed to remain suspended,
till a nature-student informed me that even the
laws of gravitation were frozen and the Ten Com-
mandments had stopped running, for the first time
on record.

“F.H.R.”: There was only one concer-
tina player at the party Ryan gave when Kate
was “spliced,” and that one wanted a dance.
Several of us boys could just manage to knock out
a tune, so I “ showed willin’ ” with a bar or two
of the “ Billy Boils Over,” which sufficed them to
scramble through the first figure of the lancers.
All through the ordeal I had been trying to think
of that other tune I used to play, and I had it in
my head, when —slap went the M.C.’s hands, and
away the memory vanished. Both girls and boys,
to say nothing of Ryan, whistled tunes to me in
the hope of calling it back again ; but it was gone
like the Lost Chord. Kate was dancing with her
newly-wed husband, and just then she remem-
bered he could play the “ Wearin’ o’ the Green ” ;
so, much to my enjoyment and Jim’s displeasure,
he swapped the missus for the concertina. Jim
managed two whole figures when his repertoire
ran out, and, although Ryan tried to whistle
something by way of filling the void, the musician
himself had to play the fourth figure while Jim
danced with his partner. Just then I thought of
my remaining tune, “ Where Did You Get That
Hat?” so let the musician finish the last figure
with the bride. For an all-round mixture of
music it took the cake, and the bride had only
three partners in one set of lancers.
