“ Walilia ” : How’s this for cosmopoli-
tanism ? Here in Papua, although the total
population, other than the natives of the soil, only
counts some 600 odd, we have a fairly representa-
tive Jot .of the nations of the globe. If Australia
is a separate nation, its representatives have the
top position for number. Next to them are
Englishmen; then come Germans, Frenchmen,
Scandinavians, Italians, Greeks, Yankees, Bel-
gians, Spanish, Portuguese, a few South
Americans, American negroes, Japs, Javanese,
Chinamen of the 41 welly ni’ flesh callot ” persua-
sion, Filipinos, Malays, Kanakas, and South Sea
Islanders of sorts. As the majority of the colored
immigrants have married with the natives, the
resulting mixture will be interesting, and will
give the Government much victuals for reflection.
The bulk of the copra and fishing industries have
been collared by the colored strangers, and the
sooner they are hunted out of the Possession the
better it will be for both the white and native
population.

“ Lonx ” : Referring to length of Queens-
land crocodiles (“Backwash,” B. 27/6/’O7, and
“Flinderino,” B. 18/7/ 07). The North Queens-
land gavial, named by some naturalists the John-
stone gavial, is locally known as the “crocodile.”
It is from 6ft. to Bft. long, and inoffensive so far as
man is concerned. It infests the upper waters of
N.Q. rivers; while the real crocodile, found in the
tidal and lower parts of the Johnstone and other
rivers, is miscalled the “alligator,” and grows to
20ft. long. The crocodile ranges much farther
south than the gavial.

A cry from the tail-end of N.S.W. :
The one time glory of Silverton has indeed departed.
The town which once boasted of its 10,000 inhabitants,
now has a population of 40 or 50, and the majority of
these are too old to leave and too tired to die. About
the only buildings left intact are the gaol, Courthouse,
Council Chamber, and one pub. All the wood and iron
habitations have been removed to Broken Hill, and those
of stone have been denuded of roofs, doors, windows, and
everything else movable. That once famous hostelry,
De Baun’s, where sixty dozen of fizz were once demolished
in an hour, has been razed to the ground, and right op-
posite stands the grinning skeleton of that one-time
busiest of banks—the Bank of South Australia, now the
habitation of goats. The main thoroughfare, over which
the spanking four-in-hands of W. P. McGregor, Willie
Wilson, Reg Pell, and other heroes of the roaring days,
were wont to prance, is seldom disturbed, except by a
Chow’s vegetable cart wending its way to Broken Hill.
The town still boasts a mayor, but no councillors, and
no funds. The Courthouse opens, on an average, about
twice a year, when the Broken Hill S.M. sits on a case,
usually that of a wood-carter working without a license.
In spite of Silverton’s decay, the oldest inhabitant still
fondly dreams of the restoration of the place to its for-
mer glory, through the discovery of the huge Plying
Dutchman of a silver lode, which legend declares to be
hidden thereabouts, though all the legend on earth
seems unable to locate it.
“Ginghi”: Phoenix (B. 18/7/’O7) says
that the “hat” method of stopping a snake
applies principally to the carpet snake. But
surely no Australian native would run the risk of
sunstroke, by leaving his hat to watch a carpet
snake whilst he looked for a stick to kill the harm-
less reptile. Most bush people keep a stray
carpet snake or two about the place for the same
purpose as city folks keep cats; whilst the ordi-
nary bush bloke treats them with the same amount
of contempt that Alfred Deakin should show
towards Joe Carruthers over the Federal capital
site.

“ Janor ” on snakes ;
“Paul Oupid’s ” advice to those about to be snake-
bitten (B. 20/6/’O7) is right on top. He says “ You must
catch his eye.” To make the catch he advises “ get
straight in front ” of the snake. Not at all. Get
crookedly behind it, seize it roughly but gently by the
ears, and at arm’s length elevate it to the level of your
own eye. Then, and then only, can you see the eye of a
snake. When a snake is hustling over the landscape its
eye is not visible to a man in an upright position. It is
deeply sunken and heavily lidded. If it were not so,
snakes would be blind at an early age. As they canter
over stones and through undergrowth the head acts as
does the cowcatcher of a loco. The faster the pace the
nearer the surface glides the head. Now, having caught
the eye of your snake, you will find that it is not a
“ beady optic,’’ as “ Paul 0.” affirms, but that its exterior
presents the Asiatic slant. Within it you will notice (if
you keep calm) a diabolic gleam, even as of the port side-
light of Hell. However, having caught the ophidian
eye, place the reptile on the ground and proceed to
hypnotise it, and you will find that it will light
straight out for its camp. A snake always does
when it’s mesmerised or irritated. Saw a curious
instance of this devotion to the home last summer.
Riding along the Dargo (Vic.) road, I spotted thereon a
large copper-head, which retired into a roadside log
before I could argue with it. I set Are to the log, and had
a smoke while the Are got fairly going. Turning to re-
mount, saw another copperhead travelling towards me at
a great bat. Grabbed at it as it Aashed by, and missed,
and it darted into the burning log. Returning next day,
I found the charred remains of the two snakes. “P.
Oupid’s ” incident of the hat is remarkable, for the reason
that nothing (barring a human) alarms a snake so much
as an unfamiliar object that moves when he comes in
contact with it, and he scoots directly. That is the
reason why dwellers in snake-infested localities are safer
from intrusion in tents than in solid structures. Have
killed scores of the brutes in houses and huts, but nary a
one in a tent.
“Dux” : The fellow who does the wrong
thing at the psychological moment crops up in
every game, but when Maoris take the football
field all ordinary rules suspend themselves. Such
an event occurred lately in Wanganui (M.L.),
where sculler Webb hails from. The local cham-
pions engaged the Kaierau natives at a willing
exposition of the Rugby game. Skin and hair,
hoarse shouts and sickening thuds filled the
atmosphere, and Kaierau scored and goaled.
Wanganui then hurled itself into the disturbance
and bullocked things into native territory. Mat-
ters grew exciting. Solid pakehas bustled into
the fray with great violence. Wild-eyed, dis-
hevelled and frenzied Maoris rushed here and
there like raving tom-cats. A score seemed
imminent. A Maori back, Takarangi, rushed
into the melee, secured the leather, and drop-
kicked wildly for the touch-line. The ball screwed
and went back over his own goal-posts. The
referee promptly awarded Wanganui four points
for a potted goal, and the whistle pierced the
bruised and shattered atmosphere. The Maoris
won by five to four, but they had kicked the
whole nine points, and made their opponents a
present of nearly half of them.
A RAILWAY SURVEY.
The adjourned debate on the motion for the second
reading-of the Kalgoorlie (W.A.) to Port Augusta (S.A.)
Railway Survey Bill was resumed. Senator Mulcahy
(Tas.) stated that there were three reasons why Tas-
mania was opposed to the measure. The first was that
there was no moral responsibility on her part to support
it; the second was her unfortunate financial position ;
and the third was that no benefit would accrue to the
State, owing to her geographical position.— Melbourne
Herald.
Will it benefit Augusta Road, or Don, or Mangalore ?
Will Chudleigh Junction be inclined to praise it more and
more?
Then why disturb a States-man from his Stabe-ly sleep just
for
The notion that a nation needs a railway ?
Will it straightway make a profit, and will Sandfly get a
share ?
Will Little Oyster Cove, d’you think, approve of the
affair ?
And say, O say, can Rumney’s Huts the mere idea bear
That a nation ought to have a nation’s railway?
O rash indeed this scheme appears, for if it goes ahead
Some State may profit tuppence on the State to which
I’m wed :
Such ugly possibilities must e’er surround with dread
The notion that a nation needs a railway.
Thus ever some State person longs to make his little row,
And to show he hasn’t realised we’re all Australians now ;
To me it seems quite time to say “ We’re ‘ sorry for the
cow ’
That blocks the train upon the nation’s railway.”

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted , whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. tv ill be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

But away in dear old England, my own sweet native land,
To every 48,000 souls, to legislate, one man.
While over here in Germany one member’s got to mind

But the unoffending Australian in this our Continent
Has one member to every 8000 for us to legislate.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The chances of the game : Paddy Slavin is
now one of the Fat Men of Alaska (B. 11/7/’07); John
L. Sullivan has just interviewed a Cardinal in
Rome, and been blessed by him (current American
papers); and old JemMace was, at latest accounts,
taking the twopences at the door of a low -down
boxing booth in London.
Private astronomer John Tebbutt, of
N.S.W., writes that he is now too old to go out in
the early morning looking for comets. The vener-
able scientist is in his 74th year. He was born
in Windsor, and has lived and hunted for new
heavenly bodies there all his years, despite many
offers of appointments to astronomical offices else-
where. Also he collects many rents thereabouts.
Windsor is another of the dead towns that are
strangled by big landlordism.
Georges Danysz, the rabbifc-slayer’s son,
reported as having died of fever in Brittany, was
in Sydney with his father, and proved a modest,
dreamy, artistically-disposed youth. He drifted
a good deal into society, and hostesses, in their
anxiety to be agreeable, mostly talked dead
rabbit to him. Now, he hated dead rabbit —in
fact, he wasn’t a slaughterous young man, and
he hated dead anything—so he spent most of his
time in drawing-room corners, looking into albums
and trying to avoid notice.
Joseph Hatton, whose death at the age of
68 is recorded, strolled on to the English section
of the Inky Way at a very early age. His father
was one of the first men to found a penny paper
when the repeal of the newspaper stamp tax made
such enterprise possible. Hatton senior was
proud of the fact that some ancient Act of Par-
liament authorised a printer to wear a sword ; he
also believed fervently that he was a descendant
of the “ dancing Chancellor,” Christopher Hatton,
who captivated our old friend Queen Liz the
Loud by the way in which he could fling his toes
about. Joseph did not indulge in the parental
vanities, possibly because he was too busy journal-
ising and writing novels, with every now and then
a play, or a travel-book, or a biography, or
special correspondence for journals abroad, in-
cluding at one time the Sydney Morning
Herald. If circumstances had allowed him to
write less he would probably have written much
better. Still, if much of his work was tripe it was
good tripe.
THE RAILINGS IN THE PARKS.
The Sydney City Council has decided to remove all
railings from the parks.— News Item.
Judge Murray, at Sydney Quarter Sessions
on Saturday, had before him an energetic person,
one William Collins, aged 22, who pleaded guilty to
15 charges of housebreaking. The Judge said
that if he gave the prisoner the full penalty the
sentence would be 150 years. As it was, he gave
him the full penalty on each charge, but the sen-
tences were concurrent.
The late Stephen H. Lambton, who was
50 years in the N.S.W. Postal Department, had
one or two hobbies, of which chess was the most
incurable. In the old days, when Public Service
Boards weren’t invented, any fine chess-player
who strayed to Sydney could get a billet in the
post office almost as easily as any of a certain
politician’s bush constituents could get into the
police force. Lambton had no difficulty in
getting jobs for his chess-players, but had con-
siderable trouble in keeping them in the jobs.
They often had little regard for office discipline,
and one particular chess star had no regard at all.
Recently dead at Glenelg (S.A.), Mrs.
Charles Swinden, aged 84. She came to S.A. in
1840 with her father, the late Nicholas Foote,
who brought with him cattle, horses, etc., and
selected and stocked what is now National Park.
Mrs. Swinden’s husband was a pastoralist in a
large way, and owned at one time or other Prin-
cess . Royal, _ Pinda, Bartagunya, Woolundunga.
Saltia, Callioota, Winnowie, Mount Attraction,
Corrieberrie, and Pernatty stations. In fact,
Swinden’s sheep had such a habit of browsing
over the broad surface of the Province, that on the
old Government plans all the lands west of Lake
Torrens were classified as “ Swinden’s Country.”
W. H. Moule, just raised to the Bench in
Victoria, was a fairly good cricketer in his day—
his day being about the ’eighties. He was a
member of Murdoch’s first -team (1880), which
played the first test match against All England
in England. Judge Moule’s batting average for
the tour was 12, and his bowling average 17, so he
was not a phenomenal success. To be a real live
Judge is about the dizziest height that an
Australian Eleven hero has attained, though
many of the species have done well. Mur-
doch himself married one of the wealthy
Bendigo Watsons. H. H. Massie has a
highly-salaried post with the Commercial Bank-
ing Co. of Sydney. H. J. H. Scott (Tuppenny
Scott) is a flourishing New England doctor.
T. W. Garrett and Dave Gregory are in good
Government billets. Also there was Mr. Coning-
ham, of the divorce proceedings.
Mr. Stephen Harbord Lambton, formerly
Deputy-Postrnaster-General of N.S.W., died sud-
denly at his home at Drummoyne on Thursday,
aged 73 years. He was born in Edinburgh in
November, 1834, and came to Sydney with his
parents two years afterwards. His father, who
was a solicitor, settled at Windsor, where lie
practised for several years. (Sixty years ago there
were four solicitors practising in the now dead
Hawkesbury town.) The deceased entered the
postal service at the age of 16, and dwelt therein
all the rest of his days till the leaden foot of
Superannuation overtook him.
William Morgan, the Sydney solicitor
who died suddenly at Paddington on July 31,
aged 65 years, was a native of Kelso, near
Bathurst, where his father was a manufacturer of
bricks. Most of the red brick town of Bathurst
emerged from the kilns of Morgan, senior.
William Morgan was in N.S.W. Assembly for a
while, but left no extraordinary footprint there.
His brother George, who suicided in Sydney
Domain a few years back, was well known as a
cricketer, and had been for years in the Civil
Service, first in the Lands Department, and finally
with the Sheriff.
Though well on in his sixties and showing
unmistakable signs of wear and tear—especially
of tear—J. L. Purves, of
Melbourne, still contrives to
keep his place as the best-
known criminal lawyer in
Australia. But there is very
little in his slow and heavy
gait and his thick, drawling
voice to remind you of the
fierce and relentless cross-
examiner who was the terror
of witnesses 20 or 30 years,
ago. In one respect only
does he give you any con-
vincing clue to his identity—
his propensity for contempt
of Court. But the wily old
lawyer knows the exact
length of his legal tether.
In a long case at Beech-
worth, the other day, Justice-
Hodges sternly ordered him
to sit down. Purves kept
his feet and debated the
point, and his Honor said to-
the gaoler, “Fetch the
Sheriff.” J.L. immediately
collapsed, and the next,
moment slipped into his seat..
Dr. Bevan, the elderly
Cupid of Collins-street, pas-
tor of the empty Independ-
ent Church, has the reputa-
tion of owning the healthiest,
appetite in Melbourne, now
that Benjamin Benjamin,
gets his plate of beef handed
to him by the angels. Bevan
can get through two
meals as rapidly as an ordin-
ary alderman would get-
through one, and he talks all
the time, and yet he never
talks with his mouth full—
at least not with it any fuller
than his church—and he
doesn’t seem to bolt his food to make room for
conversation either.
How is this for tenacity of life ! Reubent
Cook, of Portland (Vic.), had a wooden spike
driven clean through his head in a local saw-mill.
The stake entered the eye, and came out at the
back of his head, near the neck. It required the
daintiest surgical skill to extract the wood,
splinter by splinter, and now Reuben is doing
well. He has not even lost his sight; the ball of
the eye was pressed aside, but wasn’t seriously
injured.
“ Alba ” writes :
Mark Twain arrived in London amid the guffaws of
friendly donkey engines and steam sirens. Mark has.
developed boiled eye since he was in Australia ; his brow
wears a crushed, concertina-like appearance through
firing off tinned joke at the boy reporter. One or two.
big London papers pleaded hard for the King to see
Mark, and decorate him with the order of the Dried
Guffaw, or something. Why does the King avoid Mark
Twain ? That has long been a burning question across,
the Atlantic. Those who know Albert Edward reckon
that he will never give the Yankee joke-man the chance-
of his life. For 40 years or thereabouts Mark has written
dozens of imaginary interviews between himself and the
Man of many Dominions. If a meeting really happened,
Twain, it is assumed, would grip his chance and set the
world laughing at England’s monarch. The councillors
at Buckingham Palace are not inclined to rush their
potentate into the jaws of the comic lion.
STRANGE BEHAVIOUR OF THE OLD MAN.
The British Government intends to further reduce the hands employed at Woolwich Arsenal by 3000. — Cablegram.
The Editor of the S.A. Register is an
ardent lover of the colored Asiatic. He is also
entirely without a sense of humor, as indeed is
evidenced daily in his paper. His dreariness and
his nigger-hunger are evidenced by the fact that
he calls his house “ Kimono.” Why not give the
English equivalent of that Jap expression and
dub the gaudy mansion “Dressing Gown,” or
“ Loose Frock ” ?
All the fuss that was made a year or two
ago concerning Charles Truelove, ex-manager of
St. Kilda (Melb.) Cemetery, and his muddled
dealings with the graves and the cash accounts of
the fashionable bone-yard, where he long-time
held sway, has boiled down to a conviction of the
poor old chap on a charge of embezzlement, which
was so unsatisfactory and trivial that the Judge
sentenced him merely to a month’s imprisonment.
Truelove was a man of considerable musical taste
and education, with an enthusiasm for the train-
ing of church choirs, and no sort of a head for
business. In his troubles he was pathetically in-
capable of extricating himself, and he went to
“do” his month in prison, pitied by all who •
knew him, without feeling persuaded that he had
been guilty of any legal offence. When one
thinks of all the Melbourne men of his acquaint-
ance whose financial jugglings, particularly at
land-boom time, deserved twenty times more
punishment, and didn’t get it, the three prosecu-
tions of Truelove (two of them abortive) seem to
qualify him for a martyr’s crown and a big benefit
concert. As a promoter of musical entertain-
ments for everybody’s benefit but his own, the
ex-manager of St. Kilda Cemetery has an honor-
able record, anyhow.
The late Vic. Judge Hickman Moles-
worth, he of the chubby and beaming counte-
nance, had the job of seeing Victoria safely
through the Bankruptcy Court after the
boom burst. It was a strenuous time ;
in one year (1894) £5,239,010 worth of
liabilities, or about 25 times the present
annual average, drifted into the witness
box to explain to Molesworth how the
thing came about. As far as the amounts
owing were concerned, Victoria did nearly
twice as much business that year as all the
other States and Maoriland put together.
Molesworth took things philosophically as
long as feminine insolvents didn’t weep in
his court. He hated having his court wept
in, and used to look inclined to come down
from the Bench and personally dry the
testifier.
It lias been decided that Prince
Edward of Wales is to be officially known
as Prince David, and, if he lives, the kid
will eventually come to the throne as King
David I. Truly, there were two Davids
of Scotland far back in the misty past,
but they are not nearly so well known as
that David who made Mrs. Goliath a widow
and afterwards jumped the throne of
Jewry. The accession of David I. would
be generally accepted as an indication that
at last the Throne itself had passed, like
many Other things in England, into the
hands of the Chosen Folk who took over the
signboard and goodwill of the business
from the Lombards.
The Stork is still very friendly with the
German settler in S. Australia. Dr. Schmid, whose
“field” covers the scattered German farming com-
munity around Kapunda, lately wrote to the local
papers to say that he had just welcomed his 1515th
little stranger. And not one mother has been
lost in the process, he added.
Frank Myers, the lately-deceased journal-
ist, revelled in early Australian history, particu-
larly of the biographical sort, and his last essay
(written for The Lone Hand) was devoted to one
of his heroes, parson-politician Dr. John Dunmore
Lang. Myers mentioned that, to carry out his
immigration ideas, Lang sold his Sydney property.
He didn’t mention, though, what is something of
a coincidence—that the principal part of the
property was the fine block now occupied by
Petty’s Hotel, overlooking on one side the park
that "bears the old warrior’s name, and on the
other the square in which stands the appalling
Lang statue.
Melb. Congregationalists tell the public,
who are quite uninterested, that Collins-street
Independent church can’t raise an attendance
big enough to stop the echoes on Sundays. The
fact is that old woolly woman Bevan has turned
his congregation away by his unchristian belief
in Anti-Sosh, and his foolish and impotent object-
tions to “ White Australia.”
Biographical sketch from “ F.” -
A much dilapidated skittle in Australian politics is
Edmund Lonsdale, Foreign-trader, Single-taxer, and Anti-
Australian generally. Lonsdale belongs to New England
(N.S. W.), and it is a favorite diversion of that district to
set Lonsdale up in order to knock him down. Armidale
first adopted Lonsdale for its pet skittle when, with grim
humor, it set up this staunch Single-taxer, who believes
in a land-tax without any exemptions, to support Reid’s
Land Tax, which depended for existence on the exemp-
tion principle. Then the Australian Constitution Bill
game came along, and Lonsdale asked Armidale to allow
him to remain perpendicular as an Anti-Bill piece of
wood, but Armidale took a steady aim, and knocked
Lonsdale down. Next Lonsdale thought he’d make a
very good Foreign-trade pin in the Federal alley,
but New England sent along a beautiful ball and
bowled him down before he was up, so to speak.
A little later, however, Armidale reckoned he was
down long enough for once, and set him up again
in the State alley, and then New England borrowed him
for a while, and made him vertical in Melbourne. Here
Lonsdale remained more or less erect until the last
Federal competitions, and then New England scored a
splendid shot, and Lonsdale went down for the third
time. This last fall rather scared Lonsdale, and he
scooted off, and offered himself to Newtown (Sydney) in
the State selection tournament. Newtown just looked
at Lonsdale, and promptly knocked him down. Now
Lonsdale is pleading to Armidale to set him up once
more, but much flooring has knocked most of the varnish
off Lonsdale, and, according to present indications,
Armidale reckons that - as a skittle he has outlived his
usefulness.
THE DOWNWARD PATE.
The British Board of Education has put a stop to the
practice of training the scholars at the public elementary
schools at Eynsford (Kent) and Shoreham (Sussex) in rifle
shooting.— Cable News.
The 8.8. of E. : “ Briggs, my boy , I'm sur-
prised at you lowering yourself to the level of
a beastly common soldier ! There's no necessity
for it. When the time comes it’s the place of
the lower classes to do the fighting — so this
must cease at once ! ”
Barrister Moule’s appointment as Yic.
County Court Judge, vice Molesworth, deceased,
was quite unexpected. Acting-Judge Neighbour,
Duffy, K.C., and Finlayson had been mentioned,
though the last two would probably have de-
clined ; but apparently nobody thought of
“ Billy ” Moule, Australian cricketer, whose
name had scarcely been heard since he quitted
politics in 1900, defeated by the same Thomas
Bent whom he had ousted in 1894. Moule is a
combination of the “ quiet brown man,” such as
Turner, and the cold, bloodless lawyer, such as
Irvine. Latterly he has helped to edit the Vic.
Law Reports, and is regarded as an authority on
case law.

UNDER VICE-REGAL PATRONAGE.
MADAME MASSERAN,
CORSETIERE, of Collins Street, Melbourne,
Begs to inform her clients that she will be at the
HOTEL METROPOLE, SYDNEY,
FROM THE 7th TO THE 15th AUGUST,
Both Days Inclusive.
The Melbourne movement towards re-
ducing the cost of State Government, abolition of
the six unnecessary Governors, etc,, would have a
better chance of success if the organisers would
hang a heavy weight on the political failures who
are trying to float into public notice again on what
they see is a rising tide. At one meeting, after
the local members had given the affair a send-off,
no less than five rejected politicians fastened their
little cards on the resolution. But the last straw
came when a telegram was read from poor old
derelict B. Goddard, of Kyabram. It only needed
Mudkins’ blessing to kill the movement stone dead.
A Red Nose is the bane of many a woman’s exist-
ence. It detracts so greatly from her appearance that all
sorts of subterfuges are resorted to to hide the defect. But
ladies should know that an absolutely certain and pain-
less 9ure can be had at the Valaze Institute. Here Dr.
Lassar’s Electric Device, operating a process of Nasal
Massage, restores a Red Nose to its normal color in from
six to eight sittings. Mile. Helena Rubinstein secured
this treatment from Vienna, where it is used in the
Beauty Salons for which that city is famed. Of course,
one must personally attend the Institute to be treated,
but those who have to delay their visit may secure a
Cream and Powder from 8/- that hides the defect.
Then, when opportunity offers, they can call and need
have no further trouble with the disfigurement. Call
or write either to the Valaze Institute, 274 Collins
Street, Melbourne, or 158 Pitt Street, Sydney.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Stdnet, August 6, 1907.
Mt Dear Arinl
Miss Emma Temple supervised the “living
Sicture ” show, given in aid of the District
fursing Society at the Palace last week. Her
friends say she looked five years- older after the
ordeal. The martyrdom of Emma ! Two playlets
were also produced under her direction. Possibly
the smart amateurs who did the pictures and
plays proved stupid or stiff-jointed at rehearsals.
But the result was so brilliant a success that the
supervisor and her assistants are hereby' awarded
the C.S.T. order for distinguished service.
Comet of Seven Tails is the full name of the new
order.
If Captain Wilson should be cut off from his
present means of money supply (whatever it may
be), J. C. Williamson or Anderson or Geach
would give him a leading-man job at once. In
the duologue, “A Breezy Morning,” that pre-
ceded the tableaux, he played the bored husband
to Miss Doris Bloomfield’s bored wife, as well as
the professional imported matinee idol. But—
the audience saw all his socks. I don’t suggest
that there were more than two of them, or that
they had Jacob’s ladders in them, but they
weren’t smart socks. Who wears those color
schemes in hose that we see in the men’s depart-
ments of swell shops—the apricot, drake-green,
and Nattier blue socks ? I didn’t wait to see him
star in the final piece, “Browne with an ‘E,’”
but possibly he changed his socks for an “ earth
of Egypt ” pair. That is a nice shade of brown.
Audience included as many Sydney Somebodies
as the theatre would hold. Some of the stalls
were resold at a guinea each, and the front rows
of the gallery (at seven-and-six) were crowded.
Young Curate Saumarez Smith, with his bride,
blessed the show. Mrs. Curate S. Smith is a
sharp contrast to her rather puggy lord, with her
thin, Elizabethan profile, and her pale and dark-
haired outlook. She seems clever and capable of
ably filling the uneasy billet of parson’s wife. I
believe it is a mighty job. Every female
parishioner thinks she should be asked to tea at
the parsonage, and treated as if her weekly silver
thruppence supported the parsonic establish-
ment. And then how sick Mrs. Minister must
get of seeing her husband in his long flying coat
and foolish collar! One can’t realise young
Saumarez in a dissipated blazer.
Another fashionable clerk in holy orders and
the dress circle was Mr. Metcalf, who is a marine
preacher on the Pah’f’l. His bride-elect, a pretty
North Shore girl, was with him.
The living-picture part of the show started with
Rosetti’s “Blessed Damozel.” You know her,
leaning out “ from the gold bar of Heaven,” with
three lilies on her arm and seven stars in her hair.
Miss Irene Marks, as the Damozel, and Miss
Gladys Bowker and Vera Moore, as the two
angels looking up from below the bar (as if in fear
that the lady in the celestial dress circle will
throw herself over back on to our planet), made a
beautiful live replica of the masterpiece. But out
of the nineteen pictures, Mrs. Ted Simpson and her
little girl, posing as Madame Le Brun and child,
were the star effect; even the seven stars in the
blessed thatch of the Damozel paled before them.
The seventeenth century picture shows Mme. Le
Brun seated erect, one arm, bare from the shoulder,
thrown around the child whose head rests
on her breast. Mrs. Ted did the picture majestic
justice, but the lighting of the “canvases” was
faulty. The little Belisario and Rodd boys re-
peated the success they made at Lady North-
cote’s children’s party with Millais’ “Princes in
the Tower.” “Joan of Arc” (Millais’ painting
of the Maid in armor, and holding a sword) fell to
Miss Y. Riddell. If the real Joan had looked like
thatlive picture, Charles“theWell-Served” would
have married her, instead of leaving her to
be burned by the English at Rouen, and with a
tidy and capable lady to look after him he might
not have died miserably of hunger on the throne
when he was 58, and his grandson might not
have worn the lamentable distinction of “the
Affable.”
All the pictures were based on imported origi-
nals. One can’t blame Captain Wilson he’s
an imported . article himself ; but Miss Eadith
Walker might have shown sufficient patriotism
to frame an Australian picture.
There’s nothing more to be said unless it be the
word “ amazement ” at the bad taste and reckless-
ness of men in the audience who struck matches to
look at their, programmes when the auditorium was
darkened. Chiffons and other highly inflam-
mable materials, and quite 20 lighted matches in
a crowded circle !! !
Circle and stalls were made auriferous in spots
with the uniforms of naval men. Mrs. Ewan
Frazer and some friends were a sombrely smart
boxful. Miss Eadith Walker, gorgeously and
bluely clad, received the State Governor and Miss
Rawson and the tribe of elect who came with
them. Next Sunday the Rawsons leave for a
saunter to Queensland.
On Wednesday the Northcotes slipped quietly
out of Sydney and fled Melbournewards. Her
Ex. will return in September, to stand on the
deep-piled carpet (now being made) and open this
State’s show of work for the Women’s Work Ex-
hibition. The Empress of Japan is sending Lady
Northcote some goods and effects done by herself,
and twenty Jap ladies are coming to Melbourne
for the Exhibition'. They won’t be exhibits, but
visitors.
More singing of “ O, Perfect Love,” more play-
ing of the “Wedding March”! Miss Ethel Wood,
the artist, is going to marry Mr. J. B. Jones, of
the legal and six-and-eightpenny firm of Jones
and Jones, possibly before the new year. The
bridegroom-elect is a notable University athlete,
and is plated with medals like an ironclad; or, at
least, he would be if he hung ’em on all at once.
As it is, he has made a most knightly use of some
of the tokens. He had them made into a belt for
his bride-elect.
Two bridal pairs, the “ Bert ” Littlejohns (she
was a Teece lamb) and the Dr. Adams (he of the
Pah’f’l, and she a Knox girl), are eating after-the-
honeymoon dinners in Sydney. First-named
brace took an Asian trip, and have now settled
down at Double Bay. Lieutenant Rawson and
his young mate are motoring about Queensland.
As that State runs to over 670,000 square miles,
they don’t expect to do the whole of it, but they
are shoving the motor for all it is worth.
Talking of Queensland, every theatrical enter-
prise going there this winter has been frosted
with many degrees of frost. Even Alice and
“ Wonderland ” on wheels didn’t have to put up
“ Tent Full ” notices. Manager Anderson, when
in Brisbane, listened to a Queenslander ski ting
about “our boundless resources” and “our four-
teen million sheep.” “What a pity we aren’t
playing to sheep ! ” said the manager, gloomily.
Miss Ackerman n has had to take her tall,
vigorous self into ambush to escape the impor-
tunity of the axe-grinding multitude. She is fol-
lowed about by an adoring bodyguard of girls. So
the star stage charmer hasn’t a monopoly of the
charmed. The drawing-room meeting must hang
on Miss Ackermann’s mind like a wet macintosh.
Dismal things are drawing-room meetings ! The
hostess mostly gives one the feeling of saying,
“Make yourself at home in my morgue.”
As the visitor is here principally in the in-
terests of the Westminster Gazette, she is
pursuing information as the hawk does his
prey. On Friday she tracked the home-made
drama to its genesis, observing “The Squatter’s
Daughter” from a box at the Cri. She is away
now, going through the country where the wheat
waves, and the butter factory flourisheth. All
the time she is reaping statistics about women’s
work in the Commonwealth.
Miss Ackermann is too ’cute not to know the
value of good frocking. Her theatre get-up and
a costume in which she attended a luncheon at
the Australia were unobtrusive but right up to
the last split second in smartness.
Influenza has smitten us so heavily that the
papers are giving it column-long articles as if it
were a distinguished visitor or a big fire. The
epidemic has gone through all the boarding
schools and commercial houses, and has rung up
the telephone bureau with the energy of an in-
fluential subscriber. On one day 130 employees-
were away from Anthony Hordern’s through the
strenuous microbe, and on a daily average 80
people belonging to the Great Providery have
had to dodder into bed with a hot and
aching head in one hand and a prescription
in the other. At the public schools nearly
half the teachers and pupils are laid low.
Three weeks ago, eighteen working girls (typists,
music-teachers, and the like) in a certain suburb
formed themselves into an “ Anti-’Flu’ Society.”
Each carries a bottle of ammoniated quinine with
her, and takes a dose at the hint of a sneeze or a
chill. If any member fails to produce her bottle
when challenged by another member, whenever
or wherever they met. she is fined tuppence. An-
other fine of sixpence is imposed if a member gets
into evening dress whilst the visitation stalketh
abroad, like Satan seeking dry places, and
the rules insist that members “shall feed
regularly and nourishingly.” It may or may
not be chance, but, so far, not one of the Anti-
’Flu’ Society has been smitten. Also, not one
fine has been collected.
The casualness of some mothers : A rather loud
young matron, who turns out every day to do the
Block or go to a race meeting, and spends half
the night at Bridge tables, was awakened
after midnight by a knocking at her front
door like unto the knocking at the gate in
“ Macbeth.” Someone answered —most probably
not the loud young matron. The coachman stood
on the mat with the matron’s baby, aged two, in
his arms. He had found the infant asleep in the
stable. Probably the nurse had put the baby to
bed, as per usual, at the usual time, and possibly
the infant had got up and wandered out. But the
pathos of the tale lies in the fact that baby’s
mother, who' had racketed home at midnight,
couldn’t have looked into his cot before getting
into her own.
Mrs. Eileen Mitchell O’Moore has started
fiddle-teaching in Sydney. She publishes letters
from her teachers, Ysaye and Sevcik, and from
Walter Damrosch, in which the writers speak of
her as quite too utterly finer than any previous
pupil or performer. Her portraits show her as a
handsome woman
Miss Dina Cooper, her brown eyes and her
turquoises, go to Tasmania in a few weeks, to
open in a new play wherein she will be leading
damsel.
Andrew Black is staying at Auckland with his
friend Dr. Stopford. Premyslav and pianiste
Beattie, with Mrs. Gower Burns of Christchurch,
are giving concerts in the South Island, and Rosina
Buckmann is having a short holiday in Taranaki,
under the cold shades of Mount Egmont. H. N.
Southwell left them all alive and doing well after
a prosperous run through Ward’s own country.
H.N.S. canoed down the Wanganui River. As he
swells fore and aft when saying this I take it that
the feat is an unusual one. A man I know tried it
once, but failed to get started. He negotiated for
two days with a mob of chattering brown men,
two of whom were to take him’down the river.
There were from 14 to 20 Maoris engaged in the
negotiation, and evfery time he fixed an arrange-
ment, with a pair of them that pair seemed pre-
sently to dissolve into the general mob, and the
clatter started afresh. Terms were fixed and un-
fixed a score of times, and in the end the mob
melted away, and matters remained as they
were, • except that a very hoarse Australian was
trying to buy a voice jujube.
At the conversazione to Dr. and Mrs. Arthur,
on Tuesday night, August. 13, Miss Eva Cham-
pion and Dr. Maguire will sing, and Mr. A. H.
Norman’s string quartet and Gilhooley’s orches-
tra will discourse heavenly melody. Messrs. Car-
ruthers, Hogue and Lee will probably be present,
and fill an hour or so with burning eloquence. _ A
supper committee is doing happily in the trifle
and sandwich line. Evening dress is discouraged.
It ought to be a fine and dandy meeting.
Mrs. Willie Robinson, daughter-in-law of the
Age Robinson, and the most graceful Melbourne
skater, has been staying at Petty’s and cutting
ice at the Glaciarium here. The example of these
Melbournians has urged local women to make
that effort for want of which Mrs. Dombey
perished, and all around us are evidences of the
evil effects of strong rink. At 10 a.m. the grown-
up girls leave mother and the one misfit to wrestle
with the household wprk. They shoulder the
white woman’s burden while Ethel and Gwen go
a-iceing. Households of women with a good staff
of misfits don’t suffer so much. Miss Rawson is
coming along capitally in the amusement, and
Mrs. Burdekin is taking to it kindly. Fat learners
are like Polar bears travelling on their hind feet.
All self-consciousness must be shed if you are
going to learn to skate. You know that the
collective grin of the onlookers is as long and
wide as a bath, but your time—the time when
you’ll waltz on the frozen lake—is coming.
Recent papers contained the announcement of
“a quiet W'edding,” at St. George’s Church,
Penang, Mr. Gordon C. Hart, Adviser
to the Government of Kedah, son of the late
Justice Hart, High Court, Bombay, and Mrs.
Norton Goodwyn, widow of Lieut.-Col. Goodwyn,
D. 5.0., of the Devon Regiment, and daughter of
the late Mr. Thomas Charles Gore, of Bathurst.
All the Big People thereabouts were present. No
mentioii is made of the first husband of the lady,
Mr. Willie Mitchell, of Tooths Limited and
Darling Point. The bride’s father, Mr. T. C.
Gore, was Registrar of the District Court at
Bathurst in the late ’sixties.
Two ancient Bathurst families became hyphened
at Holy Trinity Church, Kelso (the oldest church
beyond the Mountains), on July 25, when Mr.
“Jack” Ingersole was wedded to Miss Sarah
Kathleen, eldest daughter of Walter Rivett.
Canon Oakes, representative of one of the oldest
families in Australia, performed the ceremony.
Miss Lillian Seaife, of the W.-W. Co., who is
playing Eliza Dibbs in “Mr. Hopkinson,” is to
be married in a few weeks to Mr. Warburton
Gamble, of the same company.
Workers are wanted to complete the carpet on
which Lady Northcote will stand to open the
W omen’s Work Exhibition. Anyone with fairly-
deft fingers and a normal amount of brains can
do the work.
Saturday was a very frocky night on the various
stages. There were new pieces at all the theatres,
and new gowns. Blanche Arral, who was at the
top of her vocal condition, was at the Town Hall,
and her changes of gowns, or rather robes, gave
the concert a tableau vivant value. The living
picture moved and sang like a prima donna in the
Nine Choirs of Angels co., now appearing regu-
larly in Paradise. In the Mad Scene from
“ Hamlet,” the Arral, in long classic draperies,
with poppies in her hair and a sheaf of floral
sprays in her arms, was a charming picture. The
audience spent ten solid minutes (after the last
programme item) in fusillades of applause. It’s
about up to us that Arral tells us who is Arral.
Grace Palotta’s gowns in “Mr. Hopkinson”
are like the clothes of Heaven. She does the
Duchess in fine grande-dame fashion, and wears
in the first act a frock of cherry chiffon over white,
with elaborate yoke of cream lace and cherry
velvet trimmings. An emerald-green chiffon or
ninon over cloth of silver is another achievement.
The skirt has half-wreaths of silver leaves, with
an emerald choux in the centre. The bodice is a
complication of green and silver, with a rain of
silver tassels or pendants on chiffon.
Miss Baines, at the Palace, also exploited some
splendid gowns in her r&le of Merely Mary Ellen
masquerading as an heiress. One was a cream pop-
lin overlaid with hand embroidery, and another
of gleaming blue and silver. Everyone notices
the likeness between Florence Baines and Maggie
Moore. There is the same dazzling smile,
vivacity, and versatility, but Maggie’s plumpness
is not there—at least not all of it.
State Ex. Rawson was to have shed the light of
his uniform on the Palace Theatre on Saturday
night, with Miss; but a question arose at the last
moment about the difference between Tweedle-
dum and Tweedledee, and a straw of etiquette
failed to split precisely down the centre, so the
vice-regal brace stayed away. And the red carpet
was down, too ! Also the front entrance was hung
with foliage, and the Flag of dear old Engle-
land, as W. Barrett called it, waved over all, and
good-looking bobbies and a crowd of gazers had
assembled on the roadway.
Probably some one from “ Cranbrook ” gave
the S.M.H. an explanation, for that paper
had an “inspired statement” (lovely phrase!)
three inches long, under three headings. “Vice-
Regal Etiquette ” was one, in big black type;
the second, “ ‘Patronage’ and ‘Presence’”; the
third, “An Important Distinction.” It was at
the word “Patronage” the Governor flung his
cocked hat with deadly aim. Explanation says
the word should not have been used in this con-
nection in the advertisements announcing his
Ex.’s intention to attend the show. Only to
charity things does the Governor extend his
patronage. But the advts. had been out
for three weeks, and the management had
not been advised of his Ex.’s intention of
staying away until shortly before the theatre
opened. The explanation won’t hold champagne,
or sand, because a vice-regal command night has
often and yet again been announced as “ under the
distinguished patronage and in the presence of
Lord Nozoo.”
Personally, I’m inclined to the idea that Sir
Harry put his helm hard aport (I hope that’s right
nautically) when he saw the announcement
blazoned on the hoardings of the city. Turning
to that fine dentifrice advt., Captain Wilson, he
probably thundered : “Does the Miss Lancashire
Limited Company mistake me for a freak ? Am I
to be billed as if I were a new camel at a
circus?”
Miss Agnes Noyes Goodsir, the Victorian
artist, now living and working in London, had an
exhibition principally of portraiture work in the
Austral Club recently. Several of her pictures
were reproduced by English illustrated papers.
Miss Goodsir is a sister of Mrs. Nellie Phillipps,
the Melbournian who wrote the popular Mistletoe
Waltz.
My Brisbane correspondent writes :
Governor Chelmsford, after delivering a masterly dis-
course at the Royal Geographical meeting this week, has
gone on a riding tour through to Toowoomba, where his
lady, with her sister and her cousin, will join him.
Lady C., clad in a cream sicilienne gown, opened
the annual picture show in the Albert Hall on Thurs-
day afternoon with the briefest of brief speeches.
From an art standpoint the show is about 150 per cent,
above any of its predecessors. President Godfrey Rivers
has done himself proud with a night scene upon the river
and an early morning land-and-riverscape, which (despite
the suggestions of an inane local critic, who declared that
“ a string of ducks would have improved it ”) is a real fine
effort. Several Queensland artists who are away study-
ing beauty of form in Paris and elsewhere sent acceptable
samples of their work.
Invitations are out for a vice-regal dance in show week,
and, as over-crowding is to be strictly avoided, great is
the howl of those “on the mat.” You can’t avoid over-
crowding except by shutting out somebody, and the
shufc-out section takes wildly unreasonable views of the
situation, and expresses them frankly through the key-
hole.
Mrs. Holliday, who comes from America and England
to have another try at getting that famous mothers’
union into working order, arrived on Monday, and will
tell harrowing tales to all and sundry during the next
week. She begins with an address to nurses, and if she
can give them any further points about bagging the
eligible medical student, they will be grateful.
Snow fell within 40 miles of Brisbane on Wednesday
last, and the air was like that of a blizzard for 24 hours.
This constitutes a Queensland record.
Tittell Brune shed her steely smile and her intense
attitude over two social tea-fights on Friday. First, she
drifted to the Pioneer Club, and, amid nasturtiums
and roses, spent half-an-hour while some good music was
contributed by a number of people. Later, she flitted to
the big she-convention at the Cate Eschenhagen, where
Mrs. Jim Robertson, a substantial vision in pastel blue,
made some 90 guests forget that the old man’s dinner
was probably burning a pale black in the oven at home.
The room was a mass of exquisite white La Mark roses,
for which the garden of the h stess is famous.
Never before did such a crowd pack itself into the
Brisbane Cricket Ground at Woollangabba as that which
witnessed the third wiping out of Australia by the All-
Blacks on Saturday afternoon. The blue and chocolates
led at half-time, and mad was the cheering thereat. But
later, with the wind and the enemy against them, they
blew away like leaves at Vallombrosa, or any other gusty
old spot. Still there was plenty of excitement, and the
crowd smashed most of the seats in its enthusiasm. The
damage was mostly done during the first half of the pro-
ceedings.
Artist-novelist Mrs. Owen Harris is doing the northern
coastal tour in search of copy for both pen and brush.
She will prcbably be an exhibitor at the new Society of
Artists. „
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
The Point-to-Point Steeplechase, pet event of the Hunt
season, caught a wet day for its revels on the 27th.
Nevertheless, the dukes of Adelaide and the Semaphore
put the cover on their second-best motor, and ambled
down to Findon to see the fun. It was damp amusement,
and mud obliterated the landscape, and most of the pink-
coated persons wore a battered air by the end of the day.
A gorgeous afternoon tea was ready and waiting under
the shelter of a marquee, but the majority of the peeresses
preferred to go tea-less rather than swim to the fray. Our
fathom and six inches of Excellency and Miss Clowes
were dragged down (metaphorically and literally) in
David Waite’s smart turn-out. The Chief Judicial Way
shepherded another party, and the Leonard Bakewells,
the Melroses and Mrs. David Fulton, Richard Baker, and
some friends, were among those who braved the wild
elements.
On Friday (2nd) the Hunt Ball chanced at Strathalbyn.
It is one of the best sprees of the year, and, as usual, the
girly element of the State fished brilliantly for invita-
tions. Every house in the neighborhood was packed full
of town girls and their best chiffons, and the local male
element had the time of its life. Two other dances on
the near horizon are the “ Tarantelle,” to be held at Nor-
wood by a he-committee, and one at St. Peter’s College,
when the will host a crowd of short-frocked and
pig-tailed misses. '
The 30 or 40 sweet maidens who man (or girl) the Girls’
Realm Guild in this city manipulated a joyous spree at
the Lyric Club on the 27th. The Lady Victoria Buxton
Girls’ Club was in need of tables and chairs and a coal-
scuttle for its new residential quarters, so the maidens
aforesaid came to its rescue and invited some 70 smart
feminines to tea and music, with a modest footnote re-
questing that each one should bring a chair, a grand
piano, a soup plate, a book-case, a salt-cellar, or some
other piece of useful bric-a-brac. The snug club quarters
were in festal array with two or three cartloads of almond
bloom, asparagus fern and violets. Her Le Hunte Ex-
cellency, in a gorgeous robe of old rose silk, lent her
countenance to the affair: also Mrs. Coles and
daughter Ruth, Mrs. and Miss Wallman, the David
Lindsays, Mrs. Beach, Mrs. Cleland, Mrs. T. A. Caterer,
and a crowd more. Five brilliant damsels of the Guild
(notably the Victorian nightingale. Lizzie Newbold, and
little Ethel Sever, a 14-year-old fiddler of remarkable
promise) achieved a programme of sweet noises. After-
wards the populace bent its mighty intellect on a guess-
ing competition, in which you had to decide whether a
given parcel was a rocking-chair or only a skewer. The
prize was a set of dainty little water-color sketches,
painted for the Guild by the vice-regal chick before she
was hustled away to school in the land where they say
“ Hocb.”
Adelaide has been engaged for the past week shaking
the visiting lacrosse men by the brawny fist, and inviting
thein to dinner. The Football League hired a drag anil
tore round the suburbs with them ; the Lyric Club enter-
tained them ; and the peerage came dow n off its pedestal ;
and its ramparts, and said “ Howdy?’* All the ton and
fashion turned out to the match on Saturday, in its
wedding or Sunday garment and its best millinery out-
burst. One question the athletic visitor eternally hurls
at us is : “ Why do you Australians always havefences to
your front gardens?” My answ'er is that we do it be-
cause we feel that way. If it comes to that, why is it
that Canadians have bears and snow-shoes, and why
don’t wax matches grow' on icebergs ?
The Hammond silver wedding held at the Bray man-
sion in Hutt-street, on July 31, was rather a rare
festivity, and the titled population flocked thereunto in
considerable force. The Hammonds are a versatile
family, and have manipulated dozens of successful
charity shivoos, from Punch and Judy show’s upwards.
This time they gathered a number of clever people into a
band of white minstrels, and these troubadours, in
picturesque knee-breeches and powdered hair, achieved
a programme of good minstrelsy for the amusement of
the guests.
Adelaide is to have a batch of Albani concerts this
week, and if the world doesn’t come to a sudden end, or
any unforeseen trifle like that, we shall have heard the
star in “Elijah ” with the aid of the Choral Society, and
be girding up our shekels for her second and third con-
certs by the time this communication is held by the
public paw in front of the public orb.
Yours Affectionately,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 5, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
There is a lull in the garrotting season. The
bewatched and bediamonded city man of portly
ponderance is no longer gagged and winded ; the
gay young buck of late hours is not chased for his
cuff links, nor is the unescorted maid bereft of her
jewels and her coherence of narrative. The
strained, anxious look of the homeward bound
suburbanite relaxes a trifle. The murderous
marauders must be lying low till the scare cools
down, and then a resumption of hostilities will
take the public unawares.
Maidens aforetime were called spinsters because
of the hopeful work they got in on the prospective
trousseau. Nowadays, no one owns up to “spin-
ster.” She is just a bachelor girl or “miss-by-
ehoice,” and she never spins—except yarns or in
a motor. But I know an exception. A smart
girl (one of George Grey’s American-looking
daughters—the “Ontario” Greys) is having cosy
little afternoon teas for girl pals, and she gives
them fascinating peeps at piles of frills and acres of
tissue-papered frocks, collected for th e frou-frou of
her wedding this month. They are not “ out from
Paris,” nor labelled with a swagger Melbourne
store mark ; but are stitched by her own deft
little hands. It’s a fortune in lace and flimsies.
The lucky He who is to wed this dainty paragon
is Reg.-Jackson, of the “Young and Jackson ”
firm, and the wedding is fixed for the 20th.
“Tryphena” writes from London on a first
night at Daly’s :
It’s an occasion for which the London public, the rest-
ing professionals, the critics, and the composers give up
everything, and for which they struggle like so many
dogs after a bone. A fresh comic opera or melody farce,
either with or without a continental reputation, has a
piquant fascination for the emulative playmonger. It
has large sentimental prospects for the golden ass that
brays in the stalls the whole season through. It holds a
promise of delight for the general golden public to
whom a fresh footlight dazzler is of more consequence
than a new Prime Minister. It means a sensa-
tion for femininity, which sees in the brilliantly-
caparisoned legion of girl, fashions of glory, illuminated
and set off as they never are in the show rooms of
Paquin or Worth. Yet Daly’s is not as large as Mel-
bourne Princess’s, and out of all the desirous thousands
only some hundreds find accommodation. It happened
significantly that George Edwardes marked out the
second Saturday in gorgeous June —the month of the
year in all Merrie England—for a first night. And for
once there were no postponements. Up till the dinner-
hour, ticket-holders nervously eyed the latest
press intelligence for the dreaded word of sick-
ness of a principal, motor accident to a star,
or a fit of jealousy that ended fatally. Even
up to the door of the theatre, the heart of the enthusiast
was agitated by fears of a hasty postponement. Nothing
so miserable happened, and the baton of the conductor,
raised for the first beat of “ The Merry Widow ” music,
brought a thunder of applause from the relieved throng.
The first bars of Franz Lehar’s insinuating music created
an atmosphere that lasted till the last curtain-fall.
The small footlighter who was billed as the Widow was
a vague quantity to the theatre-goer. Lily Elsie is a
weak, forgettable name, anyway. No girl with pretensions
should have labelled herself Lily Elsie. But she started
very subtly and sweetly to write her feeble name on the
scroll of theatrical glory. Without having a notable
voice or any magnetic power she did it. Slowly the chic
little star overcame one’s prejudices against even her
physical unfitness as the dashing, devilish, worldly widow,
and got home with her clever int erpretation of the lines,
her artful acting and the fascination of her twinkling
feet. She danced, acted, chattered with consummate
daintiness,and when,in ascalygarb and exposing draperies,
she half-lay, half-floated in the arms of the Prince rake
whom she was out, to conquer, and wriggled her soft
body to the lilting waltz-refrain, her triumph was com-
plete. The audience forgot it was English, forgot it was
tired, and hurst into a roar of applause.
Among the audience were composers, artists, cele-
brities of all classes. Captain Basil Hood, who quarrels
with Edward Morton over the authorship (or rather the
adaptorship from the German) of “The Merry Widow,”
strolled round the stalls at the interval—chewing his
drooping moustache, and with battle in his eye. On the
bills appeared the name of Morton as adaptor, and the
triumph of the show led to the moustache-chewing of
Hood. Two days later, Morton hit his lip. His name
was off the bill.
Mrs. Langtry was a-droop (the Jersey Lily droops in a
chair ; she never sits) in a stall. Her brown hair (not a
grey strand visible) was parted Madonna-wise and colled
seemingly without the help of hair-pins on her indolent
neck. Many men sought her at the interval. She talked
lazily with a heavy air of indifference. Clara Butt sat
witli her handsome Rumford in a front stall. Alice
Crawford—resting because the salaries offered of late do
not reach her claims—languished in a stall, looking less
handsome than I expected. She confessed to motor-fag.
Guvment House has taken off its holland pinny
and got its gorgeous footmen a-row, now the
Northeote dame is here again. There was an At
Home on Friday afternoon, preceded by a com-
mittee meeting re the coming huge Exhibition of
Australian Women’s Work. When the North-
cote lady got through with her say, a lengthy row
of secretaries rolled up with accounts of their
stewardship so far. Dr. Greig was the most
business-like. Her particular committee is to
have an exhibit of an old-time Sairey Gamp sick-
room, and, by way of contrast, the most up-to-
date of modern hospital apartments.
The secretary of the athletic department was
the most timid lady of the lot. She whispered
strenuous rules about riding competitions and
furious sports, and repeated the regulations re
motor-racing and hair-dressing four times. These
two branches of sport were bracketed together.
Mebbe, it is an athletic feat getting one’s hair
straightened out after motoring, but the subject
is deep.
A faint suggestion of argument arose on the
point of dress. It had to do with riding and
other athletics, whereat Secretary Share looked
down his nose and smiled almost blushfully.
After intense discussions, the divided skirt has
been firmly tabooed. The athletic ladies of the
show will not ostensibly bifurcate.
When it got too dark to see reports and such,
the Governor-lady declared it was quite tea-time.
In the drawing-rooms some hidden musicians dis-
coursed sweetly and softly, and the cheerful
hostess got in a word and a smile all round.
Everybody had froeked Huffily for the function.
Lady Northeote swept round in soft trails of
autumn browns, and the Madden dame wore quite
a wake-up green (she is wont to wear deliciously
soft colors). The Forrest lady kept to her beloved
mauves and violets. The top-notch dresser was
Mrs. George Chirnside, who sparkled in all black,
and looked like the Frenchiest of Jan Van Beer’s
Eictures, with her chic hat, a diamond clasp on
er black-clad neck, and, instead of furs, the
Tobiest of tulle frills.
One of the most original specimens of crafts-
manship intended for the Women’s Exhibition is
the work of Mrs. Blamire Young. It’s a carved
door of ordinary proportions, made of Australian
wood, which is left unstained. A lithe nude
woman is the principal item of the composition,
which is further beautified by a most poetic
arrangement of stately native flowers. Mrs.
Young has succeeded in making a door fit to be
used as a barrier ’twixt a Paradise and the outside
world. It’s very suggestive, this decorative effort.
It suggests that the door, usually a bare unsightly
arrangement of when it isn’t disguised in
an equally untesthetic mass of fabrics, could be
made into the most characteristic and significant
feature of a room—the sign-manual of the room-
owner’s temperament. The door could serve as
a lure for lovers, an inspiration for students, an
appetiser for gourmets.
Nowadays, Melbourne is greeted each morning
with an invitation to avail itself of a lydy
detective’s subtle services. She is “Trapped,”
care of a suburban post office, and announces that
she is highly successful in the most involved cases
—especially divorce suits. She suggests the
nucleus of a novelette romance, in which the
wronged petitioner gets rict of his erring missus,
and takes to his buzzom the skirted ’tec who ran
the case. By the way, didn’t Mr. Willoughby run
a farcical comedy on those lines ?
Mrs. John Forrest, it seems, if the press is to
be believed, has opened her mouth so widely this
week that she may probably find she has placed
her energetic number five in it. Her topic
was (naturally) Emperor John and his defection
from the Ministry. And the journal to which she
confided her peppery pronouncements was
Truth. Amongst other rash and ill-considered
statements, she is reported to have informed the
interviewer “ My husband always had a backbone.
The others are jelly-fish.” “Mr. Deakin is a great
chum of ours ; but he’s so weak. He gives in too
often.” “ Mr. William Lyne—we call him Bill—
the Cabinet calls him the Rogue Elephant.
Chapman is his understudy. What a pity it is
that Deakin can’t stand up to them !” “My
husband is a scientific Protectionist. He has felt
most uncomfortable in the Ministry. He has put
up with a great deal.” Mrs. F. is candid—
probably some folk would say maliciously tactless.
She also animadverts on land taxation and Social-
ism. “It’s well enough,” she says, “for people
with nothing at stake to advocate Socialism. But
such people aren’t the ones who have made Aus-
tralia. . . I don’t believe in handicapping the
producer.”
Sincere regret has been felt for the death of old
Thomas Shaw, one of the first of the Western
District squatters. He was a lovable old man,
in spite of a natural gruffness of manner. The
death was very sudden. Mr. Shaw had been
staying with his sister, Mrs. Dr. Fitchett. The
eldest son, Oliphant, and his smart young wife,
are in London.
The whole of old Thomas Shaw’s Australian
life was possessed by an undying feud with
another pioneer, Patrick M‘Farlane. M’Farlane
introduced the Vermont sheep ; Shaw snorted at
them, and called their wool hair. These Ver-
monts have proved perhaps the most successful
of Victorian and Riverina sheep, but, to his death
day, Shaw could not pass an argument without
gasping “It’s hair—not wool.” After M’Farlane’s
death the feud was carried on with Horsfall, of
the Riverina, and a wool talk meant flying fur
every time. Thomas Shaw’s hobby was Aus-
tralian merino sheep. These he bred and inter-
bred till their wool was of the finest, albeit the
animals were little bigger than ’possums.
“Blue Moon ” drifted regretfully off at the
Princess’s on Friday, arid “Lady Madcap”
bounded on for the first time on Saturday. To
judge by the life and “go” in her whirling, and
the enthusiasm of the audience, the management
will have to us.e force to drag that wilful young
lady away at the end of the promised fortnight.
The frocking on the stage was so fine and changed
so often that the brilliantly-dressed house
had neither time nor eyes for the summing-
up of the other cats’ frilleries. The Ghildni
swept on to a warm reception in a silk of fainting
blue, fortified and clasped and bulwarked with
brocaded ribbon in gold, and a slashing hat of
white wings. Later, at a regimental dance,
Mrs. Latham-Ghiloni drives two officers to duels
and distraction because of her charms, decked in
blazing sapphire and silver. She also carries the
unnecessary and fashionable dog, about the size
of a threepenny bit. The Madcap (Daisie
Wallace) doesn’t stay in one dress more than
three minutes at a time. When she is not Lady
Betty she masquerades in grey and apricot and
much white frill, as a light-toed maid, and anon
as a guardsman. Pretty Maud Thorne, always
well-dressed, follows the lead with a bewildering
number of frockings. One dream of misty rose
panne velvet drives her French lilac-clad lover
(even the men are caught in the dress fever), to
sing that her dress is “ as soft and sweet as her
blush.”
Will Ashton, the S. Australian youth who
came here with a large cargo of canvases, returns
with a small one and a large bag. When he has
replaced the sales of his Melbourne Exhibition
with new pictures, and (he modestly says) better
ones, he makes a descent upon Sydney.
An illustration out of a French novel ( edition
de luxe) materialised one day last week, and
walked Collins-street. She was a stranger in
Melbourne, a willow-slender slip of a girl, white-
clad, with a garden hat smothered in flowers, and
flowers tucked in her belt. Also she wore a grace-
ful, irresponsible air, as if she wandered in a
garden with no consciousness of the attention she
attracted. Melbourne beauties have an interest-
ing rival in the owner of this cameo face with the
“radiant pallor” of the Frenchwoman, accen-
tuated by coils of black plaited hair and eloquent
eyes of golden amber brown. Amazed blockers
chorus the question—Who is she? I intend to
find out. lam searching the Public Library to
discover which French pictured book she walked
out of.
The Cathedral Hall hangs out an arresting
placard, to announce that the “ Parsifal Quadrille
Club ” will meet on Thursday for its special dance.
The Parsifal Quadrille ! Does it expect to draw
Kundry from the Magdalen Convent Laundry
round the corner?
The visiting lacrosse team has had a gay old
week in Melbourne, and the Canuckian Redskin
shout ending with “ Hurraw, Hurraw, Hurraw!”
still echoes round the city and scares the passing
horse into the shop window. The local lacrosse
boys broke exuberantly into a ball on Monday
night at Hawthorn Town Hall, where the purple
of the Canadians and the green-and-gold of the
Australians flapped excitingly all round the walls.
The Chief Justice brought his dame, who was
dazzling in silver sequins in place of her usual
soft-colored frocking. Daughter Ruby muffled
herself up to the eyes in befeathered cloak all
the evening. There was a Mayoress or so, also
member Knox brought Mrs. Knox in startling
white-spotted black, and the fair and dainty Mrs.
Best toted her Senator to the frivol. There lie
cast off the burden of politics, and forgot the weary
hours he spends with his shoulder at the wheel of
the bogged Parliamentary chariot.
The Brewery M’Cracken chicks “debbed,” and
carried lacrosse designs. There were also two very
pretty sister blossomers from Benalla—where
Bland Holt’s fictitious “ Drought” uncle died.
Brighton hadits wedding of the year on Tuesday,
30th ult. Greta Caldwell joined herself in holy
captivity to the scholarly Jack Behan—or rather
Mr. JohnC. U. Behan, winner of the first Rhodes
scholarship for Victoria. The youthful bridegroom
was a Caulfield (Vic.) Grammar School boy.
He took a scholarship, and went to Melbourne
University. There he collected honors as some
boys collect stamps or beetles, and struck the
pinnacle of p, irne with the Rhodes scholarship.
The lily-fair bride (she is of the pale gold, sylph-
like type) is step-daughter to the Rev. J. Sinclair,
and owns quite an intellectual record of her own.
This marriage of intellects is the climax of a boy
and girl love affair. All youthful Brighton
turned out and hurled flowers, and the University
was solidly at the ceremony. The white-and-silver-
clad bride slipped into a mole-colored travelling
suit, and hurried off with her Jack for a Sydney
frivol before they leave for England, where Mr.
Behan has a lectureship at Oxford waiting for him.
This year’s dancing has brought an army of
callow youths on to the man-deserted scene of
frivol. The girls moan, but still roll up. Spoke
one wiseacre of twenty-two summers “ I enjoy
the dances, a good floor, good music, and, for con-
vention sake, something in the outlines of a man
to dance with, instead of another skirt. It is the
dancing I love—the joy of movement —and
these youths generally dance well. They are
at the dancing-class stage, and enthusiastic.
They have such baby faces and ask so
prettily for ‘the pleasure’—it is taught with
the steps —and they prattle so easily. I can give
myself up to the ecstasy of the waltz when I am
with them. There is no brain energy needed to
talk to the dears.” Then this ancient lady of
twenty-two summers sighs over “their innocent
little remarks sitting out between dances,” and
chortles about the way she beams on them when
she meets them in the street, so that she will be
high in favor at the next frivol. You see, the
eallowest youth has always one great recommenda-
tion. He grows up by degrees, and in his man-
hood he may possibly propose to take one of his
early partners off the shelf and marry her. For the
sake of that contingency lie’s worth encouraging.
An experienced charmer makes sad plaint:—
I’m four-and-twenty, and there’s no sign of my going
off this season. Indeed, the only women who are
achieving honors and husbands of their own are either
silly schoolgirls or over-wise widows. The schoolgirl is
fool enough to marry almost anyone who asks her ; the
wise widow is smart enough to make most people worth
having propose, and then she picks one with a decent in-
come. I think there ought to he a limit to repeated
matrimony. Once some women have enjoyed and rid
themselves of a seeond husband thej 7 get a mania for
matrimony. As for me, since I emerged from pinafores
and attained wisdom teeth, my adorers have been divided
into classes—eligibles who didn’t speak, ineligibles who
did, and absolute outsiders. The eligible men have hovered
round my flower-like self, with intentions in their eyes—
admiration, too. But their conscious superiority has
made their lips silent; except for cryptic compliments.
That sort of man knows that he can be happy till he’s
fifty, and then get a guileless girl-wife. The ineligibles
have exceeded their incomes in tram fares to our suburb,
and in postage stamps to carry their futile vows of
eternal worship and their offers of hungry matrimony.
They’re the men who make one’s life a burden as long as
they like one. They’re inspired with a thirst for excite-
ment, and they have the consoling certainty that their
divinities won’t be foolish enough to wed them, anyhow.
Spring-like weather makes good business for
the hilly boarding-houses, where a number of ad-
vanced club women make a practice of week-end-
ing in feminine batches. A youthful and swag-
ger dress-designer joined one bunch. The hill
hostel was in the far out-of-the-way, with a
special week-end train there and back. The
frocky one had asudden summons back. There was
no train on the sleepy Sunday. A visiting Senator
also wanted to return immediately. So the two
drove in a hired and jolting jinker many miles to
another railway. They arrived half-frozen. No
train for a couple of hours ; no hotel. There
was a store, and the kindly storekeeper made a
fire in the parlor, and then the pair of travellers
thawed. Into this Arcadia came another Mel-
bourne man who knew the Senator. He had come
up on some rigidly serious business “about land-”
He also raided the store. Now no amount of ex-
planation will satisfy him as to why the Senator
was spending Sunday with a charming girl in this
out-and-beyond.
Yours affectionately,
J OHANNA.

ual defence and peopling the Northern Territory,

Given tlm necessary mental oil, the addition of

not too many—genial moments with D.C.L. *

Last, year the N.S.W. railways carried over
41,000, and didn’t kill one of them.

Government was so angry that it sacked Oliver.
“ H.F.” offers a word of advice :—■
Your views on the White Australia question are riffht
as rain, but you flick us in the eye too persistently with
that little brown caudal appendage. Don’t you think it’s
bad policy, in view of possible future developments?
Should the small tawny one (i.e. much of him) ever ar-
rive with hostile intent forninst your shop, and your
shingle strike his eye, your disrespectful editor will
never know the nature of the cyclone that hits him, so
quickly will his jugular be squeezed by the aforesaid fly-
teaser—the while his sacred commissariat is being
hurtled over the Harbor by the simian’s hind claws.
Your esteemed chief doubtless desires to quit these
scenes in the usual orthodox manner—to have time for a
prayer, and to forgive God for creating Carruchers.
Therefore would it not be well to adopt a little of the
suaviter in modo, and not press the blucher so hard on
the animal’s yesterday afternoon ?
The “yesterday afternoon” is good, and will be
noted for future reference.
With a big struggle with the House of Fat
on hand, South Australian Ministers find them-
selves deluged with paltry questions as to why
there wasn’t a foot-warmer in some railway car-
riage or other, or why Moloney was appointed
poundkeeper at Bunkum Siding. The worst
sinners have been Labor members; and Price,
exasperated by their constant heckling, and the
triviality of the queries, has arched liis back and
made some heated Welsh remarks in reply.
Finally, he has invited them to move a vote of
no-confidence, if they are dissatisfied with his
administration —either that, or cease firing “silly
questions ”at the Government. Price has dis-
appointed some members of his party. They
thought they would have a nice tame Premier,
and his backbone is an unwelcome surprise to
them.
The troubles of the Standard Oil Trust
continue. The Illinois branch co. has just
been struck by Judge Ken. M. Landis
with a fine of nearly £6,000,000 for receiving
secret rebates from the Chicago and Alton rail-
road people. The Judge regretted that he could
not send J. D. Rockefeller and his associated
criminals to gaol. Unfortunately, the Octopus
has the right of appeal, and has exercised it, and
the case will have to be fought over again in the
Federal Court. This is about the seventh big fine
that has struck the Trust, through its various
subsidiary companies, during the last year or so;
but, up to date, it is not on record that any of the
States concerned have succeeded in recovering the
money. If they had all been paid, the Standard
Oil Trust would be a very shrunken Octopus,
whereas it seems as bloated and aggressive as
ever. Present appearances indicate that the
Trust trouble will continue to grow till somebody
has been executed, or till the American Constitu-
tion has been amended so that these vast concerns
may be nationalised. That is the cure. Inflicting
fines that cannot be collected is a waste of time.

There are something over a dozen reputable
Australian fire insurance offices ; but the N. S.
Wales Government helps to pay the dividends of
dl foreign concerns to which it gives its insur-
ances. It used to insure its own properties : but
Oarruthers abolished the department, and collared
the accumulated funds to use in making a surplus.
Tlie fact that the Australian cow industry
or heifer job has brought up with a shock against
the shipping combine, which, with the aid of its
mail subsidies, is in a position to dictate terms to
' le , ? ut *' er trade, has come as a horrid nightmare
to the crowd which, led by the anti-Australian
claily press, shouted with joy at the failure of the
Ueakin Government to inaugurate a mail service
outside the Combine. The additional fact that
the cow person put his hoof down with
emphasis, and said he wouldn’t pay the
increased freights, only to have to lift
Ins dispirited hoof up again and say he would
pay anything demanded, has worried other
mu e f S - W J"!° l ,e beve that it will be their turn next.
Ihe Anti-Sosh primary industries are now de-
manding not only a subsidised mail service, but a

Last week’s champion mean man turned
up in G. M. Merivale, president of the Sydney
Chamber of Commerce. He wants to hand the
.Northern Territory over to Britain, to be filled
with “the overflow’’—meaning, no doubt, the
scum—“of the teeming millions of India,” on
condition that Britain protects “our northern
coast.’’ As a matter of fact, Australia wouldn’t
then have any northern coast worth mentioning;
but let that pass. Would Merivale be quite content
to let a filthy crowd of “overflow ” nigger slaves
have the run of his kitchen and back premises,
and wander through the ground floor of his house,
and interfere with his family on the stairs, pro-
vided the owner of the slaves paid the hire of a
policeman to sit on the back doorstep. Well
Merivale might ? But Austral' has better ideas
about running a house than that.

loggerheads with the Adelaide City Council, and
the loggerheads do Price and his Ministers great
credit._ Ihe Council wanted a Bill put through,
authorising it to borrow something well up
towards £lOO,OOO for making footpaths and carry-
lng out some more of those permanent and repro-
ductive works that Cohen has heard so much
about; but Premier Price declared that he
wouldn’t help it through unless the Bill provided
fora one-ratepayer-one-vote referendum to directly
authorise the swindle. For, of course, it is a
swindle to borrow money to make footpaths with,
and then hand your children over a footpath with
holes in it and the original bill without any holes
in it at all.
