A London paper :
TheN.S.W. Premier now
comes forward with a
programme of reduced
taxation that may make
him popular with the
people of his own State,
but is certainly not re-
assuring to the British investor. The abolition of direct
taxation in N.S.W. at a time of unexampled prosperity
in the history of that country would be a reasonable
enough thing, if it were not for the immense public in-
debtedness of the State. We have always contended
that the assets of N.S.W. are so great as to justify the
borrowing- of this money for the development of its re-
sources. At the same time, a load of debt like this must
be shouldered, and not evaded. There is no reason why
the people of N.S.W. should not submit, in their present
prosperity, to some direct taxation in order to put a
better face upon the finances of the State. The reputa-
tion of N.S.W. and of all Australia would have been
better served by such a programme, and it is not a little
alarming to find this policy of truckling to the electors is
still so popular in N.S.W.
William Lyne has decided that in future
the label on a patent medicine must clearly state
the ingredients of the compound, and in so
deciding W. Lyne has struck a worthy idea. As
a case in point, the Customs Department lately
found 30 different drugs in one patent medicine.
They were all fairly good drugs, taken separately,
but thirty mixed medicines at once are as
dangerous as thirty mixed drinks.
IN HIS LATEST CHARACTER.
But tor any man to think that the wildest flights of
intellect would ever dethrone the Creator was to indulge
in mad visions of ignorance which would be dissipated as
the true inwardness of human life and human intelligence
became brighter and brighter.— G. Reid, at Salvarmy
meeting.
A deputation of iron-workers and boiler-
makers recently waited on Minister Lyne with a
question so simple yet so unusual that it made the
Minister stagger back several feet, and then sit
down with his hand to his aching brow. The iron
and boiler people wanted to know what is the use
of trying to create a ship-building industry in
Australia while ships, built by far cheaper foreign
labor, can be imported free ? Come to think of it,
what is the use ? The idea seems to suggest two
remedies. One is a4O per cent, duty on imported
ships. The other, and very much the better
remedy, is to enact that, except as regards ships
already under order, no new vessel whatsoever
shall be permitted to take a hand in the Aus-
tralian coastal trade, unless it be locally built.
Then the ship-building industry would wake up—
likewise the iron and steel industries, and a dozen
subsidiary trades, and Australia would become
able to build its own cruisers, iron-clads, torpedo-
boats, dredges, etc., and soon would hardly need
to care whether Britain held the sea or not.
Some of the State Frights people who
threw up their hats when the efforts of the Com-
monwealth to get a new line of big mail-steamers
running to England failed, are beginning to
wonder what sort of fools they were ; for
they find out that the present Combine is
something extra hard to bump against.
When the Victorian Butter Exports Com-
mittee tried to arrange freights, it dis-
covered that the price had been sent
up with a sudden bound. Then the
N.S.W. Butter Shippers’ Association
proposed that a contract should be made
with the mail cos., leaving the balance
of the trade open to competition among
the slower boats of the other lines. That
looked all right, until it was discovered
that the Combine was so real that
the mail cos. and nearly all the other
lines are standing together, and
aren’t taking any competition; and,
after hours of arguing, the matter hasn’t
been advanced a peg.
The exigencies of the Australian Immigra-
tion Act have produced a versatile type of officer.
For instance, the steamer Yorck arrived from
Europe at Fremantle (W.A.), the other day, and
the official interpreter got to work upon the mot-
ley crowd of passengers.. He began with some
Frenchmen, went on with some Italians, pro-
ceeded with some Greeks, continued with some
Syrians, followed on with an Arab, and finished
up with a Bulgarian who spoke Turkish. Then
he leaned over the rail, and spoke some good,
plain Australian about the unnecessary abundance
of the Dago upon the face of the earth.
Federal Attorney-General Groom lately
gave the straight answer that turneth away bosh.
Spruce Myth, with his anxious eye fixed on the
dignity of Commerce, asked Groom if he would
submit a draft of the proposed Bankruptcy Bill
to Sydney Chamber of Commerce, and secure its
approval before he submitted the thing to Parlia-
ment. Groom looked upon Mr. Myth, and said
in a voice like Irvine’s best icicle that he wouldn’t.
While Spruce was recovering from his swoon,
Page wanted to know in whose interests the Bill
was being introduced. “In the interests of the
people of Australia! ” replied Groom. There were
no more questions.
“ Western Line ” writes :
At a meeting- of the quarter-million North Coast
Steamship Co. (N.S.W.) “ strong references ” were made
to the Government’s “systematic neglect of its obligations
to keep the waterways of the northern rivers open to
reasonably safe navigation.” Where did these alleged
obligations come from? If the State didn’t dig up any
sand at all, navigation by some sort of vessel would still be
possible ; so the alleged obligation evidently consists in
digging up enough sand to suit this particular co.’s
boats and to earn this particular co.’s dividends.
Then if some other co. wants to run bigger
boats, the Government will presumably be still “obliged”
to dig up enough sand to let that co.’s boats pass, too.
The writer of this lives somewhere out on the dusty-
western line, and he would often rather go home by boat
than by train ; and the Government is under an obliga-
tion to dig out enough dirt to let a ferry-boat travel that
way, and the Government’s neglect to do this obvious
duty is disgraceful. True, the Government has provided
a railway ; but, then, it is providing railways up on the
North Coast as well, and the fact that it has made rail-
ways there, and is going to make more, apparently doesn’t
affect this alleged remarkable obligation to dig up sand
and make rivers to knock the railways out.
Thus “ Thomas Clodd ” :
Maoriland post-office servants have an Appeal Board,
which sits once a year and dreams over things. Two
heavily-gilt bureaucrats and two cheap clerks compose
the Board. The clerks are alarmed at the importance of
the gilt members, and the whole are scared oftheP.M.-
General, who has the power of veto on the Board’s “ find-
ings.” All appeals must be sent in writing, and officers
who began writing ten years ago are still shedding ink on
the Board. Nothing has ever been finally settled by the
Board since it began its farcical career. The M.L. postal
service seethes with discontent, but the dissatisfied have
merely the privilege of stating their grievance to a body
which has no power of redress, and which is as useful
| to them as a silk blouse is to a scrub steer. The de-
' fections from the service are so constant that the
Maoriland P.O. will shortly have to warn correspond-
ents to break themselves of the foolish habit of letter-
writing.
Thus a person called Littlejohn (not
Littlemind) to Sydney Chamber of Commerce :
The Federal Parliament .... had passed laws which
made criminals of merchants, and other legislation, in-
stead of looking after matters such as the Federal
capital.
Quite true. The Australian Parliament has
passed laws which make criminals of merchants
who import brass jewellery marked as gold, and
goods described as all wool that are really half
cotton. Will Mr. Littlejohn come out with a
plain statement that he objects to the Commerce
Act, and that he is on the side of the swindling
merchants who live on lies and false descriptions,
and who simply rank with the three-card sharp
and the passer of bogus cheques ?
Sydney Chamber of Commerce, at its
recent annual meeting, showed the cloven hoofs
and tail of a scheme to rob N.S.W. The Aus-
tralian capital, according to the Constitution,
belongs to that large part of N.S.W. outside the
100-mile limit from Sydney. The Chamber of
Commerce seriously discussed a scheme for rob-
bing that part of N.S.W. for the benefit of one
city by shifting the capital to Sydney. This is
the first scheme for robbing N.S.W. that has been
heard of since the Commonwealth was established,
and the country should keep an eye on it, and, if
necessary, holler for the police.
Things move a little. The Bill providing
for the survey of the much-needed railwav to
Westralia has passed the Senate, and that "is a
small step towards the work of building the two
great lines that are to cross Australia from east to
west and from north to south. One of the joys
of the debate was the position of the three new
Queensland Senators, Sayers and Cliataway and
Sledger. They won their election on purely local
grab principles by advocating a wild scheme for
diverting the Northern Territory railway into
Queensland, and their inability to get the ear of
the House, and make it understand that the only
Federal railway idea worth thinking about is the
one that they shove along in their little wheel-
barrow, has made them as wild as three bears
sitting in a row.

After all Willie-motor Kelly’s screams
about the proposed Australian navy being “a
useless torpedo flotilla,” “a ridiculous mosquito
fleet,” etc., etc., the First British Sea Lord rises
unkindly to imply that Willie doesn’t know what
he is talking about, and informs the British House
of Lords that an Australian “well-manned flotilla
of destroyers and submarines would be a very
substantial defence against casual raids, and would
also be of the greatest assistance to the British
fleet in the event of war.” Thus does the British
authority sit on Willie, who thought he was being
“so English,” and prove himself a better Aus-
tralian than Willie himself.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]

Australia Speaks.
By traitors base it’s yelled aloud
Until it gets upon our nerves—
The poaching by the Federal crowd
Upon the injured States’ preserves ;
“ Injustice,” “ tyranny,” and “ wrong,"
The long array of fancied “ slights,”
That wring the anguished “ Lord, how long?”
But what about Australia’s Righ ts l
The State Frights Speak.
On patience we have long relied
To turn her taunts and jeering cries,
Unselfishly we’ve vainly tried
To point out where her duty lies ;
Her “ flagrant insults ” daily grow,
Her “ veiled oppression ” sorely bites,
We only want to run the show—
Australia Interposes.
Say, what about Australia’s Rights l
The State Frights Yell.
She wants to build a capital
On land that’s, after all. her own,
And, with unmitigated gall,
She wants to choose the site alone.
And now—ye narrow-minded, pause,
And tear your hair, provincial mites—
She wants to make Australia’s laws
Australia Speaks.
Well, what about Australia’s Rights 1
Chorus of State Ghosts.
She tries our “ dignity ” to flout —
We needs must take a second place—
She wants to keep the nigger out
And raise a clean Australian Race.
We’ll lift Secession’s mud-stained rag,
We puny, whom her strength excites,
And tear our stars from off her flag—-
Who cares about Australia’s Rights ?

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Australia engaged in three international con-
tests on Saturday, and with hideous luck lost every one
of them. On the Parramatta River, Webb, the Maori-
lander, took the sculling championship of the world and
£5OO of Australian money ; at Brisbane the All Blacks
beat the Australian Rugbyites by 14 points to five ; and
at Adelaide the Canadians downed the Australian
laerossers by six goals to three. J t was a dreary day.
Of course, the crowning disaster was Webb’s
victory. That took from Australia the proud possession
of the sculling championship of the world. Australia's so-
called champion had been elevated to the position, not on
his merits, but because his elder brother stepped down
from it—it is said to oblige a lady of the family, who
wished to have two world-champions in her flock. The
bogus champion was hopelessly outclassed and decisively
beaten. Success is the only thing that can justify such a
transaction as the passing on of the championship from
George to Charles Towns, and when Charles was put to
the proof there was no success in him. A championship
of any kind is something of a national possession, and
should be held by the man best able to defend it. In
this case it wasn’t. It was defended by a man who got it
because an obliging relative forfeited it to him, and the
relative might have forfeited in the same fashion, if he
had liked, to a middle-aged uncle with one lung. Aus-
tralia has a right to think tha*t it has been treated .in a
scurvy fashion by George Towns, and that the country’s
aquatic fame was discreditably sacrificed to petty family
vanity.
Curiously enough there was a general impression
that Webb’s chances were hopeless, or that, at the best,
he had only his own and Buckley’s. So confident were
the followers of Towns that they cheerfully laid six to four,
and they fell in with a tremendous splash. Although the
race was rowed under ideal conditions, the weather of the
morning and early part of the afternoon scared thousands
away, so that there was nothing like the usual crowd
that flocks to see a championship event on the Parra-
matta. After a considerable mob had collected, the
wind grew cold, and an angry rain swished down, soak-
ing those on the shore who could not get into the shelter
of caves and ledges. About 3.30 the trouble ceased
suddenly, leaving an opalescent sky brooding over a scene
of elysian calm. Cool atmosphere, and smooth water—
what more could a sculler require or desire? Up
at Ryde bridge they got away to a beautiful
start, and Towns jumped to the front and made
the pace. He made it for a mile, with Webb holding him
comfortably, and then, after getting so close to one
another that their sculls touched, Webb went away to
the front, and the race resolved itself into a procession.
At Bedlam Point, Webb was leading by eight lengths,
and the end of the race was close in sight. Off Abbots-
ford the Maorilander deliberately slowed down, and with
his eyes fixed on the gamely struggling Towns, paddled
home an easy winner by five lengths. The time (20miii.
45sec.), although faster than that in which Towns beat
Durnan (22min. 27sec.) on the Nepean, was slower than
the times of Stanbury (19min. 47sec.) and Towns (19min.
53Jsec.), when they won the championship in 1905 and
1906 respectively. Quite obvious that had Webb been
pushed he could have travelled much faster.
After the way the daily papers had decried
Webb’s style, people were astonished to see his clean,
easy movements, and from the boats scores exclaimed
that the man in blue was Towns : “ Only Towns could
pull like that! ” And they cheered accordingly. It was a
sad revelation. Webb was cool as a cucumber. When
people started to congratulate him, he cheerfully pro-
posed that they should wait till the protest was decided.
Umpire Rush did not decide the point right away,
but carefully referred to the rules, and gave a judg-
ment that was crammed full of horse-sense. The
people who place stock in omens regarded Webb’s chance
as hopeless on account of the attempted suicide on the
voyage from Maoriland, and subsequent death in Sydney,
of one of his chief backers. On the other hand others
discovered that what lost Towns the race was a minia-
ture Union Jack hoisted upside down on his shed. This
is the signal of distress in the language of the sea.
George Towns announced that he would not challenge
the winner. The next race for the sculling champion-
ship will take place on the Wanganui River (Maoriland),
and will be the first in that State.
* * * *
Webb’s chief backer, Tuck, landed the squeal-
ing Towns crowd a nasty one at the handing over’of the
stakes. One of Towns’ backers declared he wasn’t satis-
fied with the umpire’s decision. He offered Webb £lOO
exes, and guaranteed him agate of £250 to race Towns
again. Tuck sprang up before Webb could g'et himself
together, and told the man who made the offer that he
considered it an insult to the umpire—which it was.
Webb had won the championship, and if Towns thought
lie had not been beaten on his merits he could come to
Wanganui and win it back in the ordinary way. Whereat
there was a great silence in the Towns corner.
“Wanganui” inquires: “Taking for granted
that Webb was the better man on the day, and also that
a foul did occur, accidentally, intentionally, or other-
wise, was the umpire's decision correct?” Well,
whatever experts other offices may contain, there
is nobody in this one who knows more about scull-
ing than Michael Rush. The umpire did not
decide hastily, but having noted the occurrence very
carefully consulted the newly-framed rules, and de-
cided that Webb in no way deprived Towns of his
chance of winning the race. Towns’ statement that he
did not try after the foul reads very badly. If he wasn’t
trying off Bedlam Point and thence to the finish, writer
never saw a man try.
» * * *
The lacrosse match at Adelaide was a great
struggle, and, although beaten, the Australians played a
wildly exciting game. But it was useless against the
tactics of the Canadians, who massed their men in the
middle of the field, and carried the ball down the centre
with their amazing system of short passes. This left the
Australians hovering round the flanks as the French
cavalry hung round the squares at Waterloo. When the
Australians did get poss“ssion of the leather they found a
solid phalanx waiting for them in front of the goal. What
really beat the Australians was lack of combination. This
w’as no doubt partly due to lack of practice together, a
drawback which was quite unavoidable.
* * » «
The last of the three international matches was
the Rugby struggle at Brisbane. This did not excite any
particular enthusiasm, the disreputable tactics of the
Maorilanders having killed interest in their doings. The
men who compose the team are not good sportsmen. As
before mentioned, Maoriland won by 14 points to five.
Some sanguine souls thought that when Messenger went
north it would make a difference ; and it did, but even
the mighty Messenger can’t prevail against the finesse of
the Maoriland off-siders. The only man who can cope
with that is the umpire.
<* * *■ *
Sydney got a shock on Saturday when it was
noised abroad, “ on the best authority,” that Messenger
and three other of the finest plavers of Eastern Suburbs
would go to England with the “ Maoriland ” Professional
Rugby team. If it is true, it will just about wreck
“ East’s ” applecart and seriously damage the Australian
XV. Somehow, writer has his doubts about Messenger
or any of the Australians going to England. All the
same, it seems pretty certain that- professional Rugby
will be in full blast in Sydney by next season, unless
there is a sudden and remarkable change in public
opinion. The Metropolitan Rugby Union lias been dis-
playing wealth of late, buying new ground, for which it
wrote a cheque of £15,000. And the players are realising
that the Union gets all the loot, while tliey have a mono-
poly of the bVuises and kicks, and the abuse of the critics.
Fired by the spectacle of 50,000 odd people gathered
at Sydney Cricket Ground to watch the Maorilanders
play N.S.W., sundry showmen suddenly caught the idea
of starting professional Rugby in Sydney. When the
grounds have been secured, it is proposed to form
district clubs, in which the players will share the
gate, paying the management a certain rate of
ihterest. After the interest is paid, all profits shall be
divided equally between players and management.
First-grade Rugby continued in Sydney on
Saturday afternoon, when Eastern Suburbs broke up the
formidable South Sydney crowd by six points to three at
Sydney Cricket Ground. Both teams showed good work
in attacking, but the Eastern Suburbs’ defensive work
was too good for the others. University got Newtown
out at University Oval, and slew it in a cold spot by 12
points to five. The Oval was wet and slippery when play
started, but after a heavy shower of rain it became a
quagmire, in which the University men showed some
brilliant dribbling rushes which just sw’amped Newtown.
Glebe also caught its rival, Sydney, out in the lonely
Wentworth Park Oval, and did for it with brutal thorough-
ness, the figures being eight to nil. The corpse is there
yet. Sydney never had a show. It was just rolled
on the wet, muddy field, and trodden on. North
Sydney hillmen proved too strong for Western Suburbs
;by six points to three, the Westerners playing one man
short the whole of the first half. With a full team in the
second half the club held its own against the hillmen,
but could not score, and the points that had already been
made against it were fatal. In the unfortunate St. George
men Manly found a team of its own weight, and waded
through it in great style. An exciting game ended in
the downfall of the Saint by 14 points to 10.
This left Glebe still at the head of the com-
petition with 18 points, but reduced the second line to
two, University and Eastern Suburbs with 17 each.
South Sydney (15), Sydney (14), Newtown and Western
Suburbs (11 each), North Sydney (10), and the rest
(nowhere), follow in due order.
Thi3 means that the “ rest ” —Balmain, St.
George and Manly—are out of the competition, which
now remains with the eight above-mentioned. The play-
off will commence on August 17, tbe day the Maoriland
Professional team is to split into two and play the first of
three matches in Sydney at the Agricultural Ground.
Sydney Sunday Times has risen groggily to remark that
the financial success of the professional matches is
extremely doubtful. Is it? The chances are, more the
pity, that the public will rush to see the new thing, and
if they have bumpergates for the whole three matches the
people who are trying to arrage for professionalism here
will get a lot of encouragement.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby team’s career in Westralia was a
royal progress. The Perth Metropolitan team was beaten
by 17 to nil; the Goldfields by 16 to eight ; and the two
Westralian teams were beaten by 16 to three and three to
nil respectively. The low scoring in the last match was
due to wet, slippery ground. Also the wet state of the
N.S.W players internally is understood to have affected
them.
* * * *
In the Association struggle for the Rawson Cup
(Sydney) Pyrmont is still leading with 23 points to. its
credit. Tbe nearest rival is Glebe with 19, after which
come Balmain with 17, Rozelle with 16, Ultimo with 10,
Petersham with 10, and The Navy with five. The surprise
of Saturday was the way Balmain, playing with only nine
men, managed to prevent Glebe scoring. Under the cir-
cumstances a scoreless draw was highly creditable. It is
to be hoped the Balmain captain used some bad words to
the two men who failed to either turn up or send sub-
stitutes. Rozelle and Ultimo made a very savage struggle
of it. Rushes came fast from either side, and some
excellen t kicking was shown, but Rozelle bad the more
versatility and ran out with five goals to two. The Navy,
with its tail down, forfeited to Pyrmont; the prospect of
playing the premiers was too appalling. The result is
that Pyrmont has won the premiership for the fourth
time, and will hold the Cup.
• » * *
Whispered that the Sydney Agricultural
Ground will riot be available for the N.S.W. League’s
football next year, and the ground difficulty threatens to
interfere with the progress of the Australian game in
Sydney very sadly. Which is a pity, as the game is not
getting a fair fight for its life. When the clubs play on
open spaces like Moore Park, not only are they unable to
charge for admission and raise revenue, but they
find it hard to keep hogs from walking across their
playing space. Sydney badly wants more sports grounds.
The followers of Vic. football pretty well filled
the ground at Prince’s Oval on Saturday, to witness the
discussion between the two topmost clubs—Carlton and
Collingwood. This was conducted in quite an academic
fashion, and would have been an almost historical argu-
ment but for two things—the wind had a little too much
say in the matter, and the umpire much too little. He was
a most disinterested umpire ; even when a player tripped
another headlong before his eyes he merely viewed the
episode as something that might have happened in
Timbuctoo. In spite of these drawbacks the game
was good and exciting till the middle of the
last quarter, when Carlton, with a 3-points
lead, kicked a neat goal, and followed that up with an
attack that resulted in two more behinds, making the
scores 45 to 34. The Blues’ victor was mainly due to
splendid high marking, andto their having.picked up the
strenuous finish that ' the Magpies had apparently mis-
laid. At the East Melbourne ground Fitzroy lost its last
hope of getting into the semi-finals—lost it in the
last quarter of the game against Essendon. At
lemon time the ’Roys had scored 31 points to 29,
and, with the wind now behind them, felt quite easy :
but in that last quarter, the’Dons showed sornething'of
that splendid play which, a few years back, won the club
the premiership four seasons consecutively, and just
doubled their score while allowing the Maroons not a
single added point—sB to 31. It was a fine exhibition,
but heart-breaking for the losers. “Saints” continued
its downward career, the kick this time being adminis-
tered by Melbourne—47 to 30. And Geelong, afterflatter-
ing its supporters by leading throughout from South,
went down in the f last 10 minutes—42 to 50...
In the Association, Footscray practically
ruined its hopes of cutting up the semi-final gates by
knuckling under to Essendon (A.), by 11 points to 43. As
the match to which this was the return had been won by
’Scray by one point, after a strenuous battle, and as there
was no apparent weakness about the team, the barrackers
were much disgusted at the conclusion—also very out-
spoken. Brunswick’s combination gave it all the best of
the play against WiUiamstown, but, alas! its for-
wards couldn’t kick straight, and the victory that
would have astonished many was missed—by three
goals 13 behinds to six goals three behinds. West
beat Preston handily, and Richmond “ had a wifi.”
Nowadays Richmond mostly seems to “ have a .win ”
which is a pleasing habit for a team to get into, though
this paper is prepared to admit, upon argument, that it is
hardly feasible for every team to contract said habit.
During tbe afternoon a couple of Junior Association
teams —Carlton and Fitzroy—playing on the ’Roy ground,
got into holts, and temporarily suspended the game
while players and barrackers fought a strenuous battle
of several hundred rounds. Coburg and Footscray
Juniors, of the same association, were to have met on
the latter’s ground, but the former forfeited rather than
risk, it alleged, a repetition of the treatment handed it
when the teams met previously, on which occasion the
argument was conducted with fence palings, road metal,
etc. These juniors ought to pass some such regulation
as that of the Girls’ Hockey Club—“ In future no player
shall get unduly excited.”
* * • *
The Dunlop Co. promoted a hill-climbing
contest at Heidelberg (Melb.) recently up a slope of
1050yds., with an average gradient of one in eight, and
one in six and a half at the pinch. Over 120 riders com-
peted, and the affair was run off in 22 heats of five or six
riders. The co. had laid a wire up the hill, and a bang on
the gong announced to the time-keeper at the top the
second of departure. . The fastest man was A. W. Moore,
who got up in 2min. 26 3-ssec. He used 74 gear and 6iin.
cranks. It’s very interesting, but darned bad for the
cyclists. A man who rides up a hill like that is a fool—
it is about the best way writer knows of straining the
heart.
Senhouse, theN.S.W. disqualified cyclist, dis-
covered that the arm of Mclntosh’s League was long
enough to reach to the uttermost parts of the U.S.A.
and the isles thereof. When he forwarded his little
entry, the National Cycling Association kicked him with
both feet, and biffed him right out. After the Associa-
tion had kicked him, the press rubbed it in like this :
“Outlaw comes far for Nought! Travels from Aus-
tralia to race in America, but the Hand of Punishment
reaches across the Weary Leagues of Sea 1 ” Very flowery,
but darned nasty.
* * • «
.At last word begins to filter through about the
rearguard of the Pekin to Paris motor-car squadron.
Jean du Tallis, the press rep. aboard the 15-h.p. Spyker
car, tells of terrible experiences. They had reached the
middle of Gobi, and after leaving a village named Pon-
kong, the Spyker took most of the heavy luggage of the
Contal tri-car, while the latter took the emergency supply
of petrol. The Spyker soon left the de Dions and the tri-
car far behind, while Prince Borghese, the brilliant Dago,
was raging two days ahead. The road was marked by
the line of bones left by the people who had perished
there through the crumbling ages. The bumping
over skeletons and such suddenly split the petrol tank,
and before the motorists knew it the motive power
was all in the sand. When the De Dions came along they
could only spare four gallons, which was not nearly
enough to take the Spyker to the next petrol dep6t, 150
sandy, weary miles away. The last straw was the news
that the tri-car, with the emergency petrol abpard, un-
able to force its way through the sand, had turned back
for Pekin. Godard and Tallis found themselves in the
middle of Gobi with one chicken, a few biscuits,
and a very small quantity of water between
them. The De Dions promised to send assistance.
But it was something like six days before it came,
and in the meantime the motorists nearly died of starva-
tion and thirst. The intense heat cracked their lips and
peeled the skin from their faces, and most of the Mongo-
lians to whom they applied for assistance stared at them
stolidly, and rode on unmoved. Fortunately, they found
a somewhat more sociable crowd, which supplied them
with food, and, for a price, commenced to haul the car
with camels. They hauled it till they met the Mongo-
lians from Udde with the petrol. After the petrol arrived
the bones along the track looked quite ordinary—pre-
viously they had had a very sinister expression about
them. )
• * * *
The American motor-engine manufacturers
have decided to adopt a national standard for
horse-power, and are following the lead of the
“ effete and played-out ” Royal Automobile Club,
of England. Imagine the Yankee following Eng-
land ! Here is the formulae: Square the diameter
of the cylinder in inches, multiply by number
of cylinders, and divide by 2.5, and the result is the
standard horse-power any standard engine will develop
at a normal speed of 1000 revolutions per minute. The
Motor Boat Club of N.S.W. ought to adopt this, and
compel all owners to submit their engines for measure-
ment. Unless some of our crack boating men are pre-
varicators, we have several boats here which, for speeds
developed on low horse-power, beat everything in the
other four continents.
* • * *
Doubtful if Major Taylor will be seen on the
Australian tracks again. He has got fat inside, and can-
not get into condition, and consequently his European
appearance has been a dreadful frost.
* * * -*-
Maoriland Cricket Council is preparing for the
coming season in an almighty bad temper. The visit of
the Marylebone team was a howling success from the
standpoint of the team, but the M.L. Council finds itself
burdened with a deficit of over £lOOO, which will
probably be dropped heavily on to the shoulders of the
guarantors. The loss itself is bad enough, but it is con-
sideration of the items that makes the M.L.' Council go
on the drink. Washing and “ tips,” £4B Bs. 3d. ; hotel
exes., £457 175.; and the cost of the trips between
matches to Rotorua, the West Coast, and up the Wanga-
nui River, are the items that have made up the deficit.
The Council feels that it has been had. The team came
out to play cricket, and in between times formed itself
into a free touring party, all exes, paid by the Council,
and this the Council considers is stretching things a lot.
Any man who wants free board and residence in a com-
fortable hospital for a few months only requires to call
round to the Council’s office and ask when the next
English XI. will arrive.
* * * *
Failing Jackson and Fry, the London M.C.C.
has fallen back on A. O. Jones as captain of the coming
English cricket team. His position as successful captain
of the champion county (Notts) evidently got him the
job. Jones has been in Australia before with Maclaren’s
team, when his failure to strike his admitted form was a
disappointment. He was actually omitted from the test
matches. The new captain is a “ ladies’ reserve darling.”
Last visit he fielded at long-on, at the reserve end,
always without a hat, and with a snowy silk hanky folded
round his lily-white neck. From hanky to shoes he was
painfull}' white ; he was even careful to avoid wearing
the team’s colors, lest they should blot the white-
ness. He also mashed the girls during adjournments
without a hat. A beautiful and hatless vision was Jones.
* * • •
The Vic. Amateur Turf Club commenced its
G. N. Steeplechase meeting at Caulfield on Saturday,
under the critical survey of Excellency Northcote arid
staff. Seventeen started for the Moondah Plate, for
which an intelligent army of punters made Uro favorite,
at five to two against. The son of Havoc justified the
faith that was in him, and streaked home a length to the
good. Naturally, the interest was centered on the Grand
National Hurdle Race, of 1200 sovs. But the backers did
not find time tedious, as the Wilgah Steeplechasers’ Flat
Race saw the popular fancy, top-weight Anchor, win
by four lengths. Firelight and Prestonpans dropping
out, the G.N. Hurdles field was reduced to 10. The public
pinned its fancy to The Wizard early, and he was soon
backed down to 7 to 2, a price which he shared with his
rival favorite, Bribery. In spite of a series of mishaps, the
last of which caused The Wizard to strip his leg to the
bone from the hock downwards, he managed to hold the
fast-running Dog of War, who looked dangerous right
through, and led the way over the last obstacle. At the
half-distance they were howling “ Dog of War,” but The
Wizard soared up alongside, and won easily by nearly a
length. After weighing in, The Wizard went into hos-
pital.
* * * *
Last week A.J.C. committee went further in
their whitewashing of the “ unregistered,” and gathered
in several prominent pony “ books,” who, consequently,
looked on at Ascot meeting while their late brother
Moses literally raked the shekels in. More than one
cleared £3OO on the day, and all. had big- credit balances.
But Fate rubbed it in hard indeed, when, fielding for the
first time under the changed conditions, at Canterbury
horse meeting, the new recruits struck a particularly bad
day, and, without exception, lost heavily.
Some possibilities for coming A. J.C. Epsom, as
evidenced by form at Canterbury (Sydney) Saturday, are
Forge and Mimer. Forge won the Flying Handicap with
top weight (10.8), and Mimer secured'the Stewards’ Mile
with something to spare and lOst. on his back. Their
weights in the Epsom are : Forge, 8.13 ; Mimer, 8.1.
N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Ass. has shifted the
route of the State cross-country championship from
Manly to Flemington. The course originally a] proved of
was an ideal one to try the stamina of the athletes—that
is, from the viewpoint of those who spied out the land ;
but there was a mighty kick at the prospect of climbing
rocks and ploughing through sand, so the scene was
shifted away to the scrubby land of Flemington. One
time the A. A. A. wouldn’t sanction a route that didn’t end
on a racecourse, where the best girl could cheer her worn-
out wreck as he toiled home first; but nowadays they
take on runs that would break the heart of a motor bike
and fill an automobile with blank despair.
* * * «
Billy McColl, one time heavy champion of Aus-
tralia, lias had a suspicion for some time that he is a
back number. The fact that he only lasted five rounds
against ex-Maorilander Jimmy Griffin in Melbourne, last
Saturday night, convinced him that there are grounds
for the suspicion, and very wisely William steps out of
the ring for good.
* • • •
Boxing and wrestling championships, promoted
by the Victorian Amateur Athletic Association, put in
five prosperous nights at the Masonic Hall (Melb.).
Financially the A.A.A. extirpated all previous records.
The biffing and catch-hold wrestling drew an entry
that just missed three figures—the best up to date.
Generally speaking, the boxing was more of the slather
and whack order than usual, though several smart
experts came to light in the bantam and feathers.
Clearly the small weight division is the more popular
from the entry and spectacular point of view.
The light men do no damage worth mentioning
in proportion to the heavier exponents, and though an
occasional knock-out occurs, it does not convey that
dull sense of a fall of meat that strikes you when a
middle or heavy hits the boards. The light fast men
were the feature of the amateur contests in both boxing
and wrestling. Roy Ralph out-pointed Gibby Hobbs
after three rounds of shifty and dodgeful work—the con-
test making the third year of success for Roy. Williams,
a feather from Ballarat, went clean through his herd
of opponents, only one man standing more than half a
round before his noble punch. He defeated Watson
in the final with one hand, his right being gone before he
entered the ring. Harold Gray secured the bantam
championship with a good, forceful left that persisted in
pushing' t’other fellow’s face sideways and all over the
shop. Denis Herlihy (middles) outlasted Frank Brown over
three rounds, and won. Heavyweight Ernie Lafranchi
(13st. 21b.) obtained the decision over practically a middle,
Val Quirk (list. 101 b.), who, by the way, would keep
Snowy Baker more than busy. The awful knock-out
occurred in the welter division, where Hollis landed
Reddaway a very definite clout in the second round, and
ended that section of the argument right there. The
’rastlers showed decided improvement, with the
exception of heavyweight Hugh Gunter (13st. 21b.).
He found it difficult to settle Sohemnitz, a
middle-weight, who managed to overturn the b'g
fellow in a surprising manner. Gunter, however,
ultimately secured two falls, and won. Eric Taylor, a
lithe, wiry youngster, won the “light,” by defeating a
muscular, but by no means clever, exponent in W. D.
Adams. After toiling for 25 minutes, middles Smith and
McDonnell were called off, otherwise they might be still
at it. Meantime illness has settled “ Mac,” and Smith gets
the glory. Joe Stokesbury decided the boxing bouts,
and Charlie Cunningham perspired considerably looking
for falls, while between contests announcer O’Hara
Wood mostly talked, and did it well.
* * *, «
A sarcastic brute writes from Melb.: “Vic-
torian Amateur Athletic Competitions committee threw
the dead cat of scorn at several intending pugilis ic com-
petitors who laid claims to amateur status, and then, as
usual, employed the professional referee, Joe Stokesbury,
to adjudicate on the amateurs. Joe, always the puffick
gen’l’man, whispered words of wisdom to competitors,
and said ‘ Shakans ’ and ‘ Boxon ’ nicely, but some-
how the crowd didn’t seem satisfied. F’rinstance,
in the bantam final Joseph said Gray beat Boyd,
and the crowd made noises at him, and violently
said Boyd beat Gray. Someone wanted to get up
and explain that Gray’s teacher is McGowan, who
is Joe’s pal; but was suppressed. In the heavies, Lafranchi
fought Quirk, and Stokesbury said Lafranchi took the
plum, and the crowd again dissented thoroughly, and
another man wanted to explain that Joseph trains Bill
Lang, and Lafranchi is Bill’s brother, despite the dis-
similarity in names ; but someone took a grip of his coat-
tail and hauled him down. The result was that many
people left holding their noses, and declaring there was a
dead lobster under the floor.”
* * * •
Hockey and hurling are practically the same
game, and both have their followings in Sydney. Seems
a pity that they do not all amalgamate, and form them-
selves into electorate clubs, and organise a first and second
grade competition. Under proper conditions the game
would simply boom, being amongst the most exciting on
earth.

The Iron Industry—No. 1.
The Country that wouldn’t take a
Straight Tip.
In 1868 the United States offered Australia the
“straight tip,” and Australia didn’t take it. For
some years before that intermittent efforts had
been made to set up an iron-smelting industry in
N. S. Wales, and they had mostly failed at a very
early stage. But in that year 1868 the directors
of the Fitzrov works, on the road between Sydney
and Goulburn, sent away to San Francisco a few
small shipments of their product; one lot ran to
140 tons. And from MacCondray and Co., of
’Frisco, came back this advice : “ The parcels of
your pig-iron received here have given great satis-
faction. We can safely say that we can dis-
pose of from 200 to 300 tons per month, and
that it would command from two to four
dollars per ton more than any other iron
imported into California.” That was in 1868.
At that time the production of pig-iron in the
United States amounted to about 1,400,000 tons a
year, and the furnaces turned out under 50,000
tons of steel. In 1905 the same country turned
out 22,992,380 tons of pig-iron, according to the
Statesman’s Year Book (1907); and the output
of iron and steel and their products was valued at
2,176,739,726 dollars, or, say, £45,140,000. Thus
in less than 40 years, starting from the time the
United States told Australia that it was capable
of producing finer pig-iron than any that was
then available in the Californian market, the
United States production has increased nearly
16-fold. And even that remarkable progress does
not represent all the progress. In IS6B, when
America offered Australia the straight tip before
mentioned, the output of United States steel,
made out of the pig-iron and opening up vast
secondary industries, was under 50,000 tons per
year. In 1905 the Bessemer steel plants alone had
a capacity of ingots or direct castings of 41,448
tons per day , and the open-hearth steel plants
had a daily capacity of a further 34,243 tons.
# 4e * tr
But while this extraordinary progress was
taking place in the United States, what of Aus-
tralia, the country which had been told that its
actual product was better than any on the Cali-
fornian market ? It is a sorry history, almost too
disgraceful to repeat. That the local product was
the best in the Australian market, just as it had
been declared to be the best in the Californian
market, most Australian engineers admitted.
At about that time the big block of buildings in
Pitt-street, Sydney, known as Vickery’s Cham-
bers, were being planned ; and, after ample tests,
this same Fitzroy iron was adopted for use in the
girders ; and the result has stood the proof of time
just as it then stood the proof of the engineer and
the chemist. But the Australian manufacturer
had to grope his way, and while he got no Protec-
tion from his own Government as he groped, he
got plenty of kicks from outside. The expe-
rience of the Australian ironmaster was the expe-
rience of the. early American ironmaster over
again, varied slightly to meet the new times and
the altered circumstances. Mulhall puts into a
few words what that experience was :
The first regular foundry in North America was that
established by Joseph Jenks, in 1663, at Lynn, Massa-
chusetts. There were six existing in the Colonies in 1750,
when the British Parliament passed a law to close all
mills, forges, or furnaces in the Colonies, the better to
protect British manufactures. After the Independence,
this branch of industry made progress, but soon, col-
lapsed owing to the influx of British merchandise.
The British Parliament no longer needs to pass
a law “to close all mills, forges, or furnaces
in the colonies.” The same thing, as America
afterwards discovered, can be accomplished by
other means. When the United States, after the
Independence, had got beyond the power of the
British Parliament, the industry, nevertheless,
soon collapsed, “owing to the influx of British
manufactures.” Then the United States learned
something else. It learned to stop that influx
and give its own infant a chance. The output of
£45,140,000 worth of iron and steel and their pro
ducts in 1905 ought to be a good enough answer
to the question whether that policy has succeeded
or not.
• * * *
Yet the people who want today the foundations
of a new iron industry nowadays have to en-
counter a competition utiknowm to the days when
the United States were struggling to rear their
baby without offering it the protection of a prac-
tically prohibitive tariff. There is more now
than “ the influx of British manufactures ”
to contend with, aibeit even that influx was
sufficient to cause the collapse of the American
infant. That infant itself, grown into a giant
under a complete reversal of the old policy, does
now for every other infant what Great Britain did
for it while it lay struggling helplessly in its own
cradle. And to that giant the German giant is
added—raised under precisely similar conditions.
In 1850 the German production of iron was 410,000
metric tons. In 1905 it amounted to 10,875,061
metric tons. Protected from outside competition,
assured of a home market sufficient to meet all
their running charges, these giants have developed
the great art of “dumping,” which would be a
terror to a grown competitor, but is something
more than that to an infant not sure as
yet of its feet. It is jiu jitsu applied
to the nursery. Yet, when it was with-
out Protection, the American industry
“collapsed”—to use Mulhall’s words—even be-
fore the new trick in industrial garrotting was dis-
covered. Whether it deserved to collapse—
whether it wasn’t worth saving—its later history- :
tells. And still, 38 years ago, when both the
Australian and the American industries were in-
fants, it was the Californian who said : “ The
parcels of your (Australian) pig-iron received
here have given great satisfaction. . . It would
command from two to four dollars per ton more
than any other iron imported into California.”
* * * *
What then, have we lost ? Or rather, what
have we thrown away, seeing that, in comparison
with the United States, Australia began with
a natural advantage ? The population test
is perhaps the best of all. People will go
to where they can get most work for
most pay, and there is no doubt about it
either that the natural increase will also be
largest where work is most plentiful and the
returns highest. And on that subject G. H.
Knibbs, the new Commonwealth Statistician, had
something interesting to say in the very first
report he made on Australian “Population and
Vital Statistics.” If the population continued to
increase as it had increased in the past six years,
he showed, Australia would have 7,964,000 people
on December 31, 1950 ; but if it increased at the
rate the population of the United States increased
between 1790 and 1860—to come no later—it
wo.uld by the same time have a population num-
bering 15,226,000. In other words, if Australia
continues its present policy, by 1950 it may be
expected to have barely half the population
that it might be expected to have if it
adopted the American policy. And the fur-
ther you take ' such calculations, the more
startling the figures become. The census of
1900 showed 66,893,405, or an increase of no
less than 35,693,405 in the last 32 years.
That was in the United States. In Australia,
in 35 years to the end of 1905, the total in-
crease was 2,359,089. Where Australia has added
one to its population—in spite of its remarkably
fine record in the matter of the death rate —
the United States has thus added 16. Yet,
there was probably never a country in the
world which wanted population worse than
Australia, does. It has a great territory,
but its people are so few that the occupation of a
vast portion of it is only nominal; and that
portion happens to be the most open to the
inroads of peoples whose presence is the least
desirable. At the end of 1906 the Commonwealth
only owned one inhabitant to every 460 acres. If
the population increases as it has done during the
past six years, then even by 1950 we shall still
have but one person to every 238 acres—say one
male of fighting age for every two square miles.
That will be poor enough ; but we must get
through 44 years more at our present snail’s pace
before we can accomplish even that much. On
the other hand, if we can catch up to the Ameri-
can pace that ruled between 1790 and 1860, and
has often been even more rapid still, by 1950 we
shall have one Australian to guard every 124
acres of Australia.
From this point of view of defence there is pro-
bably no better man in the world than the man
who is called off the farm. The S’African war
provided all the evidence that was wanted on that
point—provided it out of the experiences of both
sides. But there was never much of an impres-
sion made by an army of farmers who smote their
enemy with a pound of butter, or hurled at them
a frozen lamb. Butter and lamb are well enough
in their places, and for their own purposes, and
even a ton of pumpkins is not to be despised ;
but they are not much good against an enemy that
fights at long range. This is the age of iron and
of steel. Industry is built upon them, and so is
war. They make a thousand other enterprises
possible. And those thousand enterprises feed
the farmer—the raw material for armies, for such
defensive war at any rate as Australia is liable to
be called upon to fight—just as surely as the
farmer feeds the ironworker and everybody else.
* * * *
It is from this point of view that the firm estab-
lishment of the iron and steel industry has been
of such tremendous importance both:to the United
States and to Germany— not to mention Great
Britain—and it is from this point of view that its
tremendous importance to Australia will have to
be considered. Coghlan, in 1900, estimated that
for five years from 1894 onwards the average
yearly value of the iron and manufactured iron
imported into Australia had been £5,306,729; or,
including Maoriland, £0,535,901. But those years
included some in which Australia was still suffer-
ing a slow and painful recovery from perhaps the
worst drought it has ever had. For the past three
years the figures, as contained in the Common-
wealth “Trade and Customs Returns,” are even
more impressive. The imports of “Metals and
Machinery,” as stated in the just issued volume
for 1906, run upwards in this manner :
1904 £7,186,181
1905 7,480,494
1906 9,143,861
Of iron and steel not worked up into machinery,
the record for last year alone was as follows :
Bar, rod, angle, tee 999,633 cwt.
Galvanised, plate and sheet .. 1,245,211 „
Girders, beams, joists, etc. .. 222,155 „
Hoop 110,783 „
Ingots, blooms, slabs, etc 88,083 „
Plate and sheet 458,197 „
Pig 898,297 „
Scrap 321,939 ~
Total .. .. .. .. 4,344,298 cwt.
That means 217,21.5 tons of iron and steel, apart
from the metal contained in manufactures; and
in iron and steel wire alone there were 55,944
tons. Coghlan estimated that the quantity of
pig-iron required to produce the average
£6,535,901 of imports between 1894 and 1899 was
470,000 tons; it seems quite safe to say that there
is a call now for at least 600,000 tons —probably
650,000 tons.
* * * iA
Six years ago or thereabouts, some mostly-
• Victorian people turned their attention to a re-
markable deposit of iron-ore, which exists on the
Blythe River, in Tasmania; introduced J. H.
Darby, one of the foremost English authorities on
iron-making, to report; and started out to
establish the industry on the Parramatta River,
near Sydney. The industry was never started.
They shivered on the brink of the sea that Bad
swallowed up the previous efforts, and drew
back ; but some of the figures they got together
they left behind them. They only proposed to
turn out 150,000 tons of iron and steel per annum,
yet apart from coal production and the mining
and shipment of the ore, they calculated that
they would have to employ at least 3000 men. If
it takes 3000 men to produce 150,000 tons —apart
from coal-miners, coke-makers, seamen, lumpers,
ore miners, quarrymen, and railway people—it
looks as though it would take something over
12,000 to produce the pig-iron and steel which at
this moment Australia is calling for. To make a
ton of iron at Middlesborough, in England,
on the average they need from 20 to
21 cwt. of coke; at Pittsburg, according to
a report quoted by J. B. Jaquet, who, in 1901,
wrote a report on the subject of N. 3- Wales iron
deposits for the Mines Department, it was done
with 16cwt. of coke. He would not, however,
assume less than one ton of coke per ton of pig-
iron to be produced at Lithgow (N.S.W.). On
top, then, of the men at the iron works is the
equal or greater number at the coal mines and
coke works. Jaquet, before mentioned, estimated
the labor in the actual production of pig-iron at
6s. per ton ; the coke would cost 15s. per ton—the
15s. to be divided between the coal-miner, the coke-
burner, and the railway man. So the 12,000 men
whom Australia might employ—and as a matter
of fact does employ, though it doesn’t employ
them in Australia —multiply themselves by two
and three. Then these multiplied thousands have
their own needs of iron and steel and manufac-
tured goods; and those who feed these new
mouths have their needs of iron and steel and
manufactured goods ; and those who supply those
new needs must be fed ; and those who feed them
have their own needs; and so the multiplication
goes on. That extraordinary progress in the
population figures of the United States is,
after all, then, no miracle. A self-contained
community of a dozen people lives by ministering
to one another’s needs. If one more comes it is
another mouth to feed, but there are another pair
of hai)ds to work* If the raw material is there, it
is the same whether it is one new mouth or a
million. One million in a self-contained com-
munity, supplying its own needs out of its own
natural resources, makes it possible for another
million to live. So, because its policy has been
to supply its own needs, the American population
has advanced by millions, while the Australian
has crept on by such tardy thousands that to-day
it views with increasing apprehension its great
empty spaces, and almost dreads to think upon
what is likely to happen next.
Vic. political forecast by “L.” :
Possible that Victoria will be governed by a Parliament
without Bent before very long. Unless the stout song-
and-dance Premier is in much better health than when he
left, the work of running Victoria will be beyond
his strength. With Bent eliminated, Vic. politics would
take some interesting turns. The Peacock-Mackinnon
piece of the Cabinet cannot mix very long with the
fossilised portion represented by Cameron and McLeod,
or with the Methodist Toryism to which Swinburne grows
more and more devoted, and a split threatens. The so-
called “ Country Party ” is only a temporary political
weapon, which would smash as soon as serious fighting
began. What is beginning to loom largely is a revival of
the Liberal-Labor alliance, which procured all the most
successful democratic legislation of the State in the
past. The Liberal and half-Liberal members who clustered
round Bent (after thanking God that they were rid of
Irvine) grow uneasy. On the other hand, the Labor
party has started the session with a more temperate tone
and policy than it has shown for years. Those who have
disappeared from its ranks include Scott-Bennett;
Martin Hannah, the strike promoter; and bitter-
mouthed Bob Solly. It can do without these. A
working platform, as the basis of an alliance between
Liberalism and Labor, is an immediate possibility. But
nothing will be done while Thomas Bent remains in office.
That weighty and humorless customer is too formidable
to be thrust aside in a shuffle of parties.
The chances of a progressive land-tax in
Victoria, never bright, are duller than ever since
Mackinnon and Peacock were gobbled up by the
Bent Ministry. Practically, outside the Labor
party. Jack Murray is the only notable man in
the Assembly who now raises his voice for this
most urgent reform. If the bulky man from
Warrnambool does not become too lazy or too
dead, he will some day be Victoria’s democratic
Premier. But as that day still seems far off, the
Australian Parliament had better impose the tax
in the meantime. The sooner the better for Vic-
toria’s agricultural prosperity.

Fairbairn, M.H.R., who has toll tele-
phones on the brain, again got on to the subject
of his pet horror the other day, and wept over
the poor attendants who have so much extra work
to do—they have to keep a record of the calls, as
well as answer the rings. After the Minister had
implored Fairbairn to bear up, and remember that
the toll-attendants don’t have to answer more
than about a third as many calls as those working
on the flat-rate machines, Fairbairn- replied that
anyhow people had the uniform flat-rate in some
countries. And when some Labor member said
that they had, and that the people paid a great
deal more for it, Fairbairn lit up the whole
situation with the remark —“Yes, but in those
cases the telephones have to pay !” Probably he
sees now that he gave the whole weep away there.
The marvellous thing is that people send men
like Fairbairn to the House (1) to urge that State
works shouldn’t be expected to pay; (2) to curse
Socialism because State works don’t pay. It
seems to be a fairer thing to curse Fairbairn for a
change.
The Sydney Telegraph (2/8/’O7) described
the N.S.W. Freetrade contingent in the House of
Peps.:—
The fine fighting force on the Opposition side is on the
direct left benches, where the N.S.W. members and the
Queenslanders are gathered. The N.S.W. member, no
matter what p irty lie belongs to, has a perfect genius for
•political battling. This is why nearly all the leaders are
gained from this State. From Mr. Reid, who is un-
doubtedly the most distinguished parliamentarian in the
Chamber, down to the rawest of the newcomers, the first
move of the N.S.W. man, whether he has had a term in
the State Parliament or not, is to do something that will
stir up or harass some other party, and preferably the
Government. The member who does not talk in his first
session never comes from N.S.W. The N.S.W. member
speaks if he can on the first day, begins to manoeuvre on
the second, and is ready with a concealed censure motion
on the third. Even Dr. Liddell, who stepped very gin-
gerly forward long after he entered Parliament, now
takes his place in all big debates, and is getting in train
f >r tripping up Ministers with points of order against
their Bills.
It isn’t a flattering description. Put shortly, it
amounts to the statement that the N.S.W.
Foreign-traders are about the most quarrelsome
members of the House ; that instead of attending
to the interests of Australia their chief ambition
is to “harass some other party and preferably
the Government ” ; that they are horribly gar-
rulous—the fresh N.S.W. Oppositionist, it seems,
begins to talk on the day of his arrival in the
House ; and that Dr. Liddell has, to the great
admiration of the Telegraph, acquired the dirty
little finesse of the police-court attorney, and is
great at blocking the business of Australia by
“ tripping up Ministers with points of order.”
The S. M. Herald clawed the air vio-
lently in a leading article last week because the
head of the Federal Meteorological office is to be
stationed for the moment in Victoria, and said
that “it is the fixed policy of the Government to
concentrate the administration as far as possible
in Melbourne.” Well, what does the Herald
object to? Also what does it expect? The ad-
ministration must be concentrated where the
Government is, or how on earth can the Govern-
ment administer ? It would be concentrated on
Monaro, in N.S.W., if the Sydney daily papers
and the Carruthers creature would cease blocking
the way, but as they won’t cease blocking the
way, things of necessity keep moving Melbourne-
wards. Unless the Sydney daily papers and the
Carrutbers creature get some sense, everything in
the departmental way will be at Melbourne in
course of time.
Some ramblings by this paper’s Oldest
Inhabitant :
An interesting picture of Old Sydney by Conrad
Martens, dated 1839, has been purchased in England by
Mr. William Dixson, and lent to the National Art Gallery.
The wonder is that so rare a work by such an artist as
Martens should have been allowed to pass into the hands
of a private buyer. One would have thought that the
Trustees of the Art Gallery would have an agent on the
look out for such treasures. Conrad Martens was the son
of a Hamburg merchant settled in England, his mother
being English. Conrad was born in 1801, at Crutched
Friars, London (the name suggests that begging clergy
witn wooden legs hung out there in days of yore).
He studied art under Copley Fielding. Captain Black!
wood offered him a cruise in H.M.S. Hvacinth, and he
sailed in that vessel for Rio. There he“ heard that an
artist was needed on board the Beagle, then engaged in
a surveying expedition, and Martens painted whales and
such like in an official capacity for two years. He reached
Sydney from Valparaiso in or'about 1835, and settled at
a lovely spot on North Shore, overlooking Berry’s Bay.
He married the daughter of'William Carter, barrister and
Registrar-General, who lived at Crow’s Nest, North Shore.
Mr. Martens settled down to the practice of his pro!
fessionT but the discovery of gold burst up art, and he
accepted (in 1863) the office of Parliamentary Librarian
which post he held until his death in 1878.
Dr. Bernstein died last week at Lismore
(N.S.W.) at the age of 70, esteemed by everybody
who knew him. A few years ago he was famous
in a much wider circle than Lismore. In the
drunken days, when Australians were fighting
and burning farms in order to get Chinese labor
for the Johannesburg mines, Bernstein refused
to applaud the craze of the moment. Then
all the refuse of the district yapped at him,
and bombarded the Government with letters,
anonymous and otherwise, about liis “dis-
loyalty* ’ and a blood-drunken Ministry de-
prived him of the valueless job of J.P., and of
various’Government billets which he held, .These
things all came back to him in later years, and
the bellowing multitude of Lismore in time put
on the semblance of humanity again, and had the
grace to be ashamed. It was vaguely suggestive
of a previous case, when some J’s.P., who said
they thought Ireland should have a Parliament
of its own, the same as N.S.W., were driven from
the Beitch at the instance of a frantic politician,
whose pwn sins found him out very soon after-
wards.
“ J.R.” writes :
Our own Bill Lyne told an interviewer, the other day
that when he boarded the Orontes at Naples, he passed
through a crowd of Italian emigrants embarking for
America, and, from what he saw of them, “America is
welcome, to them.” Bill is probably right, judging by
what we hear and read about them. Only a day oUtwo
before, there appeared in the press a correspondent’s
letter, giving some curious details about a murder society
called “ The Black Hand,” which is raging in some coaly
districts in the States. It is said to be an offshoot of the
notorious Mafia society of Sicily, and that again is likely
to be a survival of the Saracen dominion in the island.
It is a gruesome business to trace these survivals of
Asiatic domination in Europe, and it is not always clear
where the traces begin, and how far they extend. The
name of the Mafia is certainly Arabic in origin, and its
meaning,, which may be rendered “ unmitigatedly
bad,” is admirably expressive of its character as a secret
society for purposes of murder, kidnapping, and black-
mail. Robbery in the ordinary sense does not seem to be
a regular part of its business. In a poor country like
Sicily robbery would hardly be profitable enough to offer
any great temptation to an enterprising law-breaker ; but
the chance of catching, and holding for ransom, a rich
landowner or some member of his family (for there are
fabulously rich landowners in Sicily, as in all poor coun-
tries) offers temptation not to be resisted by men always
on the verge cf starvation, and too grossly ignorant to
realise the enormity of their crimes. The prisoner is
murdered if the ransom is not paid.
Senator Neild continues to knock large
pieces off N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade over
the proposal to grant an appeal to the High
Court in criminal cases. Wade asserts that in
N.S.W. a convicted person has already a limited
right of appeal to the Supreme Court, and that a
f urtner appeal to the High Court tends to intro-
duce the scandalous American system, whereby
there is no finality in the case of a moneyed
criminal. Neild replies briefly that N.S.W. isn’t
all Australia; that the proposal is to create a
uniform appeal system for all the States,
some of which need it badly, even if N.S.W.
doesn’t; and that N.S.W. can abolish the
right of appeal to the local Supreme Court,
and thus avoid the evil that Wade complains of.
That reply leaves nothing for Wade to grumble
about save “Federal encroachment.”
Died in Sydney recently Thomas Davidson
Hay, formerly secretary of the Australasian In-
stitute of Marine Engineers. He was a deter-
mined litigant, and when discharged, under
abusive circumstances, from his position as secre-
tary of the Institute, he fought strenuously for
about seven years to clear his character. He had
one arbitration on the question ; it lasted four
months, and the arbitrator’s award vindicated
him. A fresh attack gave him an opening in the
courts, and he won a verdict for £2OOO against the
then figurehead of the Institute. This verdict
was rendered abortive owing to the flight of the
defendant. He then sued thelnstitute itself, which
had meanwhile become incorporated, and a jury of
N.S.W. gave him £l5OO damages. The Supreme
Court of N.S.W. upset this, on the ground that,
while he could have succeeded against individual
libellers in the shape of the Society’s officers, the
legal responsibility of the Institute (newly incor-
porated) was too remote. The High Court
upheld this decision, and Hay was left saddled
with the costs of two big trials and two appeals.
The worry broke his heart.
Thomas Shaw, of Wooriwyrite, Camper-
down, one of the last of the pioneers of western
Victoria, died last week, aged 81. He reached
Sydney in 1843. In 1847 he overlanded to Ade-
laide with cattle, and arrived at Portland the same
year. He bought land at Camperdown originally
held by J. G. Ware, and sold by him to Oliphant
and Robertson. As owner of Wooriwyrite, he
became one of the leading flock-masters in tl)e
Western District. He devoted himself to the
improvement of the breed of Australian sheep,
and to destroying the breed of men and women
by keeping the lands locked against settlement,
and all his days he did his little bit to make the
fertile Western District the howling waste of
grass that it is. Camperdown, the principal
town in the deceased’s locality, has been just
saving burial expenses for 20 years or more.
“ Squidge ” in an incoherent sort of way ;
Par re the Victorian Vagrants Act (B. 4/7/’O7) is so
inaccurate as to cast a slur on Victoria’s House of Well-to-
do Persons (a thing that The Bulletin would never con-
sciously do), and to pay a compliment to Bent when he
ought to have a big clod hurled at him. The amending
Police Offences Act was drafted by Attorney-General
Davies as the result of some redhot articles in Melbourne
Argus on the class of persons who had invaded Melb.
for the spring racing carnival. It was pointed out that
every racecourse and street corner was thick with
“guns” of every description over whom the police,
thanks to lax laws, had no control. Davies brought his
Bill into the House of Rich Landlords with a rush, and
the House of Landlords, thinking of its massive gold
chain just out of sight below its bulging meridian,
trembled visibly and held its breath while the Bill was
dashed through all its stages, so as to he available before
Cup week came along. It was sent down to the Lower
House, and Bent did not even bother to have it read a
first time. He boggled along with the Gambling Bill, and
stumbled through with the Licensing Bill; but the Police
Offences Act Amendment Bill, not having anyone behind
it to chew Bent’s elongated ear, was part of the cargo
jettisoned at the end of the session. If Bent had done
his part of the job, the Bill would have passed the
Assembly as rapidly as it did the Council. But he
hadn’t time to think just then, and didn’t care, anyhow.
For croup there is nothing better than
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy. For sale every-
where, Is. 6d.*
The new Postal Bill has some good points
and one noteworthy omission. It endeavors to
distinguish between the paper or periodical which
carries advts. and the mere one-firm advertise-
ment which carries a thin disguise, or veil, or rim
of reading matter. Thatisgood. But why notalso
discriminate between the original publication and
the steal publication ? It is a common thing to
enact that a paper gets the benefit of the cheap
postal rates, provided it isn’t more than seven
days old when posted. But the publication
that is four-fifths steal from English magazines is
probably, on the average, six weeks old. The
bulk of the matter was first posted in London
last month, or the month before last.
The Adelaide Tramway Trust refuses to
consult its engineer as to the matter of routes ;
and Bonython’s Advertiser is scornful about it.
That scorn brings memories to some Adelaide
people. The Adelaide School of Mines—which
is almost wholly bossed by Slangdon Bonython
when he is in this country—some time ago had a
fine metallurgist named Crowther in charge. Full
of energy, Crowther wrote report after report and
made recommendation after recommendation,
and finally poured out his aspirations at a
students’ dinner. He wanted the School of
Mines and the industries section kept separate ;
he couldn't see the sense of giving the girl who
had learned how to cook tripe a School of Mines
diploma. After that speech he was promptly got
rid off; he left Australia then, and was killed a
few weeks ago by an accident at the Etruscan
Mines (Italy), where he had got a professional
billet. Now the mining students, who used to
swear by Crowther, see Slangdon Bonython’s
Advertiser making remarks to the effect that
the laymen of the Trust should listen to the ex-
pert, and they are scornful too.
“J.A.C.”: David Christie Murray, de-
ceased last week, made his acquaintance with
Australia under the aegis of “Jimmy” Mac-
mahon. He came out as a lecturer, and opened
at Melbourne Town Hall on 21st August, 1889.
The title of his first lecture was “ Making a
Novelist ” ; it was amusing and interesting, and
delivered in a colloquial manner, but it did not
catch on. It lacked stirring incident, and there
were no pictures, moving or stationary. Contem-
poraneously, Snazelle, also on his first visit and
under the same management, was “dissolving”
to big business at Melbourne Athenaeum. I re-
member one specimen .of gruesome jocularity
which Murray narrated. He represented an
English provincial daily at the execution of
Charles Peace, expert burglar and murderer.
When Peace was placed on the scaffold he was
much agitated, and gasped huskily “ Water! For
God’s sake, water ! Only a drop ! ” Almost im-
mediately the hangman drew the bolt. “ There,”
said a reporter at Murray’s elbow, “he’s got it.
He said he only wanted a drop ! ” When the lec-
tures failed, D.O.M. essayed play-acting. Together
with Harry St. Maur, he made a tour of Australasia.
For a semi-amateur, he was by no means a bad
actor —a little ponderous and deliberate, but not
bad. During the tour he wrote some new plays,
and produced them. None of them made a last-
ing impression. One I saw at Melbourne Opera
House. It was named “Gratitude,” and was
played at one consecutive matinee, on October 20,
1890. Perhaps his best novel was “ The Way of
the World,’’ in which he satirised Henry W.
Lucy, in return for a similar attention on the part
of that gentleman. The man who created “Mr.
William Amelia’’had a great latent capacity for
hatred.
Just at present the man who does the biggest
business in Australia in the biggest store south of the
Equator is holding the largest sale in the southern
hemisphere. Anthony Hordern does not hold a sale
very often, hut when ha does it is a very thorough-
going concern. The “Famous Low Prices” have come
tumbling downwards, even as Australian championships
have fallen during the week, and the pick of the world’s
markets are now to be had a t bed-rock prices. A Hordern
sale is different from any other in Sydney. In that
huge building, which really is an emporium, there is no
crushing, and the woman who wants bargains has not
got to fight a pitched battle to get to the counters.
There are miles of counters in Anthony Hordern’s, and
room for all.*
Under date September 5, 1877, an ancient
chronicle remarks: “ First female clerk in the
public service employed by the Adelaide Council
of Education.” Even so far back as that did good
old Tortoise-town lead the way.
“ J.P.” digs up a centenarian (presumably
genuine):—
Mr. H. Burling, the oldest inhabitant of the Wairarapa
(M.L.), is getting fairly ancient. He has celebrated his
106th birthday, and is looking hopefully toward his 107th.
His sight has recently failed him, but otherwise he is
hale and hearty. He amuses himself even yet by doing a
little gardening. Shortly after he completed his century
he was assisting his grandsons to sow grass seed on very
hilly country, and took the lead with a bag of seed upon
his shoulder. After a time he noticed that the boys were
lagging behind, so he asked if he was making the pace
too fast. It is not considered advisable to say anything
about the declining birth-rate in his presence. He has
over 400 descendants.
Fierce language from “F.C.” :
Re “H.A.” and the relative profits of football and
cricket matches (B. l/8/’ff7). As “ H.A." is kind enough
to admit the correctness of my figures, the only dispute
is as to the deductions to be made from said figures.
“ H.A.” says the cricket clubs “provide and equip
the ground, supply dressing-room accommodation,
etc., and undertake the management of all football
matches,” etc. To which I reply, “Go to.” Like-
wise “Rats.” Which ground does any cricket club
in Melbourne provide? Toe match which brought
up the discussion was played on St. Kilda ground for
which the cricket club pays the Albert Park Trustees
£25 a year. Ditto South Melbourne, Carlton and ’Wood
grounds, likewise’Roy, Essendon (East Melbourne) and
Melbourne. They all belong to the State.
“H. A.” then says, Before this season St. Kilda had
practically no revenue from football. South Melbourne
has splendidly improved its ground. Fitzroy has re-
cently built a fine new grandstand to accommodate foot-
ball patrons.” Jesso 1 St. Kilda has had practically no
football revenue hitherto—and its accommodation is
as yet practically nil. South’s was ditto until
a few years back, when its football club came to
the top, and indirectly supplied the wherewithal
Fitzroy likewise, until, from ’9B to 'O6, ’Roy football
club was near the top of the list, and the football
thousands demanded and paid for more accommoda-
tion. Does “H.A.” pretend that that new stand was
bought by cricket patrons, or that they would need
it! They would be lost in a corner of it" The existing
dispute about the control of the new Carlton ground °”ives
point to my contention. Here, as at Collingwood each
competing football club gets a third, and the committee
that manages the ground gets a third. The cricket club
ge ts nothing. Which is exactly what should be the case at all
other grounds. That ground is but a couple of years old •
already it h s been made, has been banked up better
than any other ground in Melbourne, has got turnstiles
and is preparing to build a stand. The receipts there last
season and this T by reason of the Carlton football club
being temporarily a great affair—have run to £2OO
and £3OO on alternate Saturdays, right through the
seasons. Could the Carlton cricket club provide
or draw one-tenth of that amount? Yet this precious
Carlton cricket club lately contended that it should have
entire control of the ground. This piece of bluff was
modified to a demand that the ground management com-
mittee should consist of equal numbers from the O.C. and
the F.C., with a, cricket club chairman.
My original contention was that the football clubs
should manage the grounds and the cricket clubs should
play thereon only with their permission—and I still
contend so. With splendid accommodation, a first-class
premiership cricket match and a band, the M.C.C. has
not been able these last two seasons to get a decent
crowd, with the gates wide open for all u'ho care to
e, ' ter ; .y, efc 25,000 will pay to stand at St. Kilda to see
a football match. Will “ H.A.” say after that who main-
tains these grounds and builds the stands and provides
the accommodation, club-rooms, promenade, etc.?
And is there any occult reason why the football sup-
porter should not have all these things, which he pays
for, while the superior cricket barracker stands out on
the embankment in the rain? At Carlton and ’Wood
°" y the F.C. member or the eighteenpenny public
get the best that is on offer ; elsewhere the cricket patron
takes the cream that the other provides, and passes him
the skim milk. v
