GOOD RESULTS.
Clements Tonic
Cures and Strengthens.
THE PROOF.
(BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
With a view to bringing before the public a
matter of considerable importance, a reporter
rapped at 114 Glebe Road, Glebe, where Mrs.
Hannah Wells resides. The lady appeared in
response, and upon the pressman’s mission being
explained, she said :
“Certainly. Nothing would give me more plea-
sure than to relate the story of my great achieve-
ment of four years ago, especially as it will be so
serviceable to many in want of guidance. You
see it was after fruitlessly trying several home
remedies that I saw about Clements Tonic in the
papers, and made up my mind to find out what it
was like for myself, as I was very ill, and was
willing to take anything so long as I got better.
I was as
WEAK AND MISERABLE AS ANY
WOMAN COULD BE,
and had such a sallow complexion, too, that to
look at me you would have thought I had the
jaundice. My chest used to cause me no end of
bother, for often I felt that something was there,
blocking my breath, besides having heavy, op-
pressive pains as well. I hadn’t any inclination
to eat.
NO APPETITE AT ALL,
so what could I expect but to get thin and deli-
cate looking, which I assuredly did. From the
time I arose from bed of a morning till I returned
again in the evening I was as languid and indo-
lent as could be. When I tried to sleep at night
I could not, and oh ! it was so wretched being
there hour after hour waiting for the daylight,
and when it came, what did iC bring? Only
another day of sorrow. My sight got bad, and
my eyes felt as heavy as lead ; the taste in my
mouth was awfully bitter, and the worst of it was
that my head was always aching.
“Indeed, I don’t know what I hadn’t got to
make life a burden, and if I hadn’t taken Clements
Tonic when I did l am afraid to think what would
have happened, for I was going down hill fast.”
“ Had your nerves given way at all ? ”
“Oh ! I was fearfully nervous, scarcely able to
move without getting a shock at some trivial thing
or other, and even when I managed to snatch a
little sleep my mind was unsettled with various
ugly dreams. Now, that is where Clements Tonic
came in with good results very speedily. It
quieted my nerves, and gave me such com-
mand over myself that nothing surprised or
excited me, and as for having nasty dreams
or getting frightened at anything—why, I
was quite exempt in both respects. I used to
meet trouble halfway too, but I got quite out of
that habit since using Clements Tonic, which also
made me eat as hungrily as if I had taken a good
sea trip before each meal. What surprised me
more, though, and my friends too, was the manner
in which my pains subsided, for when I told them
that I was perfectly at ease, and that my head-
aches were gone, they could scarcely believe me.
But it is nevertheless a fact that Clements Tonic
cured me and strengthened me thoroughly, and
in the interests of similar sufferers you may pub-
lish these remarks as it suits you.”
Subscribed and declared at Glebe, this twenty-
fourth day of August, one thousand nine hundred
and three, before me, W. T. TATE, J.P.

The parable of “ Silica "’ :
One recent Thursday, Pate, in the course of a shop-
ping expedition, happened on Brisbane, and there,
while in the act of adjusting her glove, she
negligently dropped the tail end of her skirt oyer
the political constitution of Queensland, whereat tne
greater part of that State was perturbed and
mystified, and made violently suspicious. Most people
are, where a strange female is concerned. On -lues-
day a spring hat on the show stand in Patagonia attracted
the lady, and she struck out for it. William Kidsuon was
left standing in the full glare of daylight, Kidston has
played the game well. Ever since the May elections,
Trouble, with an ugly glint in its eye, has been looming
up, and it burst into fury when the extreme Labor party
crossed the House and sat on the Opposition cross-
benches, driven there by that curious amalgam of Robert
Blatchford and T. Mann, the Brisbane Worker. Kidston
should have, by all the rules of the game, been smitten
to the earth; he should have been buried in the most
deserted spot in the political cemetery, below fl°°d-
mark ; and his name to future Labor stalwarts should
have been plain Mud. But a Dutch-built, heavy-brained,
dour Scotchman had to be reckoned with. Kidston
reached for Trouble the moment it entered the
House, and secured first fall. He hobnobbed with Fat,
to the great and intense dismay of Anti-Fat, but
he did not make the mistake of allowing the oleaginous
people to take it for granted that Labor was utterly
irreconcilable. He gave Philp rope—considerable rope,
since the Brisbane press was discovering new virtues in
Kidston every day : but he knotted the end of the rope to
the Treasury bench, so that Philp wouldn’t get too far.
Bowman and Hardacre and the Worker saw Philp appa-
rently free and independent. They saw stacks of rope
about the premises, but nothing more. They trembled,
but in the end capitulated. At the eleventh hour (which
was 2 o’clock, and about an hour before the House met) a
flag of truce was sent, and it was announced that the
party was prepared to give a general support to the
policy outlined in the Governor’s speech. William Kidston
heard, and cut Philp’s rope, and the “situation” was
over, with Kidston still in the “ stiddy job.” There is
nothing startling in the Govermnentprogramme nothing
heroic enough to even win the 1.5.0. medal that every
other policeman wears. A Bill to establish Wages Boards
and another to provide for State insurance are the two
main features. But, at the least, Kidston is a buffer
against the howling calamity of a Philp regime.
Says Mr. Dugald Thomson, M.H.R.,
rebuking Alf. Deakin for his unseemly conduct in
daring to criticise the Colonial Office : “What an
outcry there would be in Australia if a visiting
British Minister were to publicly attack a Com-
monwealth department! ” The Dugald object,
in his anxiety to say something nasty, even if it
is wholly untrue, about Australia’s representa-
tive, carefully misses the point. When the
British Government doesn’t like Australian legis-
lation it doesn’t need to criticise ; it simply vetoes
the Bill. As we can’t veto the acts of the British
Colonial Office, we have to criticise.
The annual meeting of Sydney Chamber
of Commerce carried this motion :
The time has arrived when the State of N.S.W. should
insist upon the complete fulfilment by the Commonwealth
of its obligations to N.S.W. in respect to the Federal
capital.
Quite right. The time has arrived when N.S.W*
should intimate in unmistakable language that
it is deadly tired, also weary and exhausted, of
seeing the capital business hung up through the
irresponsible cackling of such parochial small-fry
as Joseph Carruthers and the Sydney Chamber of
Commerce; and the period is tight up against
the buffer-stops when N.S.W. should insist upon
the Commonwealth Government forthwith pro-
ceeding to plant the capital at Dalgety, the place
selected by the Australian Parliament, and so
“fulfil its obligations” to N.S.W. to the letter.
About a man who lived in uncertainty :
Died recently at Dublin, Ireland, George Augustus
Jervis Meredith, baronet, aged 76. He was once a police-
man at Hobart, and after that he drove a cab—one of
those large open vehicles with the faint odor of three-
penny cigars about them—over the cobblestones of the
city till he reached the age of 72. In 1904 he succeeded
to the baronetcy and went to Ireland to take up the title
and occupy the “estate,” which may be accounted as
nil, seeing that the late baronet was resident at No. 15
Royal Foundation, Windsor Castle —said to be a sort of
almshouse for the elevated who have come down.
The deceased, by the way, wasn’t really a whole baronet.
He was only a hazy legend. His cousin and pre-
decessor, the pauper, refused to recognise him at
all, and the title was classed by most authorities as
extinct before the ex-cabman arrived at it. There
was an alleged but unproved marriage over 100 years ago,
and a mysterious tale about a pedigree that was forged in
1808, and other difficulties. In fact, the man from Hobart
abandoned his claim in disgust, and threw the title after
the vanished estates, but some people still called him
“ Sir Augustus,” and “ Debrett,” the great book of aris-
tocratic grovel and small beer, never quite made up its
mind whether he was the genuine article or not.

The Silverton Tramway Co.’s chairman claims
t° have a lower expense rate than any other railway in
Australia, the percentage of working expenses to revenue
being 31-42, as against 56-63 in S. Australia, with whose
railway system the co.’s line is connected. But it is
unfair to compare this 35 miles of line with the aggregate
system of a whole State; there may be sections of Govern -
Ulen^n ine Victoria and N.S. Wales which show up just
as well as this bit of Private Enterprise line. The Private
Enterprise line can, however, usually show a good result
on the basis of working expenses in proportion to income
simply because, its charges being higher, its income is
greater in proportion to the work done. For instance
assume that Private Enterprise charges 20s. while the
State charges only 12s. for the same service, and see how
it works out:—
Proportion
of Expenses
„ . , _ . freight. Expenses, to Income.
Private Enterprise .. 20/- 6/8 33p.c.
State .. „ ..12/- 6/- 50 p,c.
Here the State’s expenses are actually lower than Private
Enterpnse’s ; yet worked out in proportion to income,
the State is a bad second. The Silverton Co.’s comparison
isn t, therefore, worth anything without a comparison
also of freight charges. And it is no use, either, taking its
ire ght for 35 miles and, say, the South Australian freight
for a 35-mile journey, because the Silverton Co.’s, is all
through traffic ; a full train is made up at one end, and
19 times out of 20 is delivered in the same state at the
other end. Also, it is worth noting that when the co.
wanted a man to run its amazingly profitable enterprise
it got one who had had his training in State railway
employment. On the latest published figures (1906) the
Victorian aggregate results are the best, judged on this
basis of the proportion w'liich working expenses bear to
earnings : Vic., 52-78 per cent. ; N.S. Wales, 54-51 per
cent. ; Queensland, 55-84 per cent. ; S. Australia, 56’‘63
per cent. ; Westralia, 73-53 per cent. Westralia has de-
cided that it is about up toitto put in a newßailwavConi-
missioner.

The mistake made last year in paying a 6 per cent, divi-
dend has not been repeated. It isn’t every Board that
has the courage to turn back like this. Pretty often it
continues in its error, and that is mostly the beginning
of inflated balance-sheets and cooked accounts. Pro bah 1 y
because the banks have been giving extra-cheap ac.com-" 1
modation, building societies have not in general been able
to show much in the way of larger advances to mark this
wave of prosperity. Here are this society’s figures on this
and other points :

f J?i 11 E^ rofifcabl ®,h Properties standing in the books at

This highly profitable business more than holds its own.
blx a “° the value of trust estates in hand
iC is £1 >255,302. At the end
of 1904, when the figures were first published the
total was £1,016,734. The trustee co. is On a remarkabl v
good wicket, so long as it doesn’t run foul of the law and
make itself responsible for losses through applying funds
to purposes to which trust funds shouldn't be applied
according to the rather obscure statutes—some of the
earlier Victorian cos. got into trouble that way. It takes
practically no capital to run the business: capital and
reserves, which are merely the testators’ guarantee fund,
ca.n be invested apart from the business ; and the business
itself is mighty profitable. This co. (capital £15,000) has
now apart from its premises, £22,515 in public securities
S ga £ es > and other investments, bringing in probably
£9OO a year, or more than half the dividend. The
commissions are at the rate of about 9s. 6d per
cent, of the trust estates, and the total ex-
penses are only about 6s. 4d. per cent. It is
not certain, however, that the charges for administra-
tion amount to 9s. 6d. per cent. ; for possibly some of the
commissions are earned apart from that business
Wherever they come from, 44 per cent of them are profit'
On the basis of capital and reserves, the total return was
at the rate of £lB 19s. 9d. per cent. A good feature of
the case is that so large a proportion of the profits are
put to reserve, and that is a point which testators can
note with, satisfaction. If this trustee should go wrong
there would be something more than a vanished reputa-
tion for the beneficiaries to satisfy themselves with. All
the same, the time is coming when trustee cos", will
either have to reduce their charges or do more for the
money.

Areas from 80 to 156 acres, all rich volcanic soil,
and containing beautiful, permanent and running
water. ALL IDEAL DAIRYING COUNTRY,
and capable of producing splendid Sugar Cane
and other crops.
The situation being on the eastern extension of
the Blackall Range country, right on the Railway
Line, adjacent to Railway Stations, Township
handy to all Markets, and within three hours by
railway from Brisbane, renders this sale an excel-
lent opportunity to those in search of good, solid
investments enhancing in value, or intending
settlers to secure beautiful Virgin Farms. The
climate is considered one of the most genial of the
Australian Coastal Districts, with no extremes of
heat or cold.

“My trouble started with swelling in
my right hand, which became red and
Very painful and irritating. This swell-
ing spread gradually up the arm until it
reached the elbow. It is impossible for
me to give any idea of the agony I went
through during the three years that
this eczema continued. I tried first
one remedy and then another, only too
glad to test anything that my friends
suggested. Nothing seemed to be of
the least good. I went to a doctor for
four weeks in the provinces, where I was
living at the time, and the treatment
he put me under was as ineffectual as
the rest. I was almost in despair. One
day I happened to catch sight of an
advertisement of Cuticura Remedies,
and there I read of a case of eczema
having been cured by the use of Cuti-
cura Soap, Cuticura Ointment, and
Cuticura Resolvent Pills. The case
was so similar to my own that I thought
I would at least make one more try to
get my arm right. I bought the whole
treatment of the chemist, and I perse-
vered with it for a week. By that
time the improvement was apparent to
any one, and the relief I felt myself was
great. I should not have believed it
possible for any remedy to afford such
comfort in so short a time. My ex-
perience with the other remedies for
eczema—and I should think. I tried
them all enables me to appreciate
Cuticura to the full. By the doctor’s
orders I was keeping my arm in a sling
for many months, but the sling was soon
dispensed with when I started with
Cuticura. I persevered with Cuticura
Soap, Ointment, and Pills for about a
couple of months, using in all two
complete treatments, and I can tell
you candidly and heartily that I was
cured. I have occasion to have great
faith in Cuticura in skin ailments, not
only because of the great good it has
done me, but because of the benefit I
know has accrued from its use in other
cases. Mr. T. S. Gillard, 3, Hetley Road,
Shepherd’s Bush, London, Nov. 19, ’06.”

The Inky Way.
The New Zealand News is to be definitely hatched
at Wellington in August. The Times is a harassed
rag, for it had an idea that several of its writers
who had been with it for anything up to a year
meant to stay for another 12 months. The new News
bought Mick Keene, Times associate-editor, and he be-
comes assistant-editor on the fresh job. Reading, a
Times sub-editor, and Guy Schofield handed in their
resignations and prepared to depart, having promised to
man the new staff, but the Times threw more gold at them,
and they will stay at the old address. Marcus Plimmer
(brother of “Our ’Arry”) really does leave the beer-
prohibition organ and becomes chief reporter of the
News. Hoben, who, a few months ago, was implored by
the Hard Times to go from Sydney to general-manage it,
has been implored not to do it any more, and the Times
has been rushing M.’s.H.R. on to its directorate to stop
the loss of ink, or prestige or something. The steady old
Wellington Post, which didn’t know until recently that
anybody would dare to leave it, supplies in Charles Earle
the £Booper year editor of the News. “Ernie” Hall,
who has been driven by the two rival papers backwards
and forwards between their offices for the last year or two,
goes with Charles to the new inkery, and Charlie Weston
brother of Fred Weston, who “ subbed ” the Post for 16
years, and then went Hansarding—is another Post deserter.
The Times also supplies the News with a recently-acquired
specialist in Sainsbury, who will handle the big tomato
and woolly jumbuck section of the new print. The Maori-
land capital is also threatened with a new evening daily,
which reckons it is coming from somewhere in Australia,
lock, stock, and paste-pot. As the Wellington business
man is a mean advertiser, and as the population of Wel-
lington won’t be as dense as that of Manchester for
several weeks, the Poneke person will obviously have to
buy four papers a day to keep the quartette of machines
roaring.
* * * *
Hobart is threatened with a new daily—a democratic
Protectionist paper—to be called the Post. The
originator and nominal proprietor of the Post is
A. J. Nettlefold, who has risen to prominence by his
business energy in various retail lines, and is a patriotic
and uncompromising Australian. The prospect of an
early deliverance from the sway of the awful Hobart
Murky has caused the inhabitants of the Tasmanian
capital to rise to deeds of daring. Members of the
House of Assembly, notably one Jarvis, who has been
shockingly misrepresented by the prevailing rag, have
actually, “in the very forum, under the noonday
sun,” defied the Murky and all its lightnings.
Only those who are intimate .with the public life of
Southern Tasmania can realise what an extraordinary
state of affairs has existed there by reason of the Murky’s
monopoly of morning journalism. The Tasmanian w orm
has long been trying to turn, but as a political
and social worm can only turn by publishing
things, it has so far found the process very difficult.
Henceforth the worm promises to wear the aspect of a
very angry boa-constrictor, and many results may be
looked for. Persons such as McWilliams will probably
vanish from the Tasmanian representation in the Com-
monwealth Parliament, as they have from the Legislatures
of all other civilised lands. A Ministry such as that of
“ Jacky ” Evans, living upon the support of, and obeying in
all things the orders of the Murky, will no longer be
possible. The delay of measures tending to solve the
land problem, and to relieve the small farmers of their
burdens under the property-tax, will not be endured
when the small people have a paper to give voice to their
views. Probably nowhere in the white man’s world is
there such an opening for an honest paper, and it is good
to see that the chance has been seized by a patriotic Aus-
tralian.
The Victorian Women’s National Ass. is going to have
a paper of its own, published monthly at a small price,
with Miss Grace Watson as chief of staff, editress, busi-
ness manageress, publisherina, and most other things.
The production will be devoted to the interests of the
ladies and their tame pet politicians ; also to the de-
scription of branch Ass festivities. The recollection of
the Ass.’s spasmodic publications ijiises hopes of a new
form of Australian humor. As a burlesque of journalism,
and a farce of feminine false logic, it will surely
be unique. Leading articles will be a masque in
which Jeanne D’Arc, Caius Caligula, iGeorgy Reid, and the
Guelph family will link arms and polka gaily through
a maze of Federal politics, and social anachronisms.
Our humorists of to-day and yesteryear are about to be
out-laughed. The Dowager of Cliveden will write a
philosophic screed. The interesting and whirly Grace
will contribute a sensational serial, “Legal Experiences
of an Enterprising Spinster.” Mrs. Frank Madden will
explain “ The Importance of Being Respectable ! ” (which
means holding a place upon the Wimmen’s League
membership list). And the Staughton widow will ex-
pound “ The use of politics as a social side-dish.” These
announcements aren’t authorised by the respective
authors, but the programme set forth will certainly be
followed to some extent.
* ~ * • *
Pott and Shirk lately got “into holts” again—at
Casterton (Vic.), this time, where one organ accused
its “reptile contemporary” of working its employees
seven days a week, while the other retorted that it didn’t
make its male and female comps, work in the same shop,
anyhow. As a result, the provisions of the Factories
Act may be extended to Casterton’s printing trade—
which somehow recalls the old proverb about Potts and
Slurk falling out, and other people who don’t Slurk
thereby coming into possession of their own.
* * m m
“Nulla”: “Don’t want Boer war stories” is how the
Sydney Mail’s “Answers to Correspondents” column
replies to the budding author nowadays. The farm-
burners’ lot in three words: (1) Cheers, (2) beers, (3)
jeers.
* * * *
“ A.F.T.” (Vic.). What show have writers got in Mel-
bourne with very Reverend Piratical Editors of the
Fitchett type? The other day I purchased Life, and
took the trouble to make a careful analysis of its con tents.
I discovered that one thousand inches of matter (equal
to 125 columns, out of a total of 200 columns), had been
stolen, either en bloc or piece-meal, from the under-
mentioned publications :
! Tatler, Westminster Gazette, Light of India, Maori-
land Free Lance, Yellow Book (English), Daily Mail
Year Book, Pall Mall Gazette, The Bystander, Current
Literature, Munsey’s Magazine, S.A. Commissioners’
Report, The Australia, London Times, London Chronicle,
The Bookman, The Academy, Argonaut, London Punch'
The Fortnightly, World’s Work, Harper’s Magazine!
Atlantic Monthly, Sunset, Scribner’s Magazine, The
Scotsman, The Bulletin, The Age, Whitaker’s Peerage
Daily Mail, British Weekly, Book of Birds (Rothschild)!
The Critic, Review of Reviews, Public Opinion Monthly
Review, Methodist Times, M.A.P., North American
Review, The Century", Raleigh’s Life of Shakspeare,
Monthly Review, Nineteenth Century, Macmillan’s
Magazine, Modern Science, Science, The Independent,
The Reader, The Philistine, Success.
Also a complete chapter was lifted from a new book. As
a literary cuckoo, Fitchett is an easy first in the Com-
monwealth, beating even the good little Juddy. 1
THE SETTLED BARDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
These be the grim suburban days,
And, tho’ in verse we frolic,
All soberly we go our ways
Writing our sweet commercial lays
And any sort of stuff that pays
(Our Johnnie’s got the colic).
When Omar was our guiding star
We drank and quoted gaily,
But Middle Age said “ Here you are ! ”
And now we live in suburbs far,
And watch the fleeting- railway ear,
And rush to catch it daily.
We’re settled down and going bald,
And when we sit at table
The wife recites the Ones Who’ve Called
(.Some years ago it would have palled,
But—Pegasus is snugly stalled
And champing in his stable).
We’ve done with dreams—we’ve done with drinks
(Could anything be stranger !)
And when we ask Peg. thro’ the chinks
Of his abode just what he thinks,
The old steed whisks his tail, and winks
Into his well-filled manger.

TAKE DRUGS!
WHY don’t you Stop it? You are still doctoring, but changing
doctors does not cure you. You are trying one medicine after the other
with the same disgusting disappointment. You are a “ medicine doser,”
and the only change in your condition is for the worse. Why don't you
stop tHis medicine Habit ? Why don’t you avail
yourself of the modern remedy? Proceed on Nature’s own
lines. Use the life-giving, healing, strengthening forces of Electricity!
This is the electric age. You can see on every hand the wonders of Electricity.
You don’t need doctors; you don’t need medicine, for medicine gives no
nourishment. You DO need Electric Life Force. Use it as we apply it and you
will be well, strong and happy.
Nearly every ailment which afflicts humanity, outside of contagious diseases, is due to
the breaking-down of some vital organ. When a certain part fails to do its alotted task
the entire human machinery goes to smash. The only way to regain health—even life
—is to rebuild the weak part which has caused all the trouble by obtaining a new supply
of vital electric energy. This can only be properly done with the new improved

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense' of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks dov. n
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

Headache, Indigestion and
Constipation.
AN IMPORTANT DISCOVERY.
It will interest sufferers to know
that a valuable medicine, called Froo-
toids, has been discovered, which is
now completely curing each of tile
above-named complaints. Frootoids
are elegant in appearance, and plea-
sant to take, and, what is of the ut-
most importance, are thoroughly reli-
able in affording quick relief. You
do not require to go on takittg them
for a prolonged period, as is necessary
with some medicines, which even then
are mostly disappointing; you simply
take a dose of Frootoids when ill and
repeat the dose if necessary, but gene-
rally one dose is quite effective,
j Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so
far that they not only act as an ape-
rient, but do remove from the blood,
tissues, and internal organs all the
waste poisonous matter that is clog-
ging them and choking the channels
that lead to and from them. The bene-
ficial effects of Frootoids are evident
at once by the disappearance of head-
ache, the head becoming clear, and a
bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly,
and by the food being properly di-
gested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison is present, or when
Congestion of the Brain or Apoplexy
is present or threatening. They have
been tested, and have been proved to
afford quick relief in such cases when
other aperients have not done any
good at all. It is of the utmost impor-
tance that this should be borne in
mind, for in such cases to take an or-
dinary aperient is to -waste time and
permit of a serious illness becoming
fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
autibilious pills make worse. Many
people have been made sick and iil
by antibilious pills that could have
been cured at once by Frootoids. "
People should not allow themselves to
be duped into contracting a medicine-
taking habit by being persuaded to
take daily doses with each meal of so-
called indigestion cures that do NOT
cure. Frootoids have been subjected
to extensive tests, and have in every
case proved successful in completely
curing the complaints named.
A constipated habit of body will be
completely cured if the patient will on
each occasion, when suffering, take a
dose ot Froocoids, instead of an or-
dinary aperient; by so doing, the pa-
tient will require doses only at longer
intervals, and will so become quite
independent of the necessity of taking
any aperient medicine.
Frootoids are only now being placed
on the Australian market, consequently
you may at present have a difficulty
in getting them from your local che-
mist or storekeeper; but ask for them,
and if you cannot get them at once,
send stamps or postal note for price,

long, and a bottle of them will be im-
mediately forwarded to you post free.

salers can now obtain wholesale sup-
plies from W. G. Hearne, Chemist,
Geelong, Victoria.


“ F.M.” on fleas and other winged
animals :
Have you met the great flea of Balclutha (M.L.)? It is
the one ferocious wild animal the country possesses, and
can only be stalked successfully by the wise and prudent.
Its horns are very ornamental for use as hat-racks, albeit
rather large for small families and boudoirs. In the
famous Hobken collection there is a pair that weighs
two hundredweight ; but from the ferocity and
size of the savage brute to which the antlers be-
longed, there is an idea that it was bred in the
Theatre Royal, Hobart. The Hobart theatre species is
noted for its strength of bone, its indiscriminate appetite,
and its love of limelight and priina donna. It has also
been known, when hard-set, to attack the proprietor. It
can be heard calling for its young at a distance of tw o
miles. It loves American farce, and weeps so copiously
during amateur performances down there that its jaws
become nerveless and flaccid, and it can be led away by
a little child. The angels also weep ; but they are far
away and negligible. Your angel is a very modest
bird, though its costume is not considered respectable
at Dunedin (M.L.). This critical acerbity is due in part
to jealousy, and in part to ignorance. No Dunedin man
has ever seen an angel, nor ever will. The angels have
their compensations. There is also the angel without
wings, otherwise known to the initiated as the Queen of
the Earth. She is generally very beautiful by malice
aforethought, and tastes like a dispensary just suggested
across a field of clover. In the beginning she came down
from Heaven on a cheap excursion, and lost her return
ticket. Now, she has even forgotten the name of the
station she came from.

REMARKABLE
INVENTION
FOR THE
CULTURE
OF HAIR
CHE EVANS VACUUM CAP is a practical invention constructed on scientific and
hygienic principles, by the simple means of which a free and normal circulation
is restored throughout the scalp. The minute blood vessels are gently stimulated
to activity, thus allowing the food supply, which can only be derived from the blood,
to be carried to the hair roots, the effects of which are quickly seen in a healthy, vigorous
growth of hair. There is no rubbing, and as no drugs or chemicals of whatsoever kind
are employed, there is nothing to cause irritation. It is only necessary to wear the Cap
three or four minutes daily.
60 DAYS’ FREE TRIAL!
The Company's Guarantee.
An EVANS VACUUM CAP will be sent you for sixty days’ free
trial. If you do not see a gradual development of a new growth of hair,
and are not convinced that the Cap will completely restore your hair, you
are at liberty to return the Cap with no expense whatever to yourself. It
is requested, as an evidence of good faith, that the price of the Cap be
deposited with the Chancery Lane Safe Deposit Company of London, the
largest financial and business institution of the kind in the world, who will
issue a receipt guaranteeing that the money will be returned in full, on
demand, without questions or comment, at any time during the trial period.
The eminent Dr. I. N. LOVE, in his address to the Medical Board on the subject of Alopsecia (loss of
hair) stated that if a means could be devised to bring nutrition to the hair follicles (hair roots), without
resorting to any irritating process, the problem of hair growth would be solved, cater on, when
the EVANS VACUUM CAP was submitted to him for inspection, hie remarked that the Cap wou.a
fulfil and confirm in practice the observations he had previously made before the Medical Bof rJ.
Dr. W. MOORE, referring to the invention, says that the principle upon which the Evans Vacuum
Cap is founded is absolutely correct and indisputable.

Resurrection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WESTWARD floats the drowsy Day in
her glorious disarray,
To a scarlet cloud a-slumber in the
sunset’s mist of gold ;
And a wave-born lullaby,
Creeping down the dying sky,
Croons around her splendid footstool like a love-
song overbold.
Westward go the swimming wings, from the red
cliffs and the springs,
Homeward from the dusky shore-woods, and the
upland’s wooded pride;
For they beard the feet of Night,
As she trod the darkening height,
With her twilight robes about her, shadow-shod
and starry-eyed.
Jealously her ebon hair, streaming down the
everywhere,
Blinds the purple eyes of daylight smiling from
her scarlet cloud.
And the stale that wandereth
Sings a tale of dream and death,
Of a springtime glory pallid in its western shadow
shroud.
There’s a clamor in the foam where the breakers
curl and comb,
Calling through the misty waters to the shore
waves in the dark :
“ Have you heard the bitter tale
Of the wandering western gale?
Did you catch her blown sweet kisses ere the
black night smote her stark ? ”
Out and out across the deep, where the quick-
sands slide and sleep,
Where the harbor-bar is droning to the plunging
water-ways,
Where the boulder beaches ride
’Twixt the sandstone and the tide,
There are voices sighing sorrow for this fairest of
all days.
They had loved this bright first-born since the
gateway of the morn
Gave her to them, fair and splendid—sea and sky
and waking world.
For the winter grim and gray
Swept across them many a day,
Scourging with his frosty midnights and the
north wind blindly hurled.
Bub a voice from out the deep called the Spring-
tide in its sleep
Blushing from the womb of Winter, scarlet lips
and golden eyes,
So adown the heights she came,
And the world-wide sang her fame,
Torrents on the shiny uplands, loud throats in
the glowing skies.
Leagues and leagues of tramping sea smiled a
welcome graciously,
Never went the gale so gently through the wood-
lands by the bay ;
There were songsters north and south,
And the sinuous harbor-mouth
Shook his shoulders, laughing, laughing, in a flood
of falling spray. - *
Lo, a whisper down the thrall of the mystery
over all—
Oh the hapless world is sighing, and her hopeless
voices weep!
Lo, a whisper, tenderly,
Stirs the air and sobbing sea
With a tale of Resurrection when the Night slips
off the deep.
‘ ‘ When the thousand, thousand eyes down against
the morning rise,
She shall wake once more the beauty, still the
dearest of all days.
With the dawn wind on her lips,
She shall rock the sleeping ships,
Kiss the slumb’rous world to joyance in her won-
drous, winsome ways.”
Drowsy hours rise and fail, swims the darkness,
croons the gale ;
There’s an echo in the white east of a clear up-
springing song.
She is coming up the skies
With the sun-burst in her eyes.
Hist, the gladness of the shore-woods and the
waves that loom along !
Maoriland.
C. ALLAN MARRIS.
The Shepherd Kings of
Maoriland.
THE USURPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS confidential (and only) clerk and
head engrosser in the office of Martin and
Drinkwater, Barristers and Solicitors,
Violetville, Maoriland. Incidentally I collected
accounts and did anything else that was going,
between taking down confidences and engrossing
deeds. One afternoon, while Martin was working
up a divorce case in his private room and Drink-
water was heavily adulterating it with whisky,
a tall, handsome man with cold blue eyes and a
clean-shaven face strode in, and, leaning across
the counter, inquired, as man to man, was there
any chance of a billet.
I said there wasn’t, and then proceeded to take
stock of him, for he was of striking appearance.
He was tall and finely built, and ought to have
been attractive. But he wasn’t, and I wondered
why. Then I placed it against his cold blue eye
and hard, cruel mouth. He was undoubtedly
intellectual, but there was something about him I
didn’t like. He was very anxious to get some,
thing to do, as he had just arrived from England
and had a little son in delicate health. The boy’s
mother was dead, and he had brought the little
fellow all the way to Maoriland on what he termed
his “dead ace.”
I told him plainly that he had come to the
wrong quarter to do any good to himself. The
country was a huge sheep walk, and employment
was growing scarcer every day. The only thriving
industry I knew of was marrying “Maori prin-
cesses.” Several of our most estimable citizens
had made fortunes that way, but I had not heard
of anybody having amassed a competence by hard
graft.
He said he didn’t want to make a fortune, but
he was willing to do anything to make a decent
living. I didn’t tell him that his mouth belied
him, but I thought it all the same.
Martin came out, held the door open for the
lady, and while she drew on her gloves, he men-
tioned there were some letters on his desk I might
attend to. He rather thought he wouldn’t be back
that afternoon.
On Martin’s desk, among others, was a letter
from old Mr. Morphett, of Oreti Valley sheep
station, asking the firm to get him a good, reliable
overseer and bookkeeper. Previous experience
with sheep was not necessary, but a thorough
knowledge of bookkeeping was. He was begin-
ning to find that at 65 years a man wanted some-
one to lean on.
I stepped back into the front office. The tall
stranger still leaned meditatively over the
counter.
“ Are you a bookkeeper ? ” I inquired.
“I am ; I have good references —English ones—
if you would care to see them.”
“Well, do you know anything about jum-
bucks ?”
“ Er— no ; I’m afraid I do not even know the
animals.”
“ Woollies, sheep, baa-lambs—you know.”
“ Well, no, I regret to say ”
“It doesn’t matter a hang. Let’s see your
references. I might be able to put something in
your way.”
He spread them out on the counter, and I
glanced over them. They seemed pretty good.
Anyhow, he wanted the billet badly, and the end
of it was he was engaged for a month on trial.
* * * *
Old Mr. Morphett was the soul of kindness,
and, discovering that his new bookkeeper was a
man of education, he had him at his own table.
A good conversationalist, alert and well-read, he
appealed to Morphett. Also, it soon began to be
said that he appealed even more to Mrs. Mor-
phett, who was at the dangerous age of 29, and
was given to writing passionate poetry on the
beauties of youthful love.
I received a note from Mr. Warner that he had
fallen on his feet and among the whitest people
on earth.
It was nearly three months before Mr. Morphett
was in Violetville, and when he called in to shake
hands with Martin, he expressed himself as de-
lighted with the mau we had sent him.
Martin didn’t seem wildly enthusiastic. He
had himself finished working up his divorce case
—in fact, he had been made co-re just ten minutes
before Morphett arrived. I foresaw that the firm
of Martin and Drinkwater was doomed. Drink-
water wouldn’t see anything ; he stuck religiously
to whisky. But this divorce case would kill us. I
began to regret that I had sent Mr. Warner to
Oreti Valley—l wished I had gone myself.
I asked Mr. Morphett if he had any more
vacancies.
“ Yes,” he said, “ we’ve a vacancy for a guest
for three months any time you feel that way.
Now, don’t forget. Just drop a line, and we’ll
send the waggonette down for you.
* * * *
When the firm of Martin and Drinkwater had
been dissolved, and Drinkwater carefully placed
in the asylum for inebriates, I found myself a
guest at Oreti Valley till something should turn
up.
“ You’re just in time, my boy. Governor
coming up next week—meet all the best people
and have some excellent shooting among the hares.
Splendid, couldn’t be better,” said Morphett,
gleefully.
I didn’t see much of Warner —in fact, I felt
that he avoided me. But I heard much about him,
and the thing that puzzled me was that Morphett
didn’t hear it too ; it seemed to be such common
talk.
The hare drive was to take place in a couple of
clover paddocks bounded on one side by a patch
of gohi trees, and a deep, wide stream on the
other. The men were to beat up the gohi patches,
which were a great cover for the hares, and drive
them across the clover paddocks, where they
would be in the line of fire. It might be bad for
the hares, but it meant joy in the hospitals.
Warner was on the right wing, against the
stream. His Ex. and Morphett were between the
centre and the left wing. The result of this
arrangement was that the harts that came under
Warner’s aim had run the gauntlet of the whole
party—and were painfully scarce.
The yellow gohi blooms filled the atmosphere
with perfume, the morning air was delightfully
crisp, and the hares being very fast and wary,
some pretty shooting resulted. His Ex. became
very enthusiastic, and pressed eagerly forward,
Morphett accompanying him.
There was a sudden rush of nearly a dozen
hares; a volley of shouts and shots rang out.
Morphett dropped his gun, spun round, and fell
heavily against his Ex. The Governor was a
man who had seen active service, and he knew the
symptoms of a mortal wound.
“ How the h did you do that ? ” he snorted
to Warner, as that gentleman rushed up.
“I didn’t do it,” said Warner, kneeling down,
and drawing out a handkerchief. “ Where is it.
Mr. Morphett?”
But Morphett never spoke again.
“ He’s dying,” said his Ex. “ Mr. Warner, if
you value your peace of mind and other people’s
safety, never handle a gun again.”
There was no suggestion that it was anything
else but a most unfortunate accident. His Ex.
didn’t associate with murderers, and a merely
formal inquest ended the matter. The shooting
party broke up, and Mrs. Morphett publicly
acquitted Warner of all blame in the matter.
Then she came into Violetville and ordered some
most expensive mourning.
I was back at Violetville, hard at work in the
office of Holt and Macpherson, who had annexed
most of the business of my late employers, when
I heard, about sixteen weeks after the shooting of
Morphett, that Warner had married the widow.
It was spoken of in the social column of the papers
as “a very quiet wedding.”
The whole of the property had been bequeathed
to Mrs. Morphett by a will made a few days after
her marriage with her late husband.
That was sudden enough, and, somehow, I
wasn’t surprised—except at the rapidity with
which events moved. People said loudly the
inquest ought to be reopened.
Meanwhile Warner rode proudly through the
streets of Violetville. His stirrups and mouth-
pieces were silver-plated, and the great bay
stallion he rode had the small, thin, tapering
head, the clean limbs, and the hairless heels that
bespeak the thoroughbred.
He* was devotion itself to the woman he had

RHEUMATISM,
Sciatica, Neuralgia, Etc.
AS an Embrocation for Rheumatism, Sciatica, and all deep-seated pains,
Zam-Buk Balm possesses many superior merits. The refined vegetable
essences, of which Zam-Buk is composed, are so pure that they penetrate right
through the tissues, get at the very root of the trouble, and, once there, simply
drive the pain out. There is no waste —one of the many advantages of a Balm
over a liquid. With a liquid preparation half of it is wasted through running
olf the part to be massaged on to the floor. With Zam-Buk Balm you take out
with the finger the exact amount you may require. Under the friction and heat
of the hand it soon liquidises, forming an Embrocation of unequalled efficacy.
These excruciating pains usually occur about the joints of
the lumbar region, and may be caused through cold or
damp. Neuralgia, of course, affects the head and face.
Zam-Buk Balm should be freely and vigorously rubbed in
for several minutes each night and
morning, when the pain will be
soothed, and the muscles so stimu-
lated that in future they will be
better capable of resisting cold and
damp.

courtly grace took back all they said. He was
evidently doing his best to atone for the sad acci-
dent by life-long devotion.
Six months later, Holt and Macpherson were
favored with instructions from Mrs. Warner to
draw up deeds, making over to her beloved
husband, Herbert Lloyd Warner, the whole of her
property. Holt, whom I had always regarded as
a man whom nothing on earth could shock, swore
vehemently. Macpherson was so distressed at
the prospects of young Morphett that he went
out and took to drink. The deeds were executed
at the station, and no one had any opportunity to
urge the claims of the little boy, who was away at
college.
Then retribution fell on that foolish, infatuated
woman. Warner threw off the mask and scoffed
at the woman who had dragged herself in the dirt
for his profit. He sent his own son to Oxford
University on her dead husband’s money, and her
son he sent to a third-rate boarding school in a
southern town. He ceased to take his wife about,
or to accompany her anywhere. Society cast her
forth, and every house in the country was closed
against them both. Warner didn’t care. Society
was not one of his cravings. He revelled and
rioted round hotels ; he took select parties out to
Oreti Valley ; the old station homestead was
given up to the wildest orgies, and Mrs. Warner
was flouted in her own home by vulgar half-castes
and arrogant adventuresses of her own race. It
broke her heart and drove her to drink. That he
did not deny her. In the matter of liquor he
was liberality itself, and less than a year saw her
dead of alcoholic poisoning.
Oreti ceased to be mentioned in polite society,
but its owner was a power to be reckoned with.
Wild as his life was he looked after the station,
and worked it for all he was worth. Immediately
his mother was dead young Morphett left school
and started to earn his own living somewhere —•
we hardly knew where.
• * • •
It was seven p.m. on a wintry evening that I
arrived at Oreti Valley homestead, in response to
an urgent summons, and was conducted to a room
packed from ceiling to floor with elegantly bound
volumes. A wood fire burned in the big chimney,
an old-fashioned clock ticked drowsily, and
shaded lamps threw the light on the table imme-
diately beneath. Hid in shadow was the figure
of Warner lying on a couch, with a Maori girl
kneeling beside him. The atmosphere reeked
with the smell of drugs. I crossed over and
looked curiously at this unutterable scoundrel.
Strange to say, his face was not so repellent to
me as in those far-off days when he had entered
the old office seeking employment. I had heard
that he was ill, but he looked worse than ill.
There were deep moraines furrowed by mortal
agony on his face, and the aspect of suffering
roused sympathy.
“ I want you to draw up a will. There’s all the
material you require at that table. Hurry up,
for I want to get relief from this agony. ’
“ How do you want to divide the property ? ” I
queried.
“ I don’t want to divide it. I leave everything
to my son, Herbert Lloyd Warner, at present of
Oriel College, Oxford, England.”
“ What about young Morphett ? ”
“I am not concerned with the prospects of
young Morphett.”
“But, you infernal robber, do you mean to
leave him none of his father’s property ? You’ll
have to die someday, and how do you think you’ll
feel if you commit such a crime as this ? ”
“Look here, do you know that I am as good as
dead already. Hurry, hurry, man, I must get
relief.”
“ Well, get relief, and I’ll draw the thing up
afterwards.”
He looked at me curiously.
“ I am afraid that when I get relief I shall take
no interest in wills or anything else.”
“Why, will you be unconscious?”
“No, you fool! I’ll be dead."’
“ You don’t look like a man about to die.”
He threw back the rug that covered him, held
from his body by a wire screen. And I’d sooner
look into the unveiled face of Death any time
than even think fully on what I saw. Maori
leprosy in a white man is awful beyond words.
I recoiled. I started for the door. A big
Maori stepped out of the shadows and caught me
round the neck, dragging me to the ground. I
was hauled back.
His face hardened, and a look of ferocity over-
came the stamp of suffering.
“Look here, if you haven’t got that will drawn
up in proper form in ten minutes, just as I require
it. I’ll have you inoculated with this. These
Maoris here will do anything I tell them. Than
this nothing can be worse—-and besides, in half-
an-hour I’ll be dead. Will you do it ?”
“Yes!” '
“ Put down that I am in my right mind, that I
am capable of reasoning, and that, being abouttodie
by my own hand merely to end my sufferings, I
have had this will drawn up to render impossible
any action against my son, Herbert Lloyd Warner,
for recovery of the whole or any part of the
estate. Put that down in the correct legal jargon—•
unless you want to die a leper.”
I was young, ambitious and dreadfully in want
of money, and I took the risk of my life. I sat
down at a table and drew up a will in the most
concise and lucid form, but not exactly the will
he directed. I read over to him what should have
been on the paper, and witnessed his signature,
trusting to the shadows and Warner’s failing
sight—and luck. Then he handed the paper to a
stalwart Maori, and said something to him in the
native language.
The Maori went out. I asked if I might follow
him.
“ No, I don’t trust you. Anyhow, stay and see
the end.”
The Maori girl, who had never stirred from his
side, rose, went to a desk, and returned with a
small bottle of colorless fluid.
“ Aconite, my friend—most painless thing.
Heart just slows down and—then, the bliss of
freedom from all suffering. Take it if ever you
come to anything like this.”
He poured the contents of the tiny bottle into
a champagne glass, which the Maori girl filled
with wine, and gulped it off. He drained the
last drop and threw the glass on the floor.
“ You’d better not stay. These Maoris don’t
understand, and they might think you’d some-
thing to do with it. Ride for your life.”
I was out of the room before he had finished
speaking, and climbing on my horse I tore away
through the rain.
♦ * * *
When the will was read, it divided the pro-
perty equally between young Warner and Harold
Morphett. At an early opportunity I explained
to Morphett at what desperate risk his name had
been put in the document, and then stood around
in a picturesque attitude waiting for my reward.
And Morphett, after deep consideration, told
me that if the story was true I had committed a
most remarkable breach of trust, and if it wasn’t
true I was something quite unusual in the way of
a liar.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
"Malua.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
the gem-like, scattered islands
' I ’ that, bestnd the warm seas by us,
JL Thrives a comfortable doctrine, with a
strong appeal to me ;
It is differently titled, in clashed dialects that try
us,
But sounds music, as “Malua,” to our neighbors
of Fiji.
’TistheGospel of “To-morrow,” “Hasten slowly,”
nothing hurries ;
If we live too vehemently we shall all the sooner
die.
Let things slide along untended, if their siblings
banish worries,
There’s a time for all things coming ; but it’s
coming “ by-and-bye.”
By-and-bye may bring us trouble, but we need
not half-way meet it;
Let all dreary speculations with “ Malua ” be dis-
patched !
By-and-bye may gift us pleasure, then more gladly
shall we greet it,
Having counted not our chickens ere the little
birds are hatched.
Then “Malua” to the hustler, who, all “ waste of
time ” begrudging,
Feels a call to set me working with a “method”
and a “ will ” ;
And “Malua” to the parson, when he bids pre-
pare for judging;
To my creditor, “Malua,” when he wants his
-- “ little bill.”
Maybe some day in the future, I shall waken
from my dreaming ;
Maybe some day I shall mingle with my brothers
in their strife;
Learning duly to distinguish actuality from seenV
' ing,
Bmt at present, here’s “Malua” to “realities of
life”!
Yes, “Malua’s” evanescence to all sermonising
strictures!
Though my attitude be torpid, can my mind not
swiftly move ?
And the dreamer dwells his fancies in a dozen
vivid pictures,
While.the, strenuously struggling may not jump
one narrow groove. •
By-and-bye shall bring nepenthe to the resterand
the rover,
To the Marthas and the Marys, for their tracks
together trend.
All alike, we find one level, by-and-bye, when life
is over ;
And “Malua” all are waiting, for “Malua” is
the end.
And if yet we rise immortal, as we keep so fondly
hoping,
We are fatuous if fretting lest we waste our lives
away; ....
For Eternity must find us lots of leisure time for
coping
With the lessons and the problems that wc put
aside to-day.
So “ Malua ” be my password ; sound philosophy
I borrow
From the sea-wrapt hills and corals of the isles of
far Fiji.
And our wisest might find teachings in these
slack lands of “ To-morrow,”
For “the heathen in his blindness ” sometimes sees
what we don’t see.
N.S.W.
TALAFEKAU.
Maconochie’s Death.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f is the story of Maconochie, as told
by Jackandrew, of Winterfield, via Boor-
ingaboot. Maconochie was a shrewd and
a frugal man, and was always putting by the
bawbees for his old age. When Mac reached the
age of 77 he was still saving and accumulating
that he might be well prepared for that lament-
able time when he would be stricken with years,
and incapable of wielding axe and hoe. Then
Maconochie had the misfortune of his life—he died.
The event was wholly unexpected by Maconochie.
Jackandrew found him lying in his hut awaiting
the services of the undertaker, and the doctor from
Booringaboot gave a certificate at the solicitations
of the only surviving relation, Maconochie of Wag-
tail. The funeral was a very good one at the
price. The hearse had two horses, glass sides,
and four plumes, and the coffin was polished oak,
with nickel embellishments. Maconochie of
Wagtail had found £143 in his deceased friend’s
hut, in addition to various bonds and deposit
receipts, and he was disposed to do the thing well.
The ride from Winterfield to Booringaboot was
long, hot, and trying, but the burying-ground was
reached in fair time, and Mac was unloaded beside
the fresh-made grave. Then a strange sound was
heard coming from the beautiful and expensive
coffin.
“ Man,” said Maconochie of Wagtail, “ what’s
yon ? ” And his face blanched with terror.
The sound was repeated. The coffin was tick-
ing like a great hall clock.
“ It’s the corpse ! ” said Jackandrew.
“And whyfor?” asked Maconochie, tremu-
lous with awe. “For why wad a corpse be doin’
sic foolishness ? ”
“ Maybe he wants to get out,” said Jackandrew
at a venture.
“ Na, na,” cried Maconochie, “I will na have
eet. Yon’s irreleegious.”
But the kicking became more violent, and
despite the protestations of Maconochie of Wag-
tail a pick-point was inserted, and the lid was
prized off.
Maconochie of Winterfield sat up in his coffin,
and blinked his eyes for two minutes. He looked
about, while the mourners stared at him in blank
wonder, and Maconochie of Wagtail uttered low
lamentations in quaint dialect. Mac of Winter-
field looked at the coffin, at the open grave, at
the splendid hearse, the plumes, the two black
horses and the mourning coach, and then he
spoke, and his voice was hoarse with emotion.
“Losh, man,” he said, “hoo mickle did it cost?”
“ Twenta-five poond ! ” answered Maconochie of
Wagtail. “ Twenta-five poond and saxpence, t’
be precise.”
“ Twenta-five poond ! ” ejaculated Mac of
Winterfield. “ Twenta-five poond ! It’s a peety
t’ waste it! ”
And then Maconochie of Winterfield lay back
in his coffin, and died in real earnest.
